Nedarim



Daf 1a



Daf 1b



Daf 2a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> When an individual takes a vow, he renders an object forbidden to himself or to others as though it were a sacrificial offering; this parallels the act of consecrating an offering, which also renders an item forbidden for personal use by means of a verbal declaration. The most direct expression of a vow is when an individual says: This object is forbidden to me, or to others, like an offering. Additionally, the mishna states that <b>all substitutes</b> for the language <b>of vows are like vows.</b> Consequently, if one states that an object is forbidden to him like a <i>konam</i> instead of like an offering [<i>korban</i>], the vow takes effect, as <i>konam</i> is a substitute term for the word <i>korban</i> (see 10a).
Similarly, substitutes for the language of <b>dedications are like dedications,</b> substitutes for the language of <b>oaths are like oaths, and</b> substitutes for the language of <b>nazirite</b> vows <b>are like nazirite</b> vows. Therefore, if one declared a <i>ḥerekh</i> instead of a dedication [<i>ḥerem</i>], a <i>shevuta</i> instead of an oath [<i>shevua</i>], or proclaimed that he was becoming a <i>nazik</i> instead of a nazirite [<i>nazir</i>], his statement takes effect.
With regard to <b>one who says to another: I am avowed from you,</b> or: <b>I am separated from you,</b> or: <b>I am distanced from you,</b> and he then says: <b>That</b> which <b>I eat</b> of <b>yours,</b> or: <b>That</b> which <b>I taste</b> of <b>yours,</b> even though he did not explicitly state that he is taking a vow or specify the nature of the vow, the object of his vow is nevertheless <b>forbidden.</b> His intention is understood based on his incomplete statement, known as an intimation of a vow, and his vow therefore takes effect.
However, if he says: <b>I am ostracized from you,</b> which does not clearly declare any matter to be prohibited, <b>Rabbi Akiva was uncertain</b> about this <i>halakha</i> but was inclined <b>to rule stringently about this</b> and consider it a vow prohibiting the speaker from deriving benefit from his fellow.

Daf 2b

<strong>GEMARA:</strong> It was taught in the mishna that <b>all substitutes</b> for the language <b>of vows are like vows,</b> substitutes for the language of dedications are like dedications, substitutes for the language of oaths are like oaths, and substitutes for the language of nazirite vows are like nazirite vows. The Gemara asks: <b>What is different with regard to</b> the first mishna of tractate <b><i>Nazir</i> that it does not teach all of them,</b> i.e., all of the cases listed above besides nazirite vows, <b>and what is different with regard to</b> the first mishna of tractate <b><i>Nedarim</i> that it teaches all of them</b> and not merely the case of vows, which is the subject directly relevant to this tractate?
The Gemara answers that <b>due to</b> the fact that <b>vows and oaths are written next to each other</b> in the Torah in the verse: “When a man takes a vow to the Lord, or swears an oath” (Numbers 30:3), the mishna <b>teaches</b> these <b>two</b> cases, i.e., substitutes for the language of vows and oaths. <b>And since it taught two</b> of the cases, <b>it taught all of them.</b>
The Gemara asks: If so, <b>let</b> the mishna <b>teach</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>substitutes</b> for the language <b>of oaths</b> immediately <b>after</b> the case of substitutes for the language of <b>vows.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Since it taught</b> the case of <b>vows, whereby an object becomes forbidden to one,</b> it <b>taught also</b> the case of <b>dedications, whereby an object becomes forbidden to one.</b> This is <b>to the exclusion of an oath,</b> whereby <b>one prohibits himself from</b> making use of <b>an object.</b> In the case of an oath, unlike a vow or a dedication, one prohibits himself from performing a particular action rather than declaring an object to be forbidden.
§ The Gemara asks a question with regard to the style of the mishna: The mishna <b>began with</b> the case of <b>substitutes</b> when it stated: <b>All substitutes</b> for the language <b>of vows</b> are like vows, <b>and</b> it then immediately <b>explains</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>intimations</b> of vows, as the next line addresses a case of <b>one who says to his fellow: I am avowed from you. And furthermore,</b> did the <i>tanna</i> <b>forget</b> to mention <b>intimations</b> of vows? Why doesn’t the mishna state that intimations of vows are considered vows before it gives examples of intimations?
The Gemara answers: The mishna <b>is dealing with them,</b> i.e., intimations of vows, <b>and</b> the text of the mishna <b>is incomplete and is teaching the following: All substitutes</b> for the language <b>of vows are like vows, and intimations of vows are like vows.</b> The mishna then continues by giving examples of intimations of vows.
The Gemara asks: <b>Let</b> the mishna <b>explain</b> the case of <b>substitutes</b> for the language of vows <b>first,</b> i.e., before it gives examples of intimations, just as the basic <i>halakha</i> of substitutes for the language of vows was mentioned first. In fact, it is not until later (10a) that the mishna provides examples of substitutes for the language of vows.
The Gemara answers: The general style of the Mishna is that <b>the</b> subject <b>with which it concludes is the one that it explains first, as</b> in that which <b>we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 20b): <b>With what may one light</b> the Shabbat lamp <b>and with what may one not light</b> it? <b>One may not light</b> with cedar bast, <b>etc.</b> The mishna provides examples of items one may not use to light the Shabbat lamp, which was the concluding phrase of the mishna’s introductory question, rather than beginning with examples of what one may use to light the Shabbat lamp.
Similarly, another mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 47b) states: <b>In what may one insulate</b> a pot of cooked food on Shabbat eve, <b>and in what may one not insulate</b> it? <b>One may not insulate</b> it, <b>etc.</b> A third example of this style is in the following mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 57a): <b>With what</b> items <b>may a woman go out</b> into the public domain on Shabbat <b>and with what</b> items <b>may she not go out? A woman may not go out</b> with strings of wool and other adornments that she may take off and carry.
The Gemara challenges this explanation: <b>And</b> is it true that <b>wherever it begins,</b> i.e., whichever topic the mishna mentions first, <b>it does not explain first? But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Bava Batra</i> 108a): <b>There are</b> some relatives who <b>inherit and bequeath,</b> e.g., a father and a son, who inherit property from each other, and there are those who <b>inherit but do not bequeath,</b> e.g., a son and his mother; <b>and these</b> are the ones who <b>inherit and bequeath,</b> etc. This mishna provides examples of the opening line of the introductory statement before providing examples of the concluding line of the introductory statement.
Similarly, another mishna (<i>Yevamot</i> 84a) states: <b>There are</b> some women who are <b>permitted to their husbands and forbidden to their <i>yevamin</i>,</b> i.e., their husband’s brothers if their husbands die childless. These cases include one where the <i>yavam</i> is the High Priest, who is prohibited from marrying a widow. There are other women who are <b>permitted to their <i>yevamin</i></b> if their husbands die childless <b>but forbidden to their husbands,</b> e.g., if a High Priest betrothed a widow and his brother is a common priest. The mishna immediately provides the details of the first principle: <b>And these are</b> the women who are <b>permitted to their husbands and forbidden to their <i>yevamin</i>.</b>
Similarly, another mishna (<i>Menaḥot</i> 59a) states with regard to meal-offerings: <b>There are</b> some meal-offerings that <b>require oil and frankincense</b> and some that require <b>oil but not frankincense.</b> The mishna continues: <b>And these</b> are the ones that <b>require oil and frankincense.</b> Yet another mishna (<i>Menaḥot</i> 60a) states: <b>There are</b> meal-offerings that <b>require bringing near,</b> a ritual where the priests were required to carry the offering in their hands and bring it near the altar, <b>and</b> they <b>do not require waving;</b> other meal-offerings require <b>waving but not bringing near. And these</b> are the meal-offerings that <b>require bringing near.</b>
Another mishna (<i>Bekhorot</i> 46a) states: <b>There are</b> some who are considered a <b>firstborn with regard to</b> receiving a double portion of <b>inheritance,</b> as they are the firstborn of their fathers, <b>and</b> they are <b>not</b> considered a <b>firstborn with regard to a priest,</b> i.e., with regard to the mitzva of redemption of the firstborn, which applies only to a woman’s firstborn son. There are others who are considered a <b>firstborn with regard to a priest and are not</b> considered a <b>firstborn with regard to inheritance. And who is</b> considered a <b>firstborn with regard to inheritance</b> who is <b>not a firstborn with regard to a priest?</b> In each of these five cases, the mishna first explains the opening portion of its introductory statement and only then explains the second part of its introductory statement.
The Gemara explains: In <b>these</b> cases, <b>because there are many [<i>avshu</i>]</b> categories, the mishna <b>explains</b> the statement with <b>which it began first.</b> However, when there are only two categories, the mishna first provides detail for the latter part of its opening statement.
The Gemara asks: <b>Didn’t</b> the mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 51b) state: <b>With what may an animal go out</b> into the public domain on Shabbat <b>and with what may it not go out?</b> This is a case <b>that does not have many</b> categories, <b>and</b> yet the mishna <b>teaches: A camel may go out</b> on Shabbat with an <i>afsar</i>, etc., which clarifies the opening portion of the mishna’s introductory statement.

Daf 3a

<b>Rather,</b> the Mishna is <b>not particular</b> with regard to this matter, and there is no consistent pattern. <b>Sometimes it explains first that</b> subject with which <b>it began,</b> and <b>sometimes it explains first that</b> subject <b>with which</b> the introductory line in the mishna <b>finished. And if you wish, say</b> an alternate explanation of the order of the mishna here: With regard to <b>intimations, since they are derived from</b> the <b>exposition</b> of verses and are not explicitly mentioned in the Torah, the <i>tanna</i> cherishes them and <b>explains them first.</b>
The Gemara asks: If so, <b>let him begin</b> the mishna <b>with that,</b> i.e., intimations, <b>first.</b> The Gemara answers: The <i>tanna</i> <b>begins with substitutes</b> for the language of vows, which are written <b>in the Torah, in the first clause, and then explains intimations, which are derived from</b> the <b>exposition</b> of verses.
The Gemara asks: <b>This works out well according to the one who said</b> that <b>substitutes</b> for the language of vows <b>are</b> terms for vows in a <b>foreign language.</b> Consequently, they may be considered to have been written in the Torah, as vows are certainly valid regardless of the language in which they are expressed. <b>However, according to the one who says</b> that these substitute terms are simply <b>language that the Sages invented</b> for one <b>to</b> use in taking a <b>vow</b> so as to minimize using God’s name in expressing a vow, <b>what can be said?</b> These include novelties just as intimations do.
The Gemara responds: <b>Does</b> the mishna explicitly <b>teach</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>intimations</b> of vows? <b>Do you not consider it incomplete,</b> missing the phrase that mentions intimations? Once you are inserting this phrase into the mishna, you can <b>also</b> have it <b>precede</b> the clause about substitutes for the language of vows <b>and teach</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>intimations</b> at the beginning, so that the mishna reads as follows: <b>All intimations of vows are like vows, and all substitutes</b> for the language <b>of vows are like vows. And these are intimations: One who says to his fellow:</b> I am avowed from you, etc. <b>And these are substitutes</b> for the language of vows: <b><i>Konam</i>, <i>konaḥ</i>, <i>konas</i>.</b>
§ Apropos the discussion of intimations of vows, the Gemara asks: <b>And where are intimations</b> of vows <b>written,</b> i.e., from where in the Torah is the <i>halakha</i> of intimations of vows derived? The Gemara explains that it is from the verse: <b>“When a man</b> or a woman <b>shall clearly utter a vow, the vow of a nazirite [<i>nazir</i>], to consecrate [<i>lehazir</i>] himself to the Lord”</b> (Numbers 6:2). <b>And it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that the doubled term <b>“<i>nazir lehazir</i>”</b> serves <b>to render substitutes</b> for the language <b>of nazirite</b> vows <b>like nazirite</b> vows, <b>and intimations of nazirite</b> vows <b>like nazirite</b> vows.
<b>I have</b> derived <b>only</b> intimations of <b>nazirite</b> vows; <b>from where</b> do I derive intimations of general <b>vows? The verse states: “When a man</b> or woman <b>shall clearly utter a vow, the vow of a nazirite, to consecrate himself to the Lord.”</b> This verse <b>juxtaposes nazirite</b> vows <b>to</b> other <b>vows and</b> other <b>vows to nazirite</b> vows: <b>Just as</b> with regard to <b>nazirite</b> vows, the verse <b>rendered intimations of nazirite</b> vows <b>like nazirite</b> vows, <b>so too,</b> with regard to <b>vows, it rendered intimations of vows like vows.</b>
<b>And just as</b> with regard to <b>vows,</b> one who breaks his vow <b>transgresses</b> the prohibition: <b>He shall not profane</b> (see Numbers 30:3), <b>and</b> if he does not fulfill his vow in time, he transgresses the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay</b> (see Deuteronomy 23:22), <b>so too,</b> with regard to <b>nazirite</b> vows, <b>he transgresses</b> the prohibition: <b>He shall not profane, and</b> the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay. And</b> furthermore, <b>just as</b> with regard to <b>vows, a father may nullify the vows of his daughter and a husband may nullify the vows of his wife,</b> as written explicitly in the passage concerning vows (Numbers, chapter 30), <b>so too,</b> with regard to <b>nazirite</b> vows, <b>a father may nullify the nazirite</b> vows <b>of his daughter and a husband may nullify the nazirite</b> vows <b>of his wife.</b>
The Gemara questions this explanation: <b>What is different with regard to nazirite</b> vows, with regard to <b>which it is written “<i>nazir lehazir</i>,”</b> using the doubled term, when with regard to all <b>vows as well it is written: “To utter a vow [<i>lindor neder</i>],”</b> also using a doubled term? <b>Why do I</b> need <b>the juxtaposition</b> of all other vows to nazirite vows in order to derive that intimations of vows are like vows, when this can be derived from the doubled term with regard to general vows?
The Gemara answers: <b>If</b> the Torah <b>had written: A vow to utter [<i>neder lindor</i>], as it wrote</b> with regard to a nazirite: “The vow of <b>a nazirite, to consecrate himself [<i>nazir lehazir</i>],”</b> it would be <b>as you said,</b> and <b>there</b> would be <b>no need for the juxtaposition. Now that it is written: “To utter a vow [<i>lindor neder</i>],”</b> it is possible to say that <b>the Torah spoke in the language of men,</b> and nothing can be derived from the phrase <i>lindor neder</i>, which is simply a common manner of speech.
The Gemara asks: <b>This works out well according to the one who holds</b> that <b>the Torah spoke in the language of men, but according to the one who does not hold</b> that <b>the Torah spoke in the language of men,</b> any doubled term comes to teach something. <b>What does he do with this</b> phrase: <b>“To utter a vow [<i>lindor neder</i>]”?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>He expounds it to render intimations of vows like vows</b> themselves. <b>And</b> the verse <b>juxtaposes nazirite</b> vows <b>to</b> other <b>vows</b> to teach that intimations of vows are like vows with regard to nazirite vows, and to teach the other <i>halakhot</i> mentioned above. With regard to the phrase: “The vow of <b>a nazirite, to consecrate himself [<i>nazir lehazir</i>],” he expounds:</b> This <b>teaches</b>

Daf 3b

<b>that a</b> term of <b>naziriteship takes effect upon a</b> previously accepted term of <b>naziriteship.</b> Consequently, if one became a nazirite and then again declared: I am hereby a nazirite, then when his term of naziriteship is completed he must observe a second term of naziriteship.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to the one who says that the Torah spoke in the language of men</b> and therefore nothing can be derived from the phrase “to utter a vow [<i>lindor neder</i>],” <b>and he expounds</b> the phrase <b>“the vow of a nazirite to consecrate himself [<i>nazir lehazir</i>]” to render intimations of nazirite</b> vows <b>like nazirite</b> vows, <b>from where does he</b> derive that a term of <b>naziriteship takes effect upon a</b> previously accepted term of <b>naziriteship? This works out well if he holds in accordance with the one who says</b> that a term of <b>naziriteship does not take effect upon a</b> previously accepted term of <b>naziriteship; however, if he holds in accordance with the one who says</b> that a term of <b>naziriteship takes effect upon a</b> previously accepted term of <b>naziriteship, from where does he</b> derive this <i>halakha</i>?
The Gemara answers: <b>Let the verse say: To consecrate himself [<i>lizor</i>]. What</b> is the reason the verse expressed this same idea with the word <b><i>lehazir</i>? Learn two</b> <i>halakhot</i> <b>from this:</b> That intimations of nazirite vows are considered nazirite vows, and that a term of naziriteship takes effect upon a previously accepted term of naziriteship.
The Gemara adds: <b>In the West,</b> Eretz Yisrael, they <b>say: There is a <i>tanna</i> who derives intimations</b> of vows <b>from</b> the phrase <b>“to utter a vow [<i>lindor neder</i>],”</b> as he holds that the Torah did not speak in the language of men. <b>And</b> conversely, <b>there is a <i>tanna</i> who</b> holds that the Torah spoke in the language of men, and therefore <b>derives this</b> <i>halakha</i> of intimations <b>from</b> the verse: <b>“He shall do according to all that proceeds out of his mouth”</b> (Numbers 30:3). The inclusive formulation of this verse comes to include intimations of vows.
§ <b>The Master said</b> in the <i>baraita</i> cited earlier: <b>Just as</b> with regard to <b>vows, one</b> who breaks his vow <b>transgresses</b> the prohibition: <b>He shall not profane, and</b> one who does not fulfill his vow in time transgresses the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay,</b> so too, the same is true with regard to nazirite vows. The Gemara asks: <b>Granted, you</b> can <b>find</b> a case <b>where</b> one transgresses the prohibition: <b>He shall not profane, in</b> the case of <b>vows. For example, where one said: I will eat this loaf, and he does not eat it, he violates</b> the prohibition: <b>He shall not profane his word.</b>
<b>However,</b> with regard to transgressing the prohibition: <b>He shall not profane, in</b> the case of <b>nazirite</b> vows, <b>how can you find these</b> circumstances? <b>Once he said: I am hereby a nazirite, he is a nazirite</b> as of that moment. If he then <b>ate</b> grapes, <b>he is liable for</b> violating the prohibition: <b>He shall not eat</b> (see Numbers 6:4), and if he <b>drank</b> wine, <b>he is liable for</b> violating the prohibition: <b>He shall not drink</b> (see Numbers 6:3). When would he ever become liable for violating the prohibition against profanation? <b>Rava said:</b> The prohibition against profanation serves <b>to render him liable for</b> violating <b>two</b> prohibitions. Consequently, if he eats grapes or drinks wine, he transgresses the relevant prohibition in addition to the prohibition against profanation.
The Gemara further asks: With regard to violating the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay, in</b> the case of <b>nazirite</b> vows, <b>how can you find these</b> circumstances? <b>Once he said: I am hereby a nazirite, he is a nazirite</b> as of that moment. If he then <b>ate</b> grapes, <b>he is liable for</b> violating the prohibition: <b>He shall not eat.</b> When would he ever become liable for violating the prohibition against delaying? The Gemara answers: It is <b>when he</b> specifically <b>says: I will become a nazirite when I wish,</b> in which case he does not become a nazirite immediately. The Gemara asks: <b>But if he said: When I wish, there is no</b> prohibition of: <b>You shall not delay,</b> as there is no particular time by which he must become a nazirite.
<b>Rava said:</b> It is, <b>for example, when he said: I will not depart the world until I become a nazirite, as he is a nazirite from that time</b> because he does not know when he will depart this world. This is <b>just as it is</b> in the case of a man <b>who says to his wife: This is your bill of divorce</b> that will take effect <b>one hour before my death.</b> If he is a priest and she is the daughter of a non-priest, <b>she is prohibited from partaking of <i>teruma</i> immediately. Apparently, we say every moment</b> that <b>perhaps he is</b> now <b>dead</b> and she is therefore already divorced. <b>Here, too,</b> with regard to naziriteship, <b>he is a nazirite immediately, as we say</b> that <b>perhaps he is now</b> about to <b>die.</b>

Daf 4a

<b>Rav Aḥa bar Ya’akov said:</b> The prohibition against delaying applies to a nazirite in a case <b>where one vowed</b> to become a nazirite <b>while he was in a cemetery.</b> Since it is prohibited for a nazirite to be in a cemetery, his term of naziriteship does not come into effect until he leaves the cemetery and ritually purifies himself. If he delays this process, he prevents the vow from taking effect and thereby transgresses the prohibition against delaying.
The Gemara asks: <b>This works out well according to the one who says</b> that if one takes a vow of naziriteship while in a cemetery <b>the naziriteship does not apply to him immediately. However, according to the one who said</b> that the naziriteship <b>applies to him immediately, is there a</b> violation of the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay? And furthermore, didn’t Mar bar Rav Ashi say</b> that all agree that the <b>naziriteship applies to him immediately</b> and he need not repeat his vow of naziriteship when he leaves, <b>and when they disagree</b> it is only <b>with regard to the matter of flogging,</b> and it’s only about this that they <b>disagree,</b> i.e., whether the nazirite is flogged if he drinks wine or otherwise violates the prohibitions of a nazirite.
The Gemara answers: <b>Even so, he is liable for</b> violating the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay, because</b> by remaining in the cemetery <b>he delays a naziriteship of ritual purity.</b> Although the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship take effect upon him immediately, he is still obligated to fulfill a term of naziriteship in a state of ritual purity, and if he delays doing so, he violates the prohibition against delaying. <b>Rav Ashi said: Since this is so, a nazirite who intentionally renders himself ritually impure violates</b> the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay,</b> with regard to <b>a naziriteship of ritual purity,</b> as he thereby prevents himself from completing his naziriteship in a state of ritual purity on time.
<b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rav Ika, stated</b> another explanation of how the prohibition against delaying applies with regard to naziriteship: The nazirite is commanded to shave his hair at the conclusion of his term, and if he causes this shaving to be delayed, <b>he violates</b> the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay,</b> with regard to <b>his shaving.</b>
The Gemara adds: <b>And</b> this explanation <b>is not necessary</b> to say, i.e., it is obvious, <b>according to the one who says</b> that <b>shaving is indispensable</b> for a nazirite and he is subject to all the prohibitions of a nazirite until he shaves, <b>but even according to the one who says</b> that <b>shaving is not indispensable</b> and the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship are terminated for him immediately after he brings his offerings, <b>at least</b> it can be said that <b>he does not fulfill the mitzva of shaving</b> until he actually shaves, and therefore if he delays, he violates the prohibition against delaying.
<b>Mar Zutra, son of Rav Mari, stated</b> another explanation of how the prohibition against delaying applies to a nazirite vow: <b>He transgresses</b> the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay,</b> with regard to <b>his offerings.</b> When he completes his term as a nazirite he is obligated to bring certain offerings, and if he delays bringing them, he violates this prohibition.
The Gemara asks: <b>Is this</b> <i>halakha</i>, that the prohibition against delaying applies to a nazirite vow, <b>derived from here,</b> i.e., the juxtaposition of vows and naziriteship? <b>It is derived from there,</b> a different exposition based upon the verse: “When you shall take a vow…you shall not delay to pay it; for the Lord your God <b>will surely require it</b> of you” (Deuteronomy 23:22). <b>Those</b> items included in the phrase “will surely require it” <b>are sin-offerings and guilt-offerings,</b> including the sin-offering and guilt-offering of a nazirite.
The Gemara explains: The derivation based on the juxtaposition of vows and naziriteship is still necessary <b>lest you say that</b> these offerings are <b>a novelty that the Torah innovated with regard to a nazirite</b> and therefore their <i>halakhot</i> cannot be derived from <i>halakhot</i> of other offerings.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the novelty</b> that the Torah introduced with regard to the nazirite? <b>If we say</b> it is <b>that he cannot accept</b> upon <b>himself the sin-offering of a nazirite through a vow,</b> i.e., if one who is not a nazirite says: I hereby vow to bring the sin-offering of a nazirite, his vow has no validity, this is not a unique <i>halakha</i> with regard to sin-offerings of a nazirite. There is also the case of <b>the sin-offering of forbidden fat,</b> i.e., a typical sin-offering that one brings when he inadvertently violates certain prohibitions, one of which is eating forbidden fat, <b>which one cannot accept through a vow,</b> as only one who has violated the prohibition may bring it; <b>and</b> nevertheless, <b>one</b> still <b>transgresses</b> the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay,</b> if he delays bringing the offering.
<b>Rather, what is its novelty</b> that makes the juxtaposition between vows and naziriteship necessary? It might <b>enter your mind to say</b> that <b>since if he said: I am hereby a nazirite, even</b> if he accepted upon himself only one of the prohibitions of a nazirite, e.g., the requirement to abstain <b>from grape seeds, he is</b> nevertheless <b>a nazirite with regard to all</b> aspects of naziriteship, therefore <b>say</b> that <b>he does not transgress</b> the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay.</b> Since this <i>halakha</i> is a novelty, perhaps the principles of vows do not apply. The verse therefore <b>teaches us</b> through the juxtaposition of vows and naziriteship that the prohibition against delaying applies even in this case.
The Gemara asks: <b>This works out well according to the one who said</b> that <b>when</b> one vows to be <b>a nazirite from grape seeds he is a nazirite in all</b> respects. <b>However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, who said</b> that <b>he is not a nazirite unless he</b> explicitly <b>vows to be a nazirite in all</b> respects, <b>what is there to say? And furthermore, this novelty is stringent</b> in that it renders the individual a nazirite even if he did not explicitly accept all of the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship. How could this novelty indicate that the prohibition against delaying does not apply in this case, which is a leniency, so that the juxtaposition would be necessary?
<b>Rather, what is its novelty</b> that makes the juxtaposition between vows and naziriteship necessary? It might <b>enter your mind to say</b> that <b>since</b>

Daf 4b

<b>if he shaves upon</b> bringing <b>one of the three</b> offerings that a nazirite must bring when completing his term as a nazirite, i.e., a burnt-offering, a sin-offering, and a peace-offering, <b>he has fulfilled</b> his obligation to shave and the restrictions of a nazirite are lifted, <b>he</b> therefore <b>does not violate</b> the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay,</b> for delaying the other offerings. Consequently, <b>it teaches us</b> that the prohibition against delaying applies to these offerings.
<b>And if you wish say: What is the novelty</b> of the nazirite? The <i>halakha</i> of naziriteship is considered a novelty <b>because</b> the offerings of a nazirite <b>cannot be accepted through a vow. And that which</b> poses <b>a difficulty for you</b> based on the fact that one cannot accept upon himself <b>a sin-offering of forbidden fat</b> through a vow either, and nevertheless the prohibition against delaying applies, can be resolved. <b>A sin-offering of forbidden fat comes for</b> the purpose of <b>atonement,</b> and therefore if one delays bringing it he violates the prohibition against delaying. However, <b>for what does the sin-offering of a nazirite come?</b> Since it does not come to atone for a sin, one might have thought that the prohibition against delaying does not apply.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> there is the precedent of <b>the sin-offering of a woman who gave birth, which</b> also <b>does not come for</b> the purpose of <b>atonement, and</b> yet if she does not bring the offering on time she still <b>transgresses due to</b> the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> sin-offering of a woman who gave birth <b>permits</b> the woman <b>to eat consecrated food.</b> Although it does not come to effect atonement, it does come to permit a matter. On the other hand, the sin-offering of a nazirite does not permit anything, and therefore the fact that one cannot accept upon himself an obligation to bring this offering is a novelty. Consequently, it was necessary for there to be a separate source to indicate that one is liable for violating the prohibition against delaying in the case of this offering.
§ <b>The Master said</b> in the <i>baraita</i> cited above: <b>Just as</b> with regard to <b>vows, a father may nullify the vows of his daughter and a husband may nullify the vows of his wife, so too,</b> with regard to <b>nazirite</b> vows, <b>a father may nullify the nazirite</b> vows <b>of his daughter and a husband may nullify the nazirite</b> vows <b>of his wife.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Why do I</b> need <b>the juxtaposition</b> in the verse to teach us this <i>halakha</i>? <b>Let</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>come through</b> the method of analogy known as: <b>What do we find, from</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>vows.</b> In other words, the <i>halakha</i> in the case of regular vows should serve as a legal precedent that can be applied to nazirite vows even without a biblical juxtaposition.
The Gemara responds: If not for the juxtaposition, one might have said that <b>perhaps</b> it is only <b>with regard to vows that he may nullify</b> her statements <b>because there is no</b> time <b>limit</b> with regard to vows, <b>but with regard to naziriteship, which has a</b> time <b>limit, as unspecified naziriteship is</b> for <b>thirty days,</b> one might <b>say no,</b> he may not nullify her vows. Therefore, <b>it teaches us</b> through juxtaposition that he may nevertheless nullify her vows.
§ It is taught in the mishna with regard to <b>one who says to his fellow: I am avowed</b> from you, or another intimation of a vow, that the vow takes effect. <b>Shmuel said: In all</b> these cases, the vow does not take effect <b>until he says:</b> I am avowed from you with regard to <b>that</b> which <b>I eat</b> of <b>yours,</b> or: <b>That</b> which <b>I taste</b> of <b>yours.</b> Without this addition, the statement: I am avowed from you, is not considered even an intimation of a vow.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> based on the following <i>baraita</i>: If one says: <b>I am avowed from you,</b> or: <b>I am separated from you,</b> or: <b>I am distanced from you, he is prohibited</b> from benefiting from that individual. If he says: <b>That</b> which <b>I eat</b> of <b>yours,</b> or: <b>That</b> which <b>I taste</b> of <b>yours, he is prohibited</b> from eating or tasting that individual’s food. This indicates that the phrases mentioned in the first clause of the <i>baraita</i> take effect as vows even if he does not add the phrases mentioned in the latter clause. The Gemara answers: <b>This is what</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>is teaching: In what</b> case <b>is this statement said</b> i.e., if one says: I am avowed from you, or the like, the vow takes effect? It is <b>in</b> a case of <b>one who says: That</b> which <b>I eat</b> of <b>yours,</b> or: <b>That</b> which <b>I taste</b> of <b>yours.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> in <b>the opposite</b> order? If one says: <b>That</b> which <b>I eat</b> of <b>yours,</b> or: <b>That</b> which <b>I taste</b> of <b>yours, he is prohibited</b> from eating or tasting that individual’s food, i.e., the vow takes effect. If he says: <b>I am avowed from you, or: I am separated from you,</b> or: <b>I am distanced from you, he is</b> likewise <b>prohibited.</b> In this case, the <i>baraita</i> cannot be interpreted as explained above, and it appears that if one said: I am avowed from you, the vow takes effect even if he did not say: I am avowed from you with regard to that which I taste of yours. The Gemara answers: <b>Teach</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>as follows:</b> The vow takes effect in the cases mentioned in the first clause when <b>he already said: I am avowed.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>If so, this is</b> the same as <b>the first</b> <i>baraita</i> cited above. <b>And furthermore,</b> if the vow takes effect only if he combined the statements mentioned in the first and latter clauses of the <i>baraita</i>, <b>why does</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teach</b> that he is <b>prohibited</b> in the first clause of the <i>baraita</i> and then repeat that he is <b>prohibited</b> in the second clause of the <i>baraita</i>? The repetition of this ruling indicates that these are two separate cases.
<b>Rather, Shmuel</b> actually <b>said</b> as follows: <b>The reason is</b> that <b>he said:</b> I am avowed from you with regard to <b>that</b> which <b>I eat</b> of <b>yours,</b> or with regard to <b>that</b> which <b>I taste</b> of <b>yours;</b> it is for this reason <b>that he is prohibited</b> from benefiting from his fellow, <b>and his fellow is permitted</b> to benefit from him, as this formulation indicates that he is applying his vow only to himself.

Daf 5a

<b>However,</b> if <b>he said only: I am avowed from you, they are both prohibited</b> from deriving benefit from one another. This is <b>like that which Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, said:</b> If one says: <b>I am avowed from you, they are both prohibited.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>We learned</b> in a mishna (47b) that if one says to another: <b>I am hereby to you</b> like an item <b>dedicated</b> to the Temple, <b>the one to whom the vow was said is prohibited</b> from deriving benefit from the one who made the vow, <b>but the one who made the vow is not</b> prohibited from deriving benefit from the one with regard to whom the vow was said. However, according to Shmuel, both should be prohibited. The Gemara answers: It is referring to a case <b>where he specified</b> this by saying: <b>And you are not</b> like an item dedicated to the Temple <b>for me.</b>
The Gemara asks: That mishna also taught that if one says: <b>You are to me</b> like an item <b>dedicated</b> to the Temple, <b>the one who makes the vow is prohibited</b> to derive benefit from the other, <b>but the one to whom the vow was said is not</b> prohibited from deriving benefit from the one who makes the vow. However, according to Shmuel, both should be prohibited. The Gemara answers: Here too, it is referring to a case <b>where he specified</b> this by saying: <b>And I am not</b> like an item dedicated to the Temple <b>for you.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> if he stated his vow in an <b>unspecified</b> manner, <b>what, are they both prohibited</b> from deriving benefit from each other? <b>From</b> the fact <b>that it teaches in the latter clause</b> that if one says to another: <b>I am hereby to you, and you are to me,</b> like an item dedicated to the Temple, <b>they are both prohibited</b> from deriving benefit from each other, it can be derived that <b>it is in this</b> case <b>both are prohibited, but</b> if one stated his vow in an <b>unspecified</b> manner, <b>he is prohibited</b> from deriving benefit from the other individual <b>and the other is permitted</b> to derive benefit from him. This is not in accordance with the opinion of Shmuel.
<b>Rather, this is</b> how the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, was stated:</b> If one said to another: <b>I am avowed to you, they are both prohibited</b> from deriving benefit from one another. However, if he says: <b>I am avowed from you, he is prohibited</b> from deriving benefit from the other person <b>and the other is permitted</b> to derive benefit from him.
The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t the mishna teach</b> a case of one who declared: I am avowed <b>from you, and</b> yet <b>we established the mishna, according to Shmuel,</b> as teaching that <b>in all these</b> cases <b>it is only</b> if <b>he says: That</b> which <b>I taste</b> of <b>yours, or: That</b> which <b>I eat</b> of <b>yours, that he is prohibited</b> from deriving benefit from the other person, <b>and the other is permitted? However,</b> if he merely says: <b>I am avowed from you, they are both prohibited.</b> Consequently, Shmuel does not distinguish between the expressions: I am avowed from you, and: I am avowed to you.
<b>Rather, this is how</b> the opinion <b>of Shmuel was originally stated: The reason is that he said: That</b> which <b>I eat</b> of <b>yours, or: That</b> which <b>I taste</b> of <b>yours.</b> It is for this reason <b>that he is prohibited only from eating</b> anything belonging to the other person. However, if he said: <b>I am avowed from you,</b> without further specification, <b>he is prohibited even from</b> deriving any form of <b>benefit</b> from the other.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so, let Shmuel say as follows: And if he said only: That</b> which <b>I eat</b> of <b>yours, or: That</b> which <b>I taste</b> of <b>yours, he is prohibited only from eating</b> an item belonging to his fellow, but he is permitted to derive benefit from it.
<b>Rather, this is</b> how Shmuel’s opinion <b>was stated: The reason is that he said: That</b> which <b>I eat</b> of <b>yours, or: That</b> which <b>I taste</b> of <b>yours; it is</b> in these cases <b>that he is prohibited</b> from eating any item belonging to his fellow. <b>However,</b> if <b>he said</b> simply: <b>I am avowed from you,</b> that statement <b>does not indicate that he said</b> he is <b>prohibited</b> from eating an item belonging to his fellow. <b>What is the reason</b> for this? The statement: <b>I am avowed from you, indicates: I am not speaking with you.</b> Similarly, the statement: <b>I am separated from you, indicates: I am not doing business with you.</b> The statement: <b>I am distanced from you, indicates that I will not stand within four cubits of you.</b>

Daf 5b

The Gemara asks: If so, <b>shall we say that Shmuel holds</b> that <b>ambiguous intimations are not intimations,</b> i.e., if one employs an incomplete expression to declare a vow and the expression does not state clearly what his intention is, it does not produce a vow? The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, Shmuel establishes the mishna in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, who said: Ambiguous intimations are not intimations.</b>
<b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Gittin</i> 85a–b): <b>The essence of a bill of divorce</b> is the sentence: <b>You are hereby permitted to</b> marry <b>any man. Rabbi Yehuda says</b> there is an additional statement that is an essential part of the divorce document: <b>And this shall be to you from me a document of divorce [<i>teirukhin</i>] and a letter of dismissal.</b> This demonstrates that according to Rabbi Yehuda, the wording of the bill of divorce itself must clarify that the husband is divorcing his wife through the bill of divorce.
The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> does <b>Shmuel strain to establish the mishna</b> as being <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> which is a minority opinion? <b>Let him establish it</b> as being <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis that although there are no obvious intimations</b> in one’s statements, they are still considered vows. Consequently, if one said: I am avowed to you, even if he did not add: With regard to that which I eat, the vow takes effect.
<b>Rava said: The mishna was difficult for him. Why does it teach</b> the cases where one adds: <b>That</b> which <b>I eat</b> of <b>yours,</b> and: <b>That</b> which <b>I taste</b> of <b>yours? Let it teach: That</b> which <b>I eat,</b> and: <b>That</b> which <b>I taste,</b> without the additional phrase: Of yours. Since the one taking the vow is addressing another individual, it is clear to whom he is referring even without this phrase. <b>Conclude from this</b> that <b>we require obvious intimations,</b> i.e., the intent of the individual taking the vow must be indicated by his verbal statement and not merely by the context of his statement.
§ The Gemara addresses more fully the issue mentioned in passing in the previous discussion. <b>It was stated</b> that the <i>amora’im</i> disagreed with regard to <b>ambiguous intimations. Abaye said: They are</b> valid <b>intimations, and Rava said: They are not</b> valid <b>intimations. Rava said: Rabbi Idi explained to me</b> the source of this ruling. <b>The verse states:</b> “The vow of a <b>nazirite, to consecrate himself [<i>nazir lehazir</i>] to the Lord”</b> (Numbers 6:2). The verse <b>juxtaposes intimations of naziriteship,</b> derived earlier (3a) from the doubled term “<i>nazir lehazir</i>,” <b>to naziriteship.</b> This indicates that <b>just as</b> accepting <b>naziriteship</b> must be expressed <b>with a distinct articulation, so too, intimations of naziriteship</b> must be expressed <b>with a distinct articulation</b> as opposed to ambiguous intimations.
The Gemara proposes: <b>Let us say that</b> these <i>amora’im</i> <b>disagree with regard to the</b> tannaitic <b>dispute between Rabbi Yehuda and the Rabbis. As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Gittin</i> 85a–b): <b>The essence of a bill of divorce</b> is the sentence: <b>You are hereby permitted to</b> marry <b>any man. Rabbi Yehuda says</b> that there is an additional statement that is an essential part of the divorce document: <b>And this shall be to you from me a document of divorce, a bill of release, and a letter of dismissal.</b> One could suggest that <b>Abaye,</b> who holds that ambiguous intimations are valid intimations, <b>said</b> his statement <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, and Rava,</b> who holds that ambiguous intimations are not valid intimations, <b>said</b> his statement <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda.</b>
The Gemara responds: <b>Abaye</b> could have <b>said to you: I say</b> my statement <b>even in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda. Rabbi Yehuda says</b> that <b>we require obvious intimations only with regard to a bill of divorce, as we require</b> full <b>severance</b> of the relationship, <b>and there is not</b> full severance unless the bill of divorce clearly states that the husband is divorcing his wife through that document. <b>However, did you hear him</b> state <b>generally</b> that ambiguous intimations are not valid intimations?
<b>And Rava</b> could have <b>said: I say</b> my statement <b>even in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis. The Rabbis say that we do not require obvious intimations only with regard to a bill of divorce,</b>

Daf 6a

<b>as a man does not divorce the wife of his fellow,</b> so it is obvious that he is writing the bill of divorce to his wife. <b>However, did you hear them</b> state <b>generally</b> that ambiguous intimations are valid intimations?
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> based upon the following <i>baraita</i>: If one says with regard to an object lying before him: <b>It is upon me,</b> or: <b>This is hereby upon me,</b> it <b>is forbidden, because it is an intimation</b> that he is rendering the object forbidden to himself like <b>an offering.</b> This <i>baraita</i> indicates that <b>the reason it is forbidden is</b> that <b>he</b> specifically <b>said: Upon me. However,</b> if <b>he did not say: Upon me, no,</b> it is not forbidden, because this expression is an ambiguous intimation. This is <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Abaye,</b> who holds that even ambiguous intimations are valid intimations.
The Gemara answers: <b>Abaye</b> could have <b>said to you</b> that <b>the reason it is forbidden is</b> that he specifically <b>said: Upon me. However,</b> if <b>he said: It is hereby, and did not say: Upon me,</b> his statement is not considered an intimation of a vow at all, as he could have been <b>saying: It is hereby ownerless</b> property, or: <b>It is hereby charity.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches</b> that the object is forbidden <b>because</b> his statement <b>is an intimation</b> that he is rendering the object forbidden to himself like <b>an offering.</b> With regard to consecrating an offering, even the statement: This is, without the additional phrase: Upon me, is considered an intimation. Nevertheless, the <i>baraita</i> indicates that if one does not say: Upon me, the vow does not take effect. This must be because an ambiguous intimation is not a vow.
<b>Rather, say</b> the following: <b>The reason is that he</b> specifically <b>said: Upon me;</b> consequently, <b>he is prohibited</b> from benefiting from the object, <b>but another</b> person <b>is permitted</b> to benefit from it. <b>However,</b> if <b>he said: It is hereby,</b> and did not add the expression: Upon me, <b>both of them are prohibited</b> from benefiting from it <b>because perhaps he is saying: It is hereby consecrated</b> property. In other words, there is concern that he may have actually consecrated the object rather than taking a vow that it is forbidden to him as though it were consecrated.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> to this on the basis of the following <i>baraita</i>: If one says: <b>This is hereby a sin-offering,</b> or: <b>This is hereby a guilt-offering,</b> then <b>even if he is liable</b> to bring <b>a sin-offering or guilt-offering he has said nothing,</b> as this is an ambiguous intimation. However, if he said: <b>This is hereby my sin-offering,</b> or: <b>This is hereby my guilt-offering,</b> then <b>if he was liable</b> to bring that offering <b>his statement takes effect,</b> because this is an obvious intimation. This is <b>a conclusive refutation of</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Abaye</b> could have <b>said to you:</b> In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> <i>baraita</i>? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who holds that ambiguous intimations are not intimations. The Gemara asks: <b>Wasn’t it Abaye who said: I say</b> that ambiguous intimations are valid intimations <b>even according to</b> the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda? The Gemara answers: <b>He retracted</b> this statement and admitted that Rabbi Yehuda holds that ambiguous intimations are not valid intimations.
The Gemara asks: <b>But shall we say</b> that just as Abaye retracted his statement that his opinion can be stated even according to Rabbi Yehuda, <b>Rava, who said</b> that ambiguous intimations are not valid intimations, also retracted his initial statement and admitted that his opinion is only <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> and is not in accordance with the opinion of the Rabbis?
The Gemara answers: <b>Rava</b> could have <b>said to you: I say</b> my statement <b>even according to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis. The Rabbis say that we do not require obvious intimations only with regard to a bill of divorce, as a man does not divorce the wife of his fellow,</b> so it is obvious that he is writing the bill of divorce to his wife. <b>However, generally we require obvious intimations.</b>

Daf 6b

§ <b>Rav Pappa raised a dilemma: Is there intimation for betrothal or not?</b> Does betrothal take effect via an incomplete statement? The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of such a case? <b>If we say</b> it is a case <b>where one said to a woman: You are hereby betrothed to me, and he said to another</b> woman: <b>And you too, isn’t</b> it <b>obvious</b> that <b>this is betrothal itself,</b> and it takes effect? <b>Rather,</b> it is a case <b>where one said to a woman: You are hereby betrothed to me, and he said to another</b> woman: <b>And you. Do we say</b> that <b>he said the other</b> woman: <b>And you too</b> are betrothed, <b>and betrothal takes effect with regard to the other</b> woman, <b>or perhaps he said to the other</b> woman: <b>And you see</b> that I am betrothing this woman, <b>and betrothal does not take effect with regard to the other</b> woman?
The Gemara asks: <b>Did Rav Pappa raise this</b> as a <b>dilemma? But from</b> the fact <b>that Rav Pappa said to Abaye</b> in a case concerning betrothal (see <i>Kiddushin</i> 5b): <b>Does Shmuel hold that ambiguous intimations are</b> valid <b>intimations,</b> it can be proven <b>by inference that Rav Pappa holds that there is intimation for betrothal.</b> The Gemara answers: Rav Pappa <b>spoke to Abaye</b> about <b>one of</b> the opinions <b>that Shmuel held.</b> Shmuel had ruled that even an ambiguous intimation was sufficient in the case of betrothal, and Rav Pappa questioned this ruling without expressing his own opinion that even obvious intimations are not valid with regard to betrothal.
§ <b>Rav Pappa raised</b> another <b>dilemma: Is there intimation for <i>pe’a</i>,</b> the produce in the corner of a field that must be left for the poor, <b>or is there no intimation for <i>pe’a</i>?</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of the case? <b>If we say</b> it is a case <b>where one said: This furrow [<i>ugeya</i>] shall be <i>pe’a</i> and this</b> one <b>too, this is</b> a <b>full-fledged</b> declaration of <b><i>pe’a</i>.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>He raises the dilemma</b> with regard to a case <b>where he said: And this, and he did not say:</b> And this one <b>too,</b> and therefore his statement can be interpreted to mean that this other furrow should remain his and not be included in the <i>pe’a</i> (<i>Tosafot</i>). <b>What</b> is the <i>halakha</i> in this case?
The Gemara interrupts this train of thought and wonders: Does this prove <b>by inference that</b> in a case <b>where one said: The entire field shall be <i>pe’a</i>,</b> it would all be rendered <b><i>pe’a</i>?</b> The case must be one where the first furrow was large enough to serve as <i>pe’a</i> for the entire field, because if that were not the case, it would be clear that he meant that the second furrow should also be <i>pe’a</i>. Consequently, it is clear from Rav Pappa’s question that one can designate as <i>pe’a</i> a larger portion of the field than one is absolutely required to designate.
The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, and it is taught</b> in the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>From where</b> is it derived <b>that if one wants to render his entire field <i>pe’a</i>,</b> he may <b>do</b> so? <b>The verse states:</b> “You shall not wholly reap <b>the corner of your field”</b> (Leviticus 19:9). This is expounded to mean that the entirety of “your field” may be designated as the “corner” that is left for the poor.
The Gemara returns to its discussion of Rav Pappa’s dilemma. <b>Do we say</b> that <b>since</b> <i>pe’a</i> is <b>juxtaposed to offerings, just as offerings have intimation, so too, <i>pe’a</i> has intimation? Or perhaps when</b> <i>pe’a</i> is juxtaposed to offerings, <b>it is juxtaposed to</b> indicate only that it is subject to the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay?</b> The Gemara clarifies: <b>And where is it juxtaposed? As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>:

Daf 7a

The verse states with regard to offerings: “When you shall take a vow to the Lord your God, you shall not delay to pay it; for the Lord your God will surely require it of you” (Deuteronomy 23:22). With regard to the term <b>“of you”</b> the <i>baraita</i> states: <b>This is</b> a reference to <b>gleanings, forgotten</b> sheaves, <b>and <i>pe’a</i>.</b>
§ The Gemara asks: <b>Is there intimation for charity or is there no intimation for charity?</b> The Gemara clarifies the question: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of such a case? <b>If we say that</b> it is a case where <b>one said: This dinar is for charity and this also, that itself is</b> an explicit statement of donating to <b>charity. Rather,</b> it is a case <b>where he said: This, and did not say: Also. What</b> is his intention? Is he understood to be <b>saying: This is also charity, or perhaps what is the meaning of: And this?</b> He is <b>saying</b> that this coin is <b>merely for general use, and he did not complete his statement.</b>
The Gemara explains the two sides of this dilemma: <b>Do we say</b> that <b>since</b> charity is <b>juxtaposed to offerings, as it is written</b> in a verse following the prohibition against delaying an offering: “That you have spoken <b>with your mouth”</b> (Deuteronomy 23:24) and the Sages expounded that <b>this is</b> a reference to <b>charity,</b> therefore, <b>just as there is intimation,</b> i.e., intimation is effective, <b>with regard to offerings, so too, there is intimation with regard to charity? Or perhaps</b> it is only with regard to the prohibition: <b>You shall not delay, that it is juxtaposed,</b> but not with regard to other <i>halakhot</i>?
The Gemara asks further: <b>Is there intimation for</b> rendering one’s property <b>ownerless, or perhaps there is no intimation for</b> rendering one’s property <b>ownerless.</b> Does an incomplete expression employed by an owner to relinquish property take effect or not? The Gemara notes: <b>This is</b> the same as the previous question with regard to <b>charity,</b> which is comparable to rendering one’s property ownerless for the benefit of the poor.
The Gemara responds: This question is <b>stated</b> in the style of: <b>If you say,</b> as follows: <b>If you say there is intimation for charity, as there is no partial</b> analogy based on <b>juxtaposition, do we say</b> that rendering one’s property <b>ownerless is</b> the same as <b>charity; or perhaps charity is different, as charity is suitable only for the poor, but ownerless property</b> is suitable for <b>both the poor and the wealthy,</b> and therefore it cannot be derived from the <i>halakha</i> with regard to charity.
<b>Ravina raised</b> another <b>dilemma: Is there intimation for</b> designating a location as <b>a bathroom or not?</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of the case? <b>If we say that</b> it is a case where <b>one said: Let this structure be a bathroom and this</b> one <b>also, that</b> second structure <b>is</b> certainly <b>also a bathroom. Rather,</b> it is a case <b>where he said: And this, and he did not say: Also. What</b> is his intention? Is the expression: <b>And this, that he said,</b> understood to mean: <b>And this</b> shall <b>also be a bathroom? Or perhaps what</b> is the meaning of: <b>And this? He is saying</b> that it is designated <b>for general use</b> rather than as a bathroom.
The Gemara comments: Can it be derived <b>by inference that it is obvious to Ravina that there is designation for a bathroom,</b> i.e., that if one explicitly designates a location as a bathroom, it attains that status even before it is used for that purpose, so that one may not bring sacred items to that location? <b>Didn’t Ravina raise this as a dilemma?</b> He asked: If one <b>designated</b> a particular location <b>as a bathroom, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? If one <b>designated it as a bathhouse, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? In other words, <b>is designation effective</b> to grant the location a particular status, <b>or is designation not effective?</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Ravina raised one dilemma within another</b> dilemma: <b>Is designation effective or is designation not effective?</b> And <b>if you say there is designation,</b> i.e., designation is effective, <b>is there intimation or is there not intimation,</b> i.e., is designation via intimation effective? The Gemara concludes: <b>The dilemma remains</b> unresolved.
§ It was taught in the mishna that if one said: <b>I am ostracized from you,</b> Rabbi Akiva was uncertain about the <i>halakha</i> but was inclined to rule stringently about this. <b>Abaye said: Rabbi Akiva concedes with regard to flogging that one is not flogged</b> if he violates a vow that was expressed in this way. <b>As, if so,</b> if Rabbi Akiva held that one is liable to be flogged, <b>let</b> the mishna <b>teach: Rabbi Akiva is stringent.</b> The fact that it states: Rabbi Akiva was uncertain but was inclined to rule stringently, indicates that although Rabbi Akiva holds that one may not violate this vow, he concedes that one is not liable to be flogged if he does violate the vow.
<b>Rav Pappa said: With</b> regard to a vow that one expressed with the phrase: <b>I am distanced [<i>nadeina</i>] from you, everyone agrees that he is prohibited</b> from deriving benefit from the other individual, as this is an intimation of a vow. If he employs the expression: <b>I am excommunicated [<i>meshamattena</i>] from you everyone agrees</b> that he is <b>permitted</b> to derive benefit from the other person, even though he meant to distance himself from the other individual, because this is not the terminology of a vow. <b>With regard to what do they disagree?</b>

Daf 7b

They disagree <b>with regard to</b> a case when the language one uses is: <b>I am ostracized from you, as Rabbi Akiva holds</b> that <b>it is a language of distancing</b> and therefore expresses a vow, <b>and the Rabbis hold</b> that <b>it is a language of excommunication,</b> and not the terminology with which people express vows.
The Gemara comments: <b>And</b> Rav Pappa <b>disagrees with</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Ḥisda, as</b> demonstrated in the following incident: There was <b>a certain man who said: I am excommunicated</b> from <b>the property of the son of Rav Yirmeya bar Abba. He came before Rav Ḥisda</b> to ask whether this statement was effective in generating a prohibition or not. Rav Ḥisda <b>said to him: There is no one who,</b> in practice, <b>is concerned for that</b> opinion <b>of Rabbi Akiva.</b> Apparently, Rav Ḥisda <b>holds</b> that <b>they</b> also <b>disagree with regard to</b> the phrase: <b>I am excommunicated</b> from you. This indicates that the dispute between the <i>tanna’im</i> is not with regard to specific terms but with regard to the more general question of whether terms of ostracism or excommunication are terms that can also express vows.
§ <b>Rabbi Ila said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> If <b>one ostracized</b> another individual <b>in his presence, one</b> may <b>dissolve</b> it <b>for him only in his presence.</b> If <b>one ostracized him not in his presence, one</b> may <b>dissolve it for him in his presence or not in his presence.</b>
<b>Rav Ḥanin said</b> that <b>Rav said: One who hears mention of the name</b> of God in vain <b>by another</b> individual <b>must ostracize him</b> for doing so. <b>And if he did not ostracize him, he himself,</b> the listener, <b>shall be ostracized, as wherever mention of God’s name</b> in vain <b>is common, poverty is</b> also <b>common there.</b>
<b>And poverty is</b> so harsh that it is considered <b>like death, as it is stated: “For all the men are dead</b> who sought your life” (Exodus 4:19). The Sages had a tradition that Dathan and Abiram had sought to have Moses killed in Egypt and that they were the men referred to in the quoted verse (see 64b). They were still alive at that time but had become impoverished. <b>And</b> additionally, <b>it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Wherever</b> it says that <b>the Sages set their eyes</b> on a particular individual, the result was <b>either death or poverty.</b> This also indicates that death and poverty are equivalent.
<b>Rabbi Abba said: I was standing before Rav Huna,</b> and <b>he heard a certain woman utter a mention of the name</b> of God <b>in vain. He excommunicated her and immediately dissolved</b> the excommunication <b>for her in her presence.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>Learn three things from this. Learn from this</b> that <b>one who hears mention of the name</b> of God in vain <b>by another</b> individual <b>must ostracize him; and learn from this</b> that if <b>one ostracized</b> another <b>in his presence, one</b> may <b>dissolve</b> it <b>for him only in his presence; and learn from this</b> that <b>there is nothing,</b> i.e., no minimum time that must pass, <b>between ostracism and nullification</b> of the ostracism.
<b>Rav Giddel said</b> that <b>Rav said: A Torah scholar</b> can <b>ostracize himself, and he</b> can <b>nullify</b> the ostracism <b>for himself.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Isn’t</b> it <b>obvious</b> that he can nullify the ostracism for himself, just as he is able to do for others? The Gemara answers: It states this <b>lest you say,</b> as per the popular maxim: <b>A prisoner cannot free himself from prison,</b> and since he is ostracized he cannot dissolve the ostracism for himself; therefore it <b>teaches us</b> that he can do so.
The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> where a Torah scholar might ostracize himself? It is <b>like that</b> case involving <b>Mar Zutra Ḥasida. When a student</b> in <b>the academy was liable to</b> receive <b>excommunication,</b> Mar Zutra Ḥasida would <b>first excommunicate himself and then he would excommunicate the student</b> of Torah. <b>And when he would enter his home, he would dissolve</b> the excommunication <b>for himself and then dissolve</b> the excommunication <b>for</b> the student.
<b>And Rav Giddel said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b>

Daf 8a

<b>From where</b> is it derived <b>that one may take an oath to fulfill a mitzva?</b> It is <b>as it is stated: “I have sworn and I have confirmed it, to observe Your righteous ordinances”</b> (Psalms 119:106).
The Gemara asks: <b>Is he not already under oath from</b> when each Jew took an oath at <b>Mount Sinai</b> to fulfill all the mitzvot? An oath cannot take effect if one is already bound by a different oath. <b>Rather,</b> it <b>teaches us this: It is permitted for a man to motivate himself</b> to fulfill the mitzvot in this manner, although the oath is not technically valid.
<b>And Rav Giddel said</b> that <b>Rav said: One who says: I will rise early and study this chapter,</b> or: <b>I will study this tractate, has taken a great vow to the God of Israel.</b> This clearly indicates that the vow takes effect. The Gemara asks: <b>Is he not already under oath</b> due to the general obligation to study Torah? <b>And an oath does not take effect upon a</b> preexisting <b>oath. What is he teaching us?</b> If one claims that Rav Giddel is teaching <b>that</b> such a vow may be made <b>even merely</b> to <b>motivate oneself</b> to study, <b>this is</b> the same as <b>the first</b> statement <b>of Rav Giddel.</b>
The Gemara answers: It <b>teaches us this: Since if he desires he can exempt himself</b> from the obligation to study Torah <b>with the recitation</b> of <b><i>Shema</i></b> in the <b>morning and evening, due to that</b> reason <b>the oath takes effect upon him</b> and he is obligated to study the chapter or tractate that he specified.
<b>Rav Giddel said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> With regard to <b>one who says to another: Let us rise early and study this chapter,</b> and they agree to do so, <b>it is</b> incumbent <b>upon him to rise early</b> and be the first to arrive, because it was his initiative. There is an allusion to this in a verse, <b>as it is stated: “And He said to me: Arise, go forth into the plain, and there I will speak with you.</b> Then I arose <b>and went forth into the plain; and behold, the glory of the Lord stood there”</b> (Ezekiel 3:22–23). God invited Ezekiel to that place, and His glory arrived before Ezekiel did.
§ <b>Rav Yosef said: One</b> who <b>was ostracized in a dream requires ten people to dissolve</b> the ostracism <b>for him. And</b> the ostracism can be dissolved <b>only</b> by those <b>who have studied <i>halakha</i>,</b> i.e., by Torah scholars. <b>However,</b> if they have <b>recited Mishna and have not studied</b> <i>halakha</i>, <b>no,</b> they are not fit to dissolve the ostracism. <b>And if there are not</b> ten people <b>who have studied <i>halakha</i>,</b> then <b>even</b> those <b>who have recited Mishna and have not studied</b> <i>halakha</i> are fit to dissolve the ostracism.
<b>And if there are not</b> even ten people who have studied Mishna, <b>let him go and sit at the crossroads and extend</b> a greeting of <b><i>shalom</i>,</b> meaning peace, <b>to ten people,</b> who will respond with a similar greeting, <b>until ten</b> individuals <b>who have studied <i>halakha</i> happen to come to him.</b>
<b>Ravina said to Rav Ashi:</b> If <b>he knows who excommunicated him</b> in his dream, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Can that individual <b>dissolve</b> the excommunication <b>for him?</b> Rav Ashi <b>said to him:</b> It is possible that <b>he was appointed</b> by Heaven <b>as an agent to excommunicate him,</b> but <b>he was not appointed as an agent to dissolve</b> the excommunication <b>for him.</b> Consequently, the excommunication can be dissolved only by ten people.
<b>Rav Aḥa said to Rav Ashi:</b> If <b>he was excommunicated and</b> the excommunication was <b>dissolved for him in his dream, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Rav Ashi <b>said to him: Just as it is impossible for the grain</b> to grow <b>without straw,</b>

Daf 8b

<b>so too, it is impossible to dream without idle matter.</b> It is possible that the excommunication was real and the dissolution was not real, and he must have the ostracism dissolved by ten people.
The Gemara relates that <b>Ravina’s wife had</b> taken <b>a vow</b> that he had not immediately nullified as a husband is entitled to do, and she wished to have it dissolved. Ravina <b>came before Rav Ashi</b> and <b>said to him: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to whether <b>a husband can become an agent to</b> express <b>his wife’s regret</b> to a court so that they can dissolve her vow? Rav Ashi <b>said to him: If</b> three people who can dissolve the vow <b>were</b> already <b>assembled, yes; if</b> they are <b>not</b> already assembled, <b>no,</b> a husband cannot serve as an agent to express his wife’s regret to the court.
The Gemara comments: <b>Learn from this</b> incident <b>three</b> <i>halakhot</i>: <b>Learn from this</b> that <b>a husband can become an agent to</b> express <b>his wife’s regret. And learn from this</b> that a Sage <b>is not permitted to dissolve a vow in the location of his teacher,</b> which is why Ravina, a distinguished Sage, did not dissolve the vow without consulting Rav Ashi. <b>And learn from this</b> that <b>when</b> three people <b>were</b> already <b>assembled,</b> it <b>seems well</b> for a husband to serve as an agent to express his wife’s regret, but if the three people were not yet assembled he cannot serve as her agent.
The Gemara adds: <b>And</b> one may dissolve <b>excommunication even in the location of his teacher. And an individual</b> Sage who is an <b>expert</b> can <b>dissolve excommunication</b> even without two other judges.
§ <b>Rabbi Shimon bar Zevid said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Tavla said</b> that <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya Arikha,</b> the tall, <b>of the school of Rabbi Aḥa said</b> that <b>Rabbi Zeira said</b> that <b>Rabbi Elazar said</b> that <b>Rabbi Ḥanina said</b> that <b>Rabbi Meyasha said in the name of Rabbi Yehuda bar Elai: What is</b> the meaning of that <b>which is written: “But to you that fear My name shall the sun of righteousness arise</b> with healing in its wings; and you shall go forth and leap as calves of the stall” (Malachi 3:20)? “You that fear My name”; <b>these are people who are afraid to mention the name of Heaven in vain.</b> The verse states that <b>a sun of righteousness with healing</b> will arise to heal them. <b>Abaye said: Learn from this</b> verse that <b>the dust [<i>ḥirga</i>]</b> that is illuminated by the rays of the sun during <b>the day heals,</b> as it states: “With healing in its wings.”
The Gemara comments: Abaye <b>disagrees with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish,</b> who <b>said: There is no Gehenna in the World-to-Come. Rather, the Holy One, Blessed be He,</b> will <b>remove the sun from its sheath [<i>minartika</i>],</b> and <b>the righteous will be healed by it and the wicked will be punished by it.</b> The righteous will be healed by it, <b>as it is stated: “But to you that fear My name shall the sun</b> of righteousness <b>arise</b> with healing in its wings”; <b>and moreover,</b> not only will they be healed by it <b>but they will</b> even <b>be rejuvenated by it, as it is stated: “And you shall go forth and leap as calves of the stall.”</b>
<b>And the wicked will be punished by</b> the same sun, <b>as it is stated: “For behold, the day is coming, it burns as a furnace;</b> and all the proud, and all that work wickedness, shall be stubble; and the day that comes shall set them ablaze” (Malachi 3:19). Consequently, Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish holds that the sun heals only in the World-to-Come, whereas Abaye interprets the verse as referring to this world.

Daf 9a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The mishna continues to explain the rules of intimations of vows. If an individual states that he accepts an obligation upon himself <b>like the vows of the wicked, he has vowed with regard to</b> becoming <b>a nazirite, or</b> bringing <b>an offering, or</b> taking <b>an oath.</b> This is considered a real formulation of a vow, just as the wicked customarily take vows. If he says: <b>Like the vows of the virtuous, he has not said anything,</b> because virtuous people do not generally take vows. If he says: <b>Like their gift offerings, he has vowed with regard to</b> becoming <b>a nazirite or</b> bringing <b>an offering.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks a question with regard to the first clause of the mishna. <b>And perhaps this is what he is saying: I am not making a vow like the vows of the wicked,</b> in which case he does not intend to take a vow. <b>Shmuel said:</b> It is referring to <b>one who said: Like the vows of the wicked I am hereby,</b> or: I accept <b>upon myself, or: From it.</b> If he says: <b>I am hereby,</b> he is referring to his acceptance <b>of naziriteship.</b> If he says: I accept <b>upon myself,</b> he is referring <b>to an offering.</b> If he says: <b>From it,</b> he means to restrict himself from a particular activity <b>through an oath.</b>
The Gemara challenges this explanation: If he says: <b>I am hereby,</b> does he necessarily intend to accept <b>naziriteship? Perhaps he is saying: I am hereby</b> accepting upon myself <b>to fast. Shmuel said:</b> This is not a case where he simply said: I am hereby like the vows of the wicked; rather, it is a case <b>where a nazirite was passing in front of him,</b> and the meaning of his statement is understood based on that context.
Shmuel had also stated that if he says: I accept <b>upon myself,</b> he is referring <b>to an offering,</b> and if he says: From it, he means to restrict himself by means of an oath. The Gemara asks: If he says: <b>From it,</b> does he necessarily mean to restrict himself <b>through an oath? Perhaps he is saying: I will eat from</b> this loaf, rather than: I will not eat from it. <b>Rava said:</b> The case is <b>where he said: I will not eat from it.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> he has explicitly clarified his intent, so <b>what</b> is the purpose <b>of stating</b> that this statement constitutes an oath? The Gemara answers: <b>Lest you say</b> that since <b>he did not utter</b> the term <b>oath from his mouth</b> the oath does not take effect, <b>this teaches us</b> that <b>this</b> is nevertheless considered a valid intimation of an oath.
§ It was taught in the mishna that if one states that he accepts an obligation upon himself <b>like the vows of the virtuous, he has not said anything.</b> However, if he says: <b>Like their gift offerings, he has vowed</b> with regard to becoming a nazirite and bringing an offering. The Gemara asks: <b>Who is the <i>tanna</i> according to whom</b> there is a <b>difference between a vow and a gift</b> offering? <b>Shall we say</b> that this is <b>not</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir and not</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> either?
This is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the verse <b>“Better that you should not vow,</b> than that you should vow and not pay” (Ecclesiastes 5:4), that <b>better than</b> both <b>this and that</b> is one <b>who does not take a vow at all.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. Rabbi Yehuda says: Better than</b> both <b>this and that is one who vows and pays.</b> Consequently, Rabbi Meir advocates abstaining from all vows and Rabbi Yehuda advocates making vows and fulfilling them, but neither of them distinguishes between vows and gift offerings. The mishna, however, indicates that virtuous people do not make vows but do bring gift offerings.
The Gemara answers: <b>You</b> can <b>even say</b> that it is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir.</b>

Daf 9b

<b>When Rabbi Meir said</b> that one should abstain from making vows, he was referring only <b>to a vow; he did not say it with regard to a gift</b> offering. The Gemara asks: <b>But it is taught</b> in the mishna that if one said: <b>Like the gift offerings</b> of the virtuous, <b>he has vowed with regard to</b> becoming <b>a nazirite or</b> bringing <b>an offering;</b> this indicates that the virtuous vow to become nazirites and bring offerings. The Gemara answers: <b>Teach</b> the mishna in the following emended formulation: <b>He has volunteered with regard to</b> becoming <b>a nazirite or</b> bringing <b>an offering.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is different about one who vows,</b> i.e., one who says: It is incumbent upon me to bring an offering, <b>which is not</b> proper to do due to the concern that <b>perhaps he will encounter a stumbling block</b> and not bring <b>it</b> promptly, thereby violating the prohibition against delaying? One should <b>also not</b> designate a particular animal as a <b>gift</b> offering due to the concern that <b>perhaps he will encounter a stumbling block with it.</b> Once the animal is consecrated, anyone who unwittingly benefits from it, e.g., by shearing it or working with it, transgresses the prohibition against misusing consecrated property.
The Gemara answers: In the case of a gift offering, he can act <b>like Hillel the Elder. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>They said about Hillel the Elder that no person misused his burnt-offering in his lifetime.</b> How did he ensure this? He was careful not to consecrate the animal in advance; rather, <b>he would bring it when it was unconsecrated to</b> the Temple <b>courtyard and</b> there he would <b>consecrate it, and</b> then immediately he would <b>place his hand on its</b> head <b>and slaughter it.</b> Consequently, there was no opportunity to misuse it.
The Gemara asks: <b>This works out well</b> with regard to voluntary <b>gifts</b> in the context <b>of offerings,</b> but with regard to the <b>voluntary</b> acceptance <b>of naziriteship, what is there to say?</b> There is still room for concern that he will not fulfill the obligations incumbent upon him as a nazirite. The Gemara answers: Rabbi Meir <b>holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Shimon HaTzaddik.</b>
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon HaTzaddik said: In all my days</b> as a priest, <b>I never ate the guilt-offering of a ritually impure nazirite except</b> for <b>one</b> occasion. <b>One time, a particular man who was a nazirite came from the South and I saw that he had beautiful eyes and was good looking, and the fringes of his hair were arranged in curls. I said to him: My son, what did you see</b> that made you decide <b>to destroy this beautiful hair of yours</b> by becoming a nazirite? A nazirite must shave off his hair at the completion of his term. If he becomes impure before the completion of his term, he shaves off his hair and starts his term of naziriteship again.
<b>He said to me: I was a shepherd for</b> my <b>father in my city,</b> and <b>I went to draw water from the spring, and I looked at my reflection [<i>babavua</i>]</b> in the water <b>and my</b> evil <b>inclination quickly overcame me and sought to expel me from the world. I said to</b> myself: <b>Wicked one! Why do you pride yourself in a world that is not yours?</b> Why are you proud <b>of someone who will eventually be</b> food in the grave <b>for worms and maggots,</b> i.e., your body? I swear <b>by the</b> Temple <b>service that I shall shave you for</b> the sake of <b>Heaven.</b>
Shimon HaTzaddik continues the narrative: <b>I immediately arose and kissed him on his head. I said to him: My son, may there be more who take vows of naziriteship like you among the Jewish people. About you the verse states: “When</b> either <b>a man</b> or a woman <b>shall clearly utter a vow, the vow of a nazirite, to consecrate himself to the Lord”</b> (Numbers 6:2). This is an example of voluntary acceptance of naziriteship, i.e., becoming a nazirite with entirely pure intentions rather than as a rash statement, e.g., while in a fit of anger.
<b>Rabbi Mani strongly objects to</b> the statement of Shimon HaTzaddik. <b>What is different about the guilt-offering of a ritually impure nazirite that</b> Shimon HaTzaddik <b>did not eat, because it came as a result of sin</b> when the individual violated the terms of his naziriteship by becoming impure? <b>Let him also not eat all</b> other <b>guilt-offerings, as they</b> too <b>come as a result of sin.</b>
