Rather,</b> it is apparent <b>that gourds are included in</b> the category of <b>vegetables,</b> although they differ from other vegetables, and therefore, the agent purchases gourds and explains that he found only gourds. <b>And legumes are not included in</b> the category of <b>vegetables,</b> and that is why the agent dispatched to purchase vegetables would not purchase legumes at all. <b>And</b> for one who vows that vegetables are forbidden to him, it <b>is prohibited to</b> eat <b>the fresh cowpea</b>, which is considered a vegetable, <b>and</b> it <b>is permitted to</b> eat <b>dry</b> cowpea, which is not a vegetable.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> We learned in the mishna: For <b>one who vows</b> that <b>vegetables</b> are forbidden <b>to</b> him, it is permitted to eat gourds, and Rabbi Akiva prohibits him from eating gourds. The Gemara questions Rabbi Akiva’s ruling: <b>But</b> how can his vow include gourds, which are fruits and not vegetables; <b>didn’t he vow</b> to refrain <b>from</b> eating <b>vegetables? Ulla said:</b> The mishna is referring <b>to one who said: Vegetables</b> cooked <b>in a pot</b> are forbidden <b>to me.</b> Gourds are included in the category of vegetables cooked in a pot. The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> if that is what he said, <b>perhaps he is saying: A vegetable that is eaten in a pot,</b> i.e., a vegetable that is added to flavor the food cooked in the pot, is forbidden to me? The Gemara answers: The mishna is referring <b>to one who said: A vegetable that is cooked in a pot</b> is forbidden <b>to me,</b> a statement that can include gourds.
The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to what</b> principle <b>do</b> Rabbi Akiva and the Rabbis <b>disagree?</b> The Gemara explains that <b>the Rabbis maintain: Any item with regard to which an agent must consult</b> the person who dispatched him before purchasing it, <b>is not</b> considered the same <b>type.</b> Since the agent must ask whether he can purchase gourds, apparently they are not a vegetable. <b>And Rabbi Akiva maintains: Any item with regard to which an agent</b> must <b>consult is</b> considered the same <b>type.</b> With regard to food of a different type, he does not consult. <b>Abaye said: Rabbi Akiva concedes with regard to lashes that</b> the one who vowed <b>is not flogged</b> if he ate gourds, as the issue of whether or not he violated his vow is not entirely clear.
<b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Me’ila</i> 20a): With regard to <b>an agent who performed his mission</b> properly, if he was tasked to use a particular item, and the one who dispatched him forgot that it was a consecrated item, <b>the employer,</b> who dispatched him, <b>misused</b> the consecrated item and is liable, as the agent acted on his behalf. However, if the agent <b>did not perform his mission</b> properly, and the <b>agent misused</b> the consecrated item, he is liable, as once the agent deviates from his mission, he ceases to be an agent and his actions are attributable to him.
The Gemara asks: <b>Who is</b> the <b><i>tanna</i></b> who <b>taught</b> this <i>halakha</i> in the mishna? <b>Rav Ḥisda said: The mishna</b> is <b>not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva as we learned</b> in the mishna: <b>How so?</b> If the employer <b>said to</b> the agent: <b>Give meat to the guests, and he gave them liver;</b> or if he said: <b>Give</b> them <b>liver, and he gave them meat, the agent has misused</b> the consecrated item, as he deviated from his mission. <b>And if</b> this were in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, didn’t Rabbi Akiva say: Any matter with regard to which an agent must consult is</b> considered the same <b>type?</b> Since based on that criterion, liver is certainly considered meat, <b>let the employer</b> be liable <b>for misuse</b> of consecrated property <b>and let the agent not</b> be liable <b>for misuse</b> of consecrated property, as he fulfilled his mission.
<b>Abaye said: Even</b> if <b>you say</b> that the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b>

Daf 54b

<b>doesn’t Rabbi Akiva concede that</b> the agent <b>must consult</b> his employer? Because he failed to do so and acted on his own, he is not considered to have performed his mission. This <i>halakha</i> <b>was stated before Rava. He said to</b> those who stated the <i>halakha</i> before him: <b>Naḥmani,</b> i.e., Abaye, <b>spoke well.</b>
§ The Gemara asks: <b>Who is the <i>tanna</i> who disagrees with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Akiva</b> in the mishna here? The Gemara answers: <b>It is Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel, as it is taught</b> in a dispute in the <i>baraita</i>: For <b>one who vows</b> that <b>meat</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it <b>is prohibited</b> to eat <b>all types of meat, and</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for him to eat meat <b>of the head, and of the feet, and of the windpipe, and of the liver, and of the heart,</b> although people do not typically eat meat from those parts of the body. <b>And</b> it is prohibited for him to eat meat <b>of birds,</b> as it too is popularly called meat. <b>However,</b> it <b>is permitted</b> for him to eat <b>of the meat of fish and grasshoppers,</b> as their flesh is not called meat.
<b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says: One who vows</b> that <b>meat</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, <b>is forbidden in all types of meat, and is permitted</b> to eat meat <b>of the head, and of the feet, and of the windpipe, and of the liver, and of the heart and of birds, and needless to say</b> he may also partake of <b>fish and grasshoppers. And Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel would likewise say: Innards are not</b> considered <b>meat, and one who eats them is not a person,</b> meaning that the innards are not fit for human consumption. The Gemara elaborates: With regard to <b>one who eats them,</b> in terms of the <i>halakhot</i> related to their consumption, e.g., vows, they are considered <b>as meat.</b> However, <b>with regard to purchase,</b> one who purchases them <b>is not a person.</b> In any case, apparently, Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel disagrees with Rabbi Akiva, as he maintains that although if an agent fails to find meat he is required to consult his employer before replacing it with liver, it is not considered meat with regard to vows.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is different</b> about <b>the meat of a bird according to the first <i>tanna</i>, that he prohibits it</b> since it is considered meat, due to the fact <b>that</b> when <b>the agent</b> fails to find meat, <b>he tends to consult</b> his employer <b>about it?</b> The same should be true of the <b>meat of fish too. If the agent does not find meat, he tends to consult</b> his employer <b>about it, as he says: If I do not find meat, should I bring fish? And</b> therefore, <b>let</b> fish also <b>be forbidden</b> according to the first <i>tanna</i>.
<b>Abaye said:</b> This is referring to a case <b>where he let</b> his <b>blood</b> when he vowed, <b>as</b> a person in that condition <b>does not eat fish.</b> It was common knowledge then that eating fish after bloodletting is harmful. The Gemara asks: <b>If so, he</b> would <b>not eat birds either, as Shmuel said:</b> With regard to one <b>who lets blood and eats the meat of a bird, his heart</b> rate accelerates and <b>flies like a bird.</b> Clearly, bird meat too is deleterious for his health. <b>And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One neither lets blood before</b> eating <b>fish, nor before</b> eating <b>birds, nor before</b> eating <b>salted meat. And it is taught</b> in another <i>baraita</i>: If one <b>let blood, he may eat neither milk, nor cheese, nor eggs, nor cress, nor birds, nor salted meat.</b> The Gemara answers: Meat of <b>birds is different, as</b> it <b>is possible</b> to eat it safely after bloodletting <b>by means of</b> thoroughly <b>boiling</b> it.
<b>Abaye said:</b> This is referring to a case <b>where his eyes hurt him, as fish are harmful for eyes.</b> Therefore, meat of birds is permitted, but not fish. The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> and he is suffering from eye pain, he should <b>eat fish, as Shmuel said</b> an acronym: <b><i>Nun</i>, <i>samekh</i>, <i>ayin</i>,</b> which stands for: <b><i>Nuna samma la’einayim</i>,</b> which means: Fish is a medicine for eyes. The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> statement of Shmuel is referring to the <b>latter</b> stages <b>of the</b> eye <b>infection.</b>

Daf 55a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> For <b>one who vows</b> that <b>grain [<i>dagan</i>]</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it <b>is prohibited to</b> eat <b>the dry cowpea,</b> because, like grain, its final stage of production involves being placed in a pile; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. And the Rabbis say:</b> It <b>is prohibited</b> for him to partake <b>of only the five species</b> of grain: Wheat, barley, oats, spelt, and rye, as that is the connotation of the term <i>dagan</i> in the Torah. <b>Rabbi Meir says:</b> For <b>one who vows</b> that grain is forbidden to him, and therefore he will refrain <b>from</b> eating <b>grain [<i>tevua</i>],</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for him to eat <b>from</b> only <b>the five species</b> of grain. <b>However,</b> for <b>one who vows</b> that grain is forbidden to him, and therefore he will refrain <b>from</b> eating <b>grain [<i>dagan</i>],</b> it <b>is prohibited to</b> eat <b>all</b> produce whose final stage of production involves being placed in a pile, e.g., dry cowpea, <b>and</b> it <b>is permitted</b> for him <b>to</b> eat <b>fruits of the tree and vegetables.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>Is this to say that</b> according to Rabbi Meir, the term <b><i>dagan</i> means any</b> produce <b>that is</b> harvested at one time and <b>placed in a pile [<i>midgan</i>]? Rav Yosef raised an objection:</b> After King Hezekiah called upon the people to give <i>teruma</i> and tithes properly, the verse states: <b>“And as soon as the matter was publicized, the children of Israel gave in abundance the first fruits of <i>dagan</i>, wine, and oil,</b> and honey, <b>and of all the <i>tevua</i> of the field;</b> and the tithe of all that they brought <b>in abundance”</b> (II Chronicles 31:5). <b>And if you say</b> that <b><i>dagan</i> means any</b> produce <b>that is placed in a pile, what is</b> the meaning of the words <b>“As soon as the matter was publicized,</b> the children of Israel <b>gave in abundance</b> the first fruits of <i>dagan</i>…and of all the <i>tevua</i> of the field”? There is no need to list both <i>dagan</i> and all <i>tevua</i> of the field. <b>Abaye said:</b> <i>Tevua</i> comes <b>to include fruits of the tree and vegetables,</b> which they tithed although they are not included in <i>dagan</i>, as they are not harvested at one time and placed in a pile.
§ We learned in the mishna that <b>Rabbi Meir says:</b> For <b>one who vows</b> that <b>grain</b> [<b><i>tevua</i>]</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it is prohibited for him to eat from only the five species of grain. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Everyone concedes with regard to one who vows</b> that <b><i>tevua</i></b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him <b>that</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for him to eat <b>from only the five species</b> of grain. The Rabbis do not disagree with Rabbi Meir in that regard. <b>That is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>And they agree with regard to one who vows</b> that <b><i>tevua</i></b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him <b>that</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for him to eat <b>from only the five species</b> of grain. The Gemara asks: <b>Isn’t</b> that <b>obvious,</b> as it is only those species that are called <i>tevua</i>. The Gemara answers: It is necessary; <b>lest you say</b> that <b><i>tevua</i> means all items</b> that grow from the ground, therefore, the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches us that</b> this expression <b>does not mean all items</b> that grow from the ground.
<b>Rav Yosef raised an objection:</b> With regard to the verse <b>“And as soon as the matter was publicized, the children of Israel</b> gave <b>in abundance…</b>and of all the <i>tevua</i> of the field,” the phrase “and of all the <i>tevua</i> of the field” comes to include all crops that grow in the field. <b>Rava said: <i>Tevua</i> is discrete</b> and refers to only the five species of grain, <b>and <i>tevua</i> of the field is discrete</b> and refers to all crops that grow in the field.
§ The Gemara relates: <b>The son of Master Shmuel commanded</b> his workers <b>that they give thirteen thousand dinars to Rava from the crop [<i>alalta</i>]</b> produced in his fields on the banks <b>of the Panya River. Rava sent</b> this question <b>before Rav Yosef: What is called <i>alalta</i>;</b> what crops are included in the category of <i>alalta</i>? <b>Rav Yosef said: It is</b> as it is taught in the <b><i>baraita</i></b> cited above: <b>And they agree with regard to one who vows</b> that <b><i>tevua</i></b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him <b>that</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for him to eat <b>from only the five species</b> of grain; just as <i>tevua</i> includes only the five species, so too <i>alalta</i> includes only the five species. <b>Abaye said to</b> him: <b>Are</b> the two cases <b>comparable?</b> Although <b><i>tevua</i> means</b> grain and includes <b>only the five species, <i>alalta</i> means</b> crop and includes <b>all items</b> that grow.
The messengers <b>returned</b> with the answer to his question and came <b>before Rava. He said: That was not a dilemma for me,</b> i.e., the fact <b>that <i>alalta</i> means all items</b> that grow. <b>This is</b> the matter <b>that is a dilemma for me: What is</b> the legal status of profits from the <b>rent of houses and the rent of boats? Do we say: Since they depreciate,</b> their legal status is <b>not</b> comparable to <b>that</b> of <b>a crop?</b> Only items that are consistently profitable are similar to crops. Houses and boats deteriorate with use, and their depreciation diminishes the profits. <b>Or perhaps, since their depreciation is not conspicuous,</b> their legal status <b>is</b> comparable to that of <b>a crop. The Rabbis stated</b> Rava’s reaction <b>before Rav Yosef.</b> Rav Yosef <b>said: And since he does not need us,</b> and he believes that he knows the answer himself, <b>why did he send us</b> the question? <b>Rav Yosef became angry</b> with Rava.
<b>Rava heard</b> that Rav Yosef was angry <b>and came before him on Yom Kippur eve</b> to appease him. <b>He found the attendant of Rav Yosef, who was diluting a cup of wine</b> with water <b>before him.</b> Rava <b>said to</b> the attendant: <b>Give me</b> the cup so <b>that I will dilute</b> the wine <b>for him.</b> The attendant <b>gave</b> it <b>to him and</b> Rava <b>diluted the cup of wine. While</b> Rav Yosef, who was blind, <b>was drinking</b> the wine, <b>he said: This dilution is similar to the dilution of Rava, son of Rav Yosef bar Ḥama,</b> who would dilute wine with more than the standard amount of water. Rava <b>said to him:</b> Correct, <b>it is he.</b>
Rav Yosef <b>said to</b> Rava: <b>Do not sit on your feet until you tell me the explanation of this matter: What is</b> the meaning of that <b>which is written: “And from the wilderness Mattana and from Mattana Nahaliel, and from Nahaliel Bamot”</b> (Numbers 21:18–19)?
Rava <b>said to him</b> that it means: <b>Once a person renders himself like a wilderness, deserted before all, the Torah is given to him as a gift [<i>mattana</i>], as it is stated: “And from the wilderness Mattana.” And once it is given to him as a gift, God bequeaths [<i>naḥalo</i>]</b> it to <b>him, as it is stated: “And from Mattana Nahaliel.” And once God bequeaths</b> it to <b>him, he rises to greatness, as it is stated: And from Nahaliel, Bamot,</b> which are elevated places. <b>And if he elevates himself</b> and is arrogant about his Torah, <b>the Holy One, Blessed be He, degrades him, as it is stated: “And from Bamot the valley”</b> (Numbers 21:20). <b>And not only</b> is he degraded, <b>but one lowers him into the ground, as it is stated: “And looking over [<i>nishkafa</i>] the face of the wasteland”</b> (Numbers 21:20), like a threshold [<i>iskopa</i>] that is sunken into the ground. <b>And if he reverses his</b> arrogance and becomes humble, <b>the Holy One, Blessed be He, elevates him,</b>

Daf 55b

<b>as it is stated: “Every valley shall be lifted”</b> (Isaiah 40:4). When Rav Yosef heard that interpretation, he understood that Rava was aware of the error of his ways in acting arrogantly toward his teacher, and was pacified by Rava’s display of humility.
§ <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: For <b>one who vows</b> that <b>grain [<i>dagan</i>]</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it <b>is prohibited to</b> partake <b>of the dry cowpea, and</b> it <b>is permitted</b> for him to partake <b>of fresh</b> cowpea. <b>And</b> it <b>is permitted</b> for him to partake <b>of rice,</b> as well as <b>of wheat kernels split into two parts [<i>ḥilka</i>], of wheat kernels crushed into three parts [<i>targeis</i>], and wheat kernels crushed into four parts [<i>tisnei</i>].</b> For <b>one who vows</b> that <b>produce of the year</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it <b>is prohibited to</b> partake <b>of all produce of the year</b> that grew from the ground or on trees, <b>and</b> it <b>is permitted</b> for him to partake <b>of goats, and of lambs, and of milk, and of eggs, and of chicks</b> born that year, as they are not included in the category of produce. <b>And if he said: Growths of the year are</b> forbidden <b>to me,</b> it <b>is prohibited for him</b> to eat <b>all of them.</b>
For <b>one who vows</b> that <b>produce of the land</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it <b>is prohibited</b> for him <b>to</b> partake <b>of all produce</b> that grows from <b>the land, and</b> it <b>is permitted</b> for him to partake <b>of truffles and mushrooms</b> that are not in the category of produce of the land. <b>But if he said: The growths of the ground are forbidden to me,</b> it <b>is prohibited for him</b> to eat <b>all of them.</b>
<b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from a mishna (<i>Berakhot</i> 40b): And <b>over</b> a food <b>item whose growth is not from the ground, one recites: By Whose word all things came to be. And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Over salt and over brine</b> [<b><i>zamit</i></b>], <b>and over truffles and mushrooms, one recites: By Whose word all things came to be.</b> Apparently, truffles and mushrooms are not in the category of growths of the ground. <b>Abaye said: They grow from the earth,</b> but with regard <b>to sustenance, they draw sustenance from the air and not from the earth.</b> The Gemara asks: Why is that distinction significant? <b>Isn’t it taught: Over</b> a food <b>item whose growth is not from the ground</b> one recites the blessing: By whose word all things came to be? Even according to Abaye, mushrooms grow from the ground. The Gemara answers: <b>Emend</b> the mishna to read: <b>Over</b> a food <b>item that does not draw sustenance from the ground,</b> one recites: By Whose word all things came to be.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> For <b>one who vows</b> that a <b>garment</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it <b>is permitted to</b> wear <b>sackcloth, and to</b> wear <b>a sheet, and to</b> wear <b>a coarse curtain [<i>ḥamila</i>],</b> as these are not in the category of garments. For one who <b>said: Wool is <i>konam</i></b> for me and I will therefore not <b>place it upon myself,</b> it <b>is permitted</b> for him <b>to cover himself with wool fleece,</b> which is not considered a garment, and it is prohibited for him to wear only a woolen fabric. For one who said: <b>Flax is <i>konam</i></b> for me and I will therefore not <b>place it upon myself,</b> it <b>is permitted</b> for him <b>to cover himself with</b> uncombed <b>flax in bundles,</b> and it is prohibited for him to wear only a flaxen fabric.
<b>Rabbi Yehuda says: Everything is</b> determined <b>according to the one who vows.</b> If <b>one was bearing</b> a burden of wool and linen, <b>and was sweating, and its smell was unpleasant</b> for him, and in reaction, <b>he said: Wool and linen are <i>konam</i></b> for me and I will therefore not <b>place them upon myself,</b> it <b>is permitted</b> for him <b>to cover himself</b> with wool and linen garments, <b>but</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for him <b>to sling</b> them over his shoulder <b>behind him</b> as a burden. The circumstances of his vow make it clear that he intends to forswear carrying wool and linen as a burden rather than the wearing of them as a garment.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: For <b>one who vows</b> that a <b>garment</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it <b>is permitted to</b> wear <b>sackcloth, and to</b> wear <b>a sheet and to</b> wear <b>a coarse curtain, and</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for him <b>to</b> wear <b>a money belt [<i>punda</i>], or to</b> wear <b>a sash [<i>pesakiyya</i>], or to</b> wear <b>a <i>sekurtiyya</i>, or to</b> wear <b>a leather spread [<i>katavliyya</i>], or to</b> wear <b>a leather sock [<i>anpilya</i>] or to</b> wear <b>a leather apron [<i>pelinya</i>], and trousers, and a hat.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the meaning of <b><i>iskurtei</i>,</b> mentioned in the <i>baraita</i> as <i>sekurtiyya</i>? <b>Rabba bar Ḥana said:</b> It means <b>a tanner’s apron.</b>
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may go out</b> into the public domain on Shabbat covered <b>in thick sackcloth or in a coarse</b> woolen <b>blanket [<i>sagos</i>], or in a sheet or in a coarse curtain</b> as protection <b>from the rain.</b> They are considered garments, not burdens. <b>However,</b> he may <b>neither</b> go out covered <b>in a box, nor in a basket, nor in a mat</b> as protection <b>from the rain,</b> as they are considered burdens, not garments. <b>Shepherds may go out</b> on Shabbat covered <b>in sackcloth,</b> as they typically go out in sackcloth garments. <b>And</b> the Sages <b>did not say</b> this <b>only</b> with regard to <b>shepherds; rather,</b> they said that <b>all people</b> may go out wearing sackcloth; <b>however, the Sages spoke in the present,</b> addressing situations that were prevalent.
§ We learned in the mishna that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: Everything is</b> determined <b>according to the one who vows. It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>How,</b> i.e., in what circumstances, did <b>Rabbi Yehuda say: Everything is according to the one who vows?</b> If <b>one was wearing</b> a <b>woolen</b> garment <b>and it caused him discomfort, and</b> in reaction <b>he said: Wool is <i>konam</i></b> for me, and I will therefore not <b>place it upon myself,</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for him <b>to wear</b> woolen garments, <b>but</b> it <b>is permitted to place</b> a burden of woolen garments upon him. If <b>one was burdened with flax and was sweating, and said: Flax is <i>konam</i></b> for me, and I will therefore not <b>place it upon myself,</b> it <b>is permitted</b> for him <b>to wear</b> flaxen garments <b>and</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for him <b>to place</b> a burden of flaxen garments upon him.

Daf 56a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> For <b>one who vows</b> that <b>a house</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, entry <b>is permitted</b> for him <b>in the upper story</b> of the house; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. And the Rabbis say: An upper story is included</b> in <b>the house,</b> and therefore, entry is prohibited there as well. However, for <b>one who vows</b> that <b>an upper story</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, entry <b>is permitted in the house,</b> as the ground floor is not included in the upper story.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught</b> with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of leprosy that in the verse “it appears to me as it were a plague in the house” (Leviticus 14:35), the term <b>“in the house”</b> comes <b>to include the gallery,</b> a half story above the ground floor, and <b>“in the house”</b> comes <b>to include the upper story? Rav Ḥisda said:</b> The <i>tanna</i> is <b>Rabbi Meir, as, if</b> the <i>tanna</i> were <b>the Rabbis, didn’t the Rabbis say</b> that <b>a second story is included in the house? Why</b> then <b>do I</b> need the <b>verse</b> containing the phrase <b>“in the house” to include</b> the second story?
<b>Abaye said: Even if you would say</b> that the <i>tanna</i> is <b>the Rabbis,</b> they too <b>require a verse</b> to include the second story in this case, <b>as it might enter your mind to say</b> that since <b>it is written: “In a house of the land of your possession”</b> (Leviticus 14:34), only that <b>which is attached to the ground</b> has the <b>status of a house</b> but with regard to <b>a second story, that is not attached to the ground.</b> Even according to the Rabbis, the verse is necessary to prevent the conclusion that the legal status of a second story is not that of a house with regard to leprosy.
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>is that which Rav Huna bar Ḥiyya said in the name of Ulla?</b> If the seller says to the buyer: <b>A house in my house I am selling to you, he may show</b> the buyer that he purchased the <b>second story [<i>aliyya</i>].</b> The Gemara infers: <b>The reason is that</b> the seller <b>said to him: A house in my house I am selling to you. However, if</b> he sold him <b>a house, unspecified, he may not show him a second story. Let us say</b> that <b>this</b> is the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir,</b> who states that the second story is not included in the house. The Gemara rejects this claim: <b>Even if you would say</b> that it is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, what is</b> the meaning of the term <b><i>aliyya</i></b> in this context? It does not mean second story; it means <b>the most outstanding of the houses.</b> Rav Huna bar Ḥiyya said in the name of Ulla that when one says a house in my house, he must show him the most outstanding part of his house. However, if he sold him a house without specification, he may show him a second story.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> For <b>one who vows</b> that <b>a bed</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it <b>is permitted</b> to lie <b>in a <i>dargash</i>,</b> which is not commonly called a bed; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. And the Rabbis say: A <i>dargash</i> is included</b> in the category of <b>a bed.</b> Everyone agrees that for <b>one who vows</b> that <b>a <i>dargash</i></b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it <b>is permitted</b> to lie <b>in a bed.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>What is a <i>dargash</i>? Ulla said:</b> It is <b>a bed of</b> good <b>fortune,</b> placed in the house as a fortuitous omen, and not designated for sleeping. <b>The Rabbis said to Ulla: That which we learned</b> in a mishna: <b>When</b> the people <b>serve</b> the king the <b>meal of comfort</b> after he buries a relative, <b>all the people recline on the ground and</b> the king <b>reclines on a <i>dargash</i></b> during the meal. According to your explanation, during <b>the entire year he does not sit on</b> the bed; on <b>that day</b> of the funeral <b>he sits on it? Ravina objects to</b> the question of the Rabbis: This anomaly is <b>just as it is</b> with regard <b>to meat and wine, as</b> throughout <b>the entire year if he wishes he eats</b> them, <b>and if he wishes he does not eat</b> them; on <b>that day</b> of the funeral, <b>we give him</b> meat and wine in the meal of comfort.
<b>Rather, this is difficult, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the custom of overturning the beds in the house of a mourner: With regard to <b>a <i>dargash</i></b> in his house, the mourner <b>would not overturn it, but</b> he merely <b>stands it</b> on its side. <b>And if you say</b> that a <i>dargash</i> <b>is a bed of fortune, isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: A mourner <b>who</b> is required to <b>overturn his bed</b> is required to <b>overturn not only his own bed, but</b> to <b>overturn all of the beds that he has inside his house,</b> even those not used for sleeping. Why, then, is he not required to overturn the <i>dargash</i>? The Gemara rejects this contention: <b>This</b> is <b>not difficult;</b>

Daf 56b

this is <b>just as it is</b> with regard <b>to</b> the case <b>of a bed designated</b> exclusively <b>for vessels, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>If</b> the bed in a mourner’s house <b>was a bed designated for vessels</b> and not for sleeping, <b>one need not overturn it.</b> The same is true with regard to the bed of fortune. Since it is not for sleeping, one need not overturn it.
<b>Rather, if</b> defining a <i>dargash</i> as a bed of fortune <b>is difficult, this is difficult, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says:</b> A mourner need not overturn <b>a <i>dargash</i>;</b> rather, he <b>loosens</b> the <b>loops</b> that connect the straps that support the bedding to the bedframe, <b>and it collapses on its own. And if a <i>dargash</i> is a bed of fortune, does it have loops [<i>karvitin</i>]? When Ravin came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia, <b>he said: I asked one of the Sages</b> about the meaning of <i>dargash</i>, <b>and Rav Taḥalifa, from the West, was his name, who frequented the tanners’ market. And he said to me: What is a <i>dargash</i>?</b> It is <b>a leather bed.</b>
<b>It was stated: Which is a bed and which is a <i>dargash</i>? Rabbi Yirmeya said:</b> In <b>a bed, one fastens</b> the supporting <b>straps over</b> the bedframe; in <b>a <i>dargash</i>, one fastens the straps through</b> holes in the bedframe <b>itself.</b> The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a mishna in tractate <i>Kelim</i> (16:1): With regard to <b>wooden vessels, from when</b> are they considered finished vessels and <b>susceptible to ritual impurity? A bed and a crib</b> are susceptible <b>from when he smooths them with the skin of a fish. And</b> the objection is: <b>If</b> in <b>a bed the straps are fastened over</b> the bedframe, <b>why do I</b> need <b>smoothing with the skin of a fish?</b> The wood of the bedframe is obscured from view.
<b>Rather,</b> with regard to both <b>this,</b> a bed, <b>and that,</b> a <i>dargash</i>, one fastens the straps <b>through</b> holes in the bedframes <b>themselves,</b> and the difference between them is: In <b>a bed,</b> the straps <b>are inserted and extracted through holes</b> in the bedframe; in <b>a <i>dargash</i>,</b> the straps <b>are inserted and extracted through loops</b> attached to the bedframe, as Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel said that one loosens the loops and the bedding falls on its own.
<b>Rabbi Ya’akov bar Aḥa said</b> that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said:</b> With regard to <b>a bed whose two posts [<i>nakliteha</i>] protrude,</b> rendering its overturning impossible, <b>he stands it</b> on its side, <b>and</b> that <b>is sufficient for him. Rabbi Ya’akov bar Idi said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel</b> with regard to the overturning of a <i>dargash</i>.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> For <b>one who vows</b> that <b>the city</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it <b>is permitted to enter</b> the Shabbat <b>boundary of</b> that <b>city,</b> the two-thousand-cubit area surrounding the city, <b>and</b> it <b>is prohibited to enter its outskirts,</b> the seventy-cubit area adjacent to the city. <b>However,</b> for <b>one who vows</b> that <b>a house</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it <b>is prohibited</b> to enter only <b>from the doorstop and inward.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive <b>that the</b> legal status of <b>the outskirts of a city are like that of the city</b> itself? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said</b> that it is <b>as the verse states: “And it came to pass when Joshua was in Jericho,</b> that he lifted up his eyes and looked” (Joshua 5:13). <b>What</b> is the meaning of <b>“in Jericho”? If we say</b> that it means <b>in Jericho proper, isn’t it written: “And Jericho was completely shut”</b> (Joshua 6:1)? <b>Rather, learn from here</b> that Joshua was <b>in the outskirts</b> of the city. And although he was in the outskirts, the verse states that he was in Jericho.
The Gemara asks: <b>Say</b> that the legal status of one located <b>even in</b> the Shabbat <b>boundary</b> of a city is like that of one inside the town itself, and perhaps although Joshua was merely within the Shabbat boundary, the verse characterizes him as being in Jericho. The Gemara rejects this: <b>Isn’t it written with regard to the boundary</b> of a city: <b>“And you shall measure outside the city…</b>two thousand cubits” (Numbers 35:5)? This indicates that the boundary of a city is considered outside the town and not part of the city itself.
§ We learned in the mishna: For <b>one who vows</b> that <b>a house</b> is forbidden <b>to</b> him, it <b>is prohibited</b> to enter <b>only from the doorstop and inward.</b> The Gemara infers: <b>However, from the doorstop outward, no,</b> it is permitted to enter. <b>Rav Mari raised an objection</b> based on a verse written with regard to leprosy: <b>“And the priest shall go out from the house</b> to the entrance of the house, and he shall quarantine the house” (Leviticus 14:38). And the question was raised in the halakhic midrash: One <b>might</b> have thought that the priest <b>may go to his house and quarantine</b> the leprous house that he examined from there. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “To the entrance of the house”</b> (Leviticus 14:38). <b>If</b> he may go only <b>to the entrance of the house,</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>he may stand beneath the lintel and quarantine</b> the house from there. Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “And the priest shall go out <b>from the house,”</b> indicating that he may not quarantine the house <b>until he goes out from the entire house.</b>
<b>How so?</b> <i>Ab initio</i>, the priest <b>stands</b> outside, <b>alongside the door jamb, and quarantines</b> the house. <b>And from where</b> is it derived <b>that if he went to his house and quarantined</b> the house, <b>or stood beneath the lintel and quarantined</b> the house, <b>that his quarantine is</b> an effective <b>quarantine</b> after the fact? <b>The verse states: “And he shall quarantine the house”</b> (Leviticus 14:38), which means <b>in any case.</b> Apparently, the legal status of the area beneath the lintel is identical to the status inside the house, even if it is beyond the doorstop. The Gemara answers: It <b>is different with regard to</b> a leprous <b>house, as it is written:</b> “And the priest shall go out <b>from the house,”</b> indicating that he cannot quarantine the house <b>until he goes out from the entire house.</b>

Daf 57a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> For one who says: <b>This produce is <i>konam</i> upon me,</b> or <b>it is <i>konam</i> upon my mouth,</b> or <b>it is <i>konam</i> to my mouth,</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> to partake of the produce, or of <b>its replacements, or</b> of anything that <b>grows from it.</b> If he says: This produce is <i>konam</i> for me, and for <b>that</b> reason <b>I</b> will not <b>eat</b> it, <b>or</b> for <b>that</b> reason <b>I</b> will not <b>taste</b> it, it <b>is permitted</b> for him to partake of <b>its replacements or</b> of anything that <b>grows from it.</b> This applies only <b>with regard to an item whose seeds cease</b> after it is sown. <b>However, with regard to an item whose seeds do not cease</b> after it is sown, e.g., bulbs, which flower and enter into a foliage period and repeat the process, it <b>is prohibited</b> for him to partake even of the <b>growths of</b> its <b>growths,</b> as the original, prohibited item remains intact.
For <b>one who says to his wife: Your handicraft is <i>konam</i> upon me,</b> or <b>it is <i>konam</i> upon my mouth,</b> or <b>it is <i>konam</i> to my mouth,</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> to benefit from her handicraft, and from <b>their replacements and</b> anything that <b>grows from them.</b> However, if he said to his wife: Your handicraft is <i>konam</i> for me only in the sense <b>that I</b> will not <b>eat</b> from your handicraft, <b>or that I</b> will not <b>taste</b> from your handicraft, it <b>is permitted</b> for him to benefit from <b>their replacements and</b> anything that <b>grows from them.</b> This applies only <b>with regard to an item whose seeds cease</b> after it is sown. <b>However, with regard to an item whose seeds do not cease</b> after it is sown, it <b>is prohibited</b> for him to benefit even from the <b>growths of</b> their <b>growths.</b>
If the husband said: From that <b>which you prepare, I will not eat until Passover,</b> or, with that <b>which you prepare, I will not cover myself until Passover,</b> then, if she <b>prepared</b> it <b>before Passover,</b> it <b>is permitted</b> for him <b>to eat or to cover himself</b> with them <b>after Passover.</b> If, however, he said: From that <b>which you prepare until Passover, I will not eat, or</b> from that <b>which you prepare until Passover, I will not cover myself,</b> then, if she <b>prepared</b> it <b>before Passover,</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for him <b>to eat or cover himself</b> with it <b>after Passover.</b> If he said to her: <b>Benefit from me until Passover if you go to your father’s house</b> from now <b>until the festival</b> of <i>Sukkot</i> is forbidden for you, and <b>she went</b> to his house <b>before Passover,</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for her to derive <b>benefit</b> from <b>him until Passover.</b>

Daf 57b

If she derived benefit from him before Passover and went to visit her father <b>after Passover,</b> she is liable <b>for</b> violating the prohibition of: <b>He shall not profane</b> his word (Numbers 30:3), as the condition was fulfilled and she violated the vow retroactively. If the husband vowed: <b>Benefit from me</b> is <i>konam</i> <b>for you until the Festival if you go to your father’s house</b> from now <b>until Passover,</b> then if <b>she went</b> to his house <b>before Passover,</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for her to <b>derive benefit</b> from <b>him until the Festival, and</b> it <b>is permitted</b> for her <b>to go</b> to her father’s house <b>after Passover,</b> as that time period is not included in his stipulation.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> We learned in the mishna: For <b>one who says to his wife: Your handicraft is <i>konam</i> upon me,</b> or <b>it is <i>konam</i> upon my mouth,</b> or <b>it is <i>konam</i> to my mouth,</b> it is prohibited to benefit from her handicraft. <b>Yishmael, a man of Kefar Yamma, and some say, a man of Kefar Dima, raised</b> a dilemma with regard to <b>an onion that one uprooted during</b> the <b>Sabbatical Year,</b> which was therefore sanctified with the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year, <b>and</b> he then <b>planted it during the eighth</b> year, <b>and its growths</b> that developed in the eighth year <b>exceeded its principal</b> original Sabbatical-Year onion. <b>And this is the dilemma that he raised: Its</b> eighth-year <b>growth is permitted, and its</b> Sabbatical-Year <b>principal</b> is <b>prohibited. Since its growth exceeded its principal,</b> do <b>those permitted growths neutralize the prohibition</b> of the onion, <b>or</b> do they <b>not?</b> Yishmael <b>came</b> and raised the dilemma <b>before Rabbi Ami, and he did not have</b> an answer readily <b>available.</b>
Yishmael <b>came</b> and raised the dilemma <b>before Rabbi Yitzḥak Nappaḥa,</b> who <b>resolved</b> it <b>for him from</b> that <b>which Rabbi Ḥanina Terita’a said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yannai said:</b> With regard to <b>an onion of <i>teruma</i> that one planted,</b> if <b>its growths exceeded its principal,</b> it <b>is permitted.</b> Here too, the eighth-year growth should neutralize the prohibition of the Sabbatical-Year onion. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said, and some say</b> it was <b>Rabbi Zerika</b> who said to Rabbi Yitzḥak Nappaḥa: <b>Did the Master abandon</b> the opinion of <b>two</b> Sages <b>and conduct</b> himself <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>one</b> Sage?
The Gemara asks: <b>Who are they,</b> the <b>two</b> Sages who disagree with his opinion? The Gemara answers: It is <b>as Rabbi Abbahu said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> With regard to <b>a young</b> vine within three years of its planting, whose fruits are <i>orla</i> and forbidden, <b>that one grafted onto an old,</b> permitted vine, <b>and there were fruits on</b> the younger vine, <b>even though</b> the younger vine <b>added two hundred</b> times the number of fruits that were there when it was grafted, and those additional fruits are permitted because they draw their nourishment from the older vine, the fruit that was on the younger vine before it was grafted is <b>forbidden.</b> Although, in principle, when the permitted part of the mixture is two hundred times the forbidden <i>orla</i>, the prohibition is neutralized, in this case, the prohibition is not neutralized, as the forbidden fruit was there from the outset.
<b>And Rabbi Shmuel bar Rabbi Naḥmani said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yonatan said:</b> With regard to <b>an onion that one planted in a vineyard,</b> creating a forbidden mixture of food crops in a vineyard, <b>and</b> then <b>the vineyard was uprooted,</b> and most of the onion grew in a permitted manner, it is <b>forbidden.</b> Apparently, both Rabbi Yoḥanan and Rabbi Yonatan disagree with the opinion of Rabbi Yannai, and therefore, there is no clear resolution to the dilemma.
Yishmael <b>then came</b> and raised the dilemma <b>before Rabbi Ami, who resolved</b> it <b>for him from</b> that <b>which Rabbi Yitzḥak said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> With regard to <b>a <i>litra</i> of onions that one tithed, and</b> then he <b>sowed</b> a field with the entire <i>litra</i> of onions, when the field yields the crop, <b>it is tithed according to the entire</b> crop. Although some of the onions that he sowed were already tithed, he is obligated to tithe them because the volume of the growths exceeds the volume of the original onions and the entire crop has untithed status. <b>Apparently, those growths neutralize the</b> prohibition of the <b>primary,</b> original, tithed onions.
The Gemara rejects that resolution: There is no proof from the ruling in the case of the <i>litra</i> of onions, as <b>perhaps</b> it <b>is different</b> when the ruling <b>is a stringency.</b> Perhaps, due to the concern that the growths neutralize the prohibition of the original, the ruling is that he must tithe the entire crop. However, there is no proof that the same would be true in cases where the ruling is a leniency, e.g., to neutralize the prohibition of the Sabbatical Year or <i>teruma</i>.
<b>Rather,</b> proof may be cited from <b>this</b> source; <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon says:</b>

Daf 58a

For <b>any item that can become permitted,</b> i.e., a forbidden object whose prohibition can or will lapse, <b>for example, untithed produce</b> that can be permitted through tithing, <b>and second tithe</b> that is permitted through redemption or bringing it to Jerusalem (Deuteronomy 14:24–26), <b>and consecrated items</b> that are also permitted through redemption, <b>and</b> produce of the <b>new</b> crop that is permitted after the sacrifice of the <i>omer</i> offering (Leviticus 23:14), <b>the Sages did not determine a measure for their</b> neutralization, and no mixture with any quantity of permitted items neutralizes their prohibition. <b>And</b> for <b>any item that cannot become permitted, for example, <i>teruma</i>, and <i>teruma</i> of the tithe, and <i>ḥalla</i></b> (Numbers 15:20–21); <b>fruit of a tree during the first three years after its planting [<i>orla</i>]; and forbidden food crops in a vineyard</b> (Deuteronomy 22:9), <b>the Sages determined a measure for their</b> neutralization.
The Rabbis <b>said to</b> Rabbi Shimon: <b>But isn’t Sabbatical-Year</b> produce <b>an item that cannot become permitted, and</b> nevertheless, <b>the Sages did not determine a measure for its</b> neutralization, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shevi’it</i> 7:7): <b>The Sabbatical-Year</b> produce <b>prohibits</b> permitted produce <b>of its</b> own <b>species</b> with which it is mixed <b>in any amount.</b> Rabbi Shimon <b>said to them: I too said</b> that Sabbatical-Year produce prohibits permitted produce in a mixture and permitted growths that develop from it <b>only</b> with regard <b>to</b> the <b>removal</b> of the produce. Sabbatical-Year produce may be eaten only as long as produce of that species remains in the field, after which it must be removed from one’s possession. Since it is permitted to eat the produce before that time, its legal status during this period is that of an item that can become permitted.
<b>However,</b> with regard <b>to</b> the permissibility of <b>eating</b> Sabbatical-Year produce after the time of removal has passed, when eating that produce is prohibited, the Sages determined a measure for their neutralization. The mixture is forbidden only if the measure of that produce is enough <b>to impart flavor</b> to the mixture. Apparently, permitted growths can neutralize the prohibition of the original item. The Gemara rejects the proof: <b>And perhaps here too,</b> it <b>is different</b> when the ruling <b>is a stringency.</b> In this case, the stringency is that the original item is sacred with the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year. However, here too, there is no proof that the same would be true in cases where the result is a leniency.
<b>Rather,</b> Yishmael of Kefar Yamma <b>resolved</b> his dilemma <b>from this</b> source, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shevi’it</i> 6:3): With regard to sixth-year <b>onions upon which rain fell</b> during the Sabbatical Year, <b>and they sprouted, if their leaves were black [<i>sheḥorin</i>],</b> i.e., dark green, an indication of fresh, recent growth, the onions are <b>forbidden</b> as Sabbatical-Year growth. If their leaves <b>turned green [<i>horiku</i>],</b> i.e., lighter and yellower, and appeared withered, the onions are <b>permitted,</b> as they are considered a product of the sixth year.
<b>Rabbi Ḥanina ben Antigonus says:</b> There is a different indicator; <b>if</b> the plants <b>can be uprooted by their leaves,</b> clearly the leaves are fresh and recent, and they are <b>forbidden. And</b> in the <b>parallel</b> situation, if that indicator was discovered in a Sabbatical-Year onion that sprouted <b>at the conclusion of the Sabbatical Year,</b> i.e., during the eighth year, the onions are <b>permitted.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Is this to say that</b> one may conclude from here that <b>permitted growth neutralizes the prohibition</b> of the original plant? The Gemara rejects this conclusion: <b>And perhaps</b> the <i>halakha</i> is <b>with regard to crushed [<i>medukhanin</i>],</b> pounded onions, and the reason that the prohibition of the original plant is neutralized is not that the permitted growth neutralizes the prohibition, but that it is no longer fit for consumption.
<b>Rather,</b> the dilemma can be resolved <b>from this</b> source; <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>:

Daf 58b

<b>One who weeds <i>ḥasayot</i> with a Samaritan may eat a casual meal from them</b> without tithing, as any untithed produce may be eaten in the framework of a casual meal. <b>And</b> when he completes the labor on the <i>ḥasayot</i>, places them into a pile, and they require tithing, <b>he tithes them</b> as produce that is <b>definitely</b> obligated in tithing, not as doubtfully tithed produce, as the assumption is that the Samaritan did not tithe the <i>ḥasayot</i>.
<b>Rabbi Shimon ben Elazar says: If</b> the <i>ḥasayot</i> belong to <b>a Jew</b> who is <b>suspect</b> about observance of <b>the Sabbatical Year, at the conclusion of the Sabbatical Year</b> it <b>is permitted</b> to weed with him, as there is no concern that there might be Sabbatical-Year sanctity. The Gemara asks: <b>Is this to say</b> that <b>the permitted growths</b> of the eighth year <b>neutralize the prohibition,</b> and that is why there is no concern about Sabbatical-Year sanctity? The Gemara rejects that inference: <b>And perhaps</b> the <i>tanna</i> is speaking <b>with regard to an item whose seeds cease</b> after it is sown, whose growths neutralize the original prohibition? The Gemara rejects that possibility: <b>Isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>These are <i>ḥasayot</i>, for example, arum, garlic, and onions,</b> whose seeds do not cease?
The Gemara asks: <b>And perhaps</b> the <i>tanna</i> is speaking <b>with regard to</b> plants that were <b>crushed</b> before they sprouted growths? The Gemara answers: It is the case of one who is <b>suspect about</b> observance of <b>the Sabbatical Year,</b> that <b>is taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i>, and one who is suspect would not bother to eliminate the prohibition by crushing it. The Gemara asks: <b>And perhaps</b> the <i>tanna</i> is speaking <b>with regard to a mixture</b> of forbidden <i>ḥasayot</i> and permitted ones, and the reason that it is permitted is that the prohibition was neutralized by the majority of permitted <i>ḥasayot</i>? The Gemara answers: <b>One who weeds, is taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i>, indicating that he is eating the leaves as he weeds them, leaving no opportunity for the <i>ḥasayot</i> to be mixed with others.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say that this is a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinions <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan and Rabbi Yonatan,</b> who stated regarding <i>orla</i> and food crops in a vineyard that their permitted growth does not neutralize the prohibition of the original fruit or food crops respectively. <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak said:</b> The <b>Sabbatical-Year</b> produce <b>is different. Since its prohibition</b> is engendered <b>by means of the ground, its nullification is</b> effected <b>by means of the ground as well.</b> The prohibition can be neutralized by means of the growth that results from replanting the forbidden plant in a permitted manner.
The Gemara asks: Isn’t there the case of <b>tithe, whose prohibition is</b> engendered <b>by means of the ground, but its nullification is not</b> effected <b>by means of the ground? As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>a <i>litra</i> of untithed</b> first <b>tithe</b> from which the <i>teruma</i> of the tithe was not taken, <b>which one sowed in the ground, and it grew and it is</b> now <b>approximately ten <i>litra</i>,</b> that additional growth <b>is obligated</b> to have <b>tithe</b> taken <b>and</b> is subject to the <i>halakhot</i> <b>of Sabbatical-Year</b> produce. <b>And</b> with regard to <b>that</b> original <b><i>litra</i></b> of untithed first tithe that he sowed, one <b>tithes for it from</b> produce in <b>a different place,</b> and not from the <i>litra</i> itself, <b>based on a calculation</b> of how much <i>teruma</i> of the tithe needed to be taken from that litra. Apparently, the growth that results from sowing the first tithe in the ground does not neutralize its prohibition.

Daf 59a

The Sages of the Gemara <b>say:</b> With regard to <b>tithe,</b> the ground does not engender the obligation; <b>placement</b> of the produce <b>in a pile engenders</b> the obligation, as it is only at that point that one is obligated to tithe his produce. Therefore neutralization of the prohibition is not effected by planting it in the ground.
<b>Rami bar Ḥama raised an objection</b> to the opinion of Rabbi Yannai based on the mishna (57a): For one who says: <b>This produce is <i>konam</i> upon me,</b> or <b>it is <i>konam</i> upon my mouth,</b> or <b>it is <i>konam</i> to my mouth,</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> to partake of the produce, or <b>of its replacements, or</b> of anything that <b>grows from it.</b> If he says: This produce is <i>konam</i> for me, and for <b>that</b> reason <b>I</b> will not <b>eat</b> it, <b>or</b> for <b>that</b> reason <b>I</b> will not <b>taste</b> it, it <b>is permitted</b> for him to partake <b>of its replacements or</b> of anything that <b>grows from it.</b> This applies only <b>with regard to an item whose seeds cease</b> after it is sown. <b>However, with regard to an item whose seeds do not cease</b> after it is sown, it <b>is prohibited</b> for him to partake even of the <b>growths of</b> its <b>growths.</b> Apparently, permitted growths do not neutralize the prohibition.
<b>Rabbi Abba said: <i>Konamot</i> are different; since if he wishes</b> to do so he can <b>request</b> that a halakhic authority dissolve the vows and render the objects of the vows permitted, <b>their</b> legal status <b>is like</b> that of <b>an item that can become permitted, and</b> its prohibition <b>is not nullified by a majority</b> of permitted items.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> isn’t there the case of <b><i>teruma</i>,</b> in <b>which if</b> he <b>wishes</b> he can <b>request</b> that a halakhic authority dissolve the designation of the produce as <i>teruma</i> <b>and</b> yet it is <b>nullified by a majority</b> of permitted items? <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Terumot</i> 5:1): <b>A <i>se’a</i> of ritually impure <i>teruma</i> that fell into less than one hundred</b> <i>se’a</i> of <b>non-sacred produce</b> must be left <b>to decay.</b> The impure <i>teruma</i>, which is forbidden to all, renders the entire mixture forbidden. The Gemara infers: If it fell <b>into one hundred</b> <i>se’a</i> of non-sacred produce, its prohibition <b>is neutralized.</b> The Sages of the Gemara <b>say</b> in response: <b>We are dealing with <i>teruma</i></b> that is <b>in the possession of a priest,</b> for <b>which</b> the owner <b>can no</b> longer <b>request</b> that a halakhic authority dissolve the designation. However, as long as the <i>teruma</i> is in the owner’s possession he can request that its designation be dissolved, and therefore its prohibition cannot be neutralized.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so, say the latter clause</b> of that mishna: <b>If</b> the <i>teruma</i> mixed with the non-sacred produce <b>was ritually pure,</b> it may <b>be sold to a priest,</b> who treats all the produce as though it were <i>teruma</i>. This indicates that the mishna is dealing with <i>teruma</i> in its owner’s possession that was not yet given to a priest. <b>Rather, we are dealing with</b> the case of <b>an Israelite who inherited</b> the produce <b>from</b> a member of <b>the house of his mother’s father,</b> who is <b>a priest.</b> The heir owns the <i>teruma</i>; however, since he was not the one who designated it as <i>teruma</i>, he may not request that the designation be dissolved.
The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it taught in</b> that <b>latter clause</b> of that mishna: It must <b>be sold to a priest;</b> however, the price must reflect the value of the entire mixture <b>except for the value of that <i>se’a</i></b> of <i>teruma</i> that fell into the non-sacred produce, as the <i>teruma</i> belongs to the priest. If the mishna is referring to the case of an heir who owns the <i>teruma</i>, why can he not collect the value of that <i>se’a</i> as well, as it is his property?
<b>Rather, say</b> that there is another distinction between <i>konamot</i> and other cases where one may request dissolution by a halakhic authority. <b>Granted,</b> in the case of <b><i>konamot</i>,</b> there is <b>a mitzva to request</b> that a halakhic authority dissolve <b>them, due to</b> the statement <b>of Rabbi Natan, as Rabbi Natan said: Anyone who vows, it is as if he built</b> a personal <b>altar</b> outside the Temple, <b>and one who fulfills</b> that vow, it is <b>as though he burns</b> an offering <b>upon it.</b> However, in the case of <b><i>teruma</i>, what mitzva</b> is there <b>to request</b> that a halakhic authority dissolve <b>its</b> designation? Therefore, items forbidden by <i>konamot</i> are considered items that can become permitted, and <i>teruma</i> is not.
§ With regard to <b>the</b> matter <b>itself, Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> With regard to <b>a <i>litra</i> of onions that one tithed, and</b> then <b>sowed, it is tithed according to the entire</b> crop. <b>Rabba sat and stated this <i>halakha</i>. Rav Ḥisda said to him: Who listens to you and Rabbi Yoḥanan, your teacher? The permitted part of</b> the <i>litra</i>, <b>to where did it go?</b> The original <i>litra</i> that he sowed was permitted by virtue of the fact that he tithed it, but why is he obligated to tithe the entire crop? The original <i>litra</i> should be subtracted from the crop that must be tithed. Rabba <b>said to</b> Rav Ḥisda: <b>Didn’t we learn a corresponding</b> <i>halakha</i> in a mishna (<i>Shevi’it</i> 6:3): With regard to sixth-year <b>onions upon which rain fell</b> during the Sabbatical Year, <b>and they sprouted,</b>

Daf 59b

<b>if their leaves were black,</b> the onions are <b>forbidden.</b> If their leaves <b>turned green,</b> the onions are <b>permitted. And if</b> the leaves <b>are black, why</b> are the onions <b>forbidden? Let us say</b> in this case too: <b>The permitted part,</b> the original onion, <b>to where did it go?</b> Rav Ḥisda <b>said to</b> Rabba: <b>Do you maintain</b> that this <i>halakha</i> <b>is taught about the primary,</b> original onion, that it is prohibited? It <b>is taught with regard to the additional</b> growth that sprouted, and it is those leaves that <b>are forbidden.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>If so, what is Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel,</b> who apparently disagrees with the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna, <b>coming to say? As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says: That which grew during</b> a period of <b>liability is liable</b> and is considered Sabbatical-Year produce, and that <b>which grew during</b> a period of <b>exemption is exempt.</b> According to Rav Ḥisda’s explanation, <b>the first <i>tanna</i>,</b> cited in the mishna, <b>also said that.</b>
The Gemara explains: This is not difficult, as this <b>entire mishna, Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel taught it.</b> In the <i>baraita</i> Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel is not disagreeing with the opinion of the first <i>tanna</i> of the mishna; he is merely restating it. <b>And</b> nevertheless, the mishna and the <i>baraita</i> pose no difficulty with regard to the opinion of Rabba, as <b>you heard that Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel</b> said that the prohibition of the primary, original part is not neutralized <b>only</b> in a case where <b>he did not exert</b> himself, and the leaves sprouted on their own. <b>However,</b> in the case <b>where he exerted</b> himself, e.g., by sowing or planting, the prohibition of the original onions is <b>neutralized by the majority.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And anywhere that one exerts</b> himself, <b>is</b> the original part <b>nullified by the majority?</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> isn’t there the case of one who sowed <b>a <i>litra</i> of untithed tithe,</b> where <b>he exerts</b> himself to sow it, <b>and it is taught: And that</b> original <b><i>litra</i></b> of untithed first tithe that he sowed, one <b>proportionally tithes for it from</b> produce in <b>a different place,</b> and its prohibition is not neutralized by the growth. The Gemara answers: It <b>is different with regard to tithe, as the verse states: “You shall tithe</b> all the produce of your seed that is brought forth in the field” (Deuteronomy 14:22), indicating that all permitted seeds that are sown must be tithed, since <b>permitted</b> seeds that were tithed, <b>people</b> typically <b>sow. Forbidden</b> seeds that were not tithed, <b>people do not</b> typically <b>sow,</b> but the Sages penalized one who sowed untithed seeds and required him to tithe that which he was originally obligated to tithe and decreed that it is not neutralized by the majority.
§ With regard to <b>the</b> matter <b>itself, Rabbi Ḥanina Tirta’a said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yannai said:</b> With regard to <b>an onion of <i>teruma</i> that one planted,</b> if <b>its growths exceeded its principal,</b> it <b>is permitted.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Is this to say that growths</b> that are

Daf 60a

<b>permitted neutralize the prohibition? But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna: <b>The growths of <i>teruma</i> are <i>teruma</i>,</b> indicating that they do not neutralize the prohibition of the original part of the plant? The Gemara answers: <b>We are speaking of the growths of growths.</b> Rabbi Yannai permits the <i>teruma</i>, not due to the majority of direct growths of <i>teruma</i>; he permitted it due to the majority of growths that sprouted from its growths. The Gemara asks: <b>We</b> already <b>learned that too: The</b> status of <b>growths of growths</b> of <i>teruma</i> is that of <b>non-sacred</b> produce. The Gemara answers: <b>This teaches us</b> that the growths of growths are permitted <b>even in items whose seeds do not cease,</b> e.g., onions.
The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna: With regard to <b>untithed produce, its growths are permitted in items whose seeds cease; however,</b> concerning <b>items whose seeds do not cease, the growths of growths are forbidden.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>It teaches us that</b> if <b>the increase of the growths</b> of growths <b>exceeded its primary,</b> original part, that original part is <b>permitted.</b>

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one vows: <b>Wine is</b> forbidden to me <b>as if</b> it were <b>an offering [<i>konam</i>],</b> and for <b>that</b> reason <b>I</b> will not <b>taste</b> it <b>today,</b> he is <b>prohibited</b> from drinking wine <b>only until</b> the conclusion of that day at <b>nightfall,</b> and not for a twenty-four hour period. If one vows not to drink wine <b>this week,</b> he is <b>prohibited</b> from drinking wine <b>for the entire</b> remainder of the <b>week. And</b> as <b>Shabbat</b> is considered part of the week <b>that passed,</b> i.e., it is the end of the week, he is prohibited from drinking wine on the upcoming Shabbat. If one vows not to drink wine <b>this month,</b> wine is <b>forbidden</b> to him <b>for the entire</b> remainder of the <b>month; and</b> as <b>the New Moon</b> of the following month is considered part <b>of the next</b> month, he is permitted to drink wine on that day.
If he vowed not to drink wine <b>this year,</b> he is <b>prohibited</b> from drinking wine <b>for the entire</b> remainder of the <b>year; and</b> as <b>Rosh HaShana</b> is considered to be part <b>of the upcoming</b> year, not the current one, he is permitted to drink wine on that day. If he vowed not to drink wine during <b>this seven-year</b> Sabbatical cycle, wine is <b>forbidden</b> to him <b>for the entire</b> remainder of the <b>seven-year</b> cycle; <b>and</b> as <b>the Sabbatical</b> Year is considered part of the cycle <b>that passed,</b> he is prohibited from drinking wine during the upcoming Sabbatical Year.
All this applies if he said that he would not drink wine on this day or this week, <b>but if he said</b> that wine is forbidden to him for <b>one day,</b> or <b>one week,</b> or <b>one month,</b> or <b>one year,</b> or <b>one seven-year</b> cycle, he is <b>prohibited</b> from drinking wine <b>from</b> the <b>day</b> and time he took the vow <b>to</b> the same time the next <b>day,</b> or week, etc.
If he takes a vow that wine is forbidden to him <b>until Passover, it is forbidden</b> to him <b>until</b> Passover <b>arrives.</b> If he said: <b>Until it will be</b> Passover, <b>it is forbidden</b> to him <b>until</b> Passover <b>ends,</b> as he may have intended for the vow to apply as long as it was still Passover (Rosh). If he said: <b>Until before Passover, Rabbi Meir says:</b> It is <b>forbidden</b> to him <b>until</b> Passover <b>arrives. Rabbi Yosei says: It is forbidden</b> to him <b>until it ends.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> We learned in the mishna that if one says: <b>Wine is</b> forbidden to me <b>as if</b> it were <b>an offering [<i>konam</i>],</b> and for <b>that</b> reason <b>I</b> will not <b>taste</b> it today, he is prohibited from drinking wine only until nightfall. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said:</b> Even <b>when darkness falls</b> he is not permitted to drink wine immediately; rather, <b>he is required</b> to <b>request</b> that <b>a halakhic authority</b> dissolve his vow.
The Gemara poses a question: <b>What is the reason</b> that he must request the dissolution of the vow once it has already expired? The Gemara answers that <b>Rav Yosef said:</b> The Sages issued a rabbinic <b>decree</b> in the case of one who said that his vow applies <b>today, due to</b> the confusion that might be caused in a case where one said that his vow applies for <b>one day,</b> and is therefore forbidden to drink wine for twenty-four hours. There is a concern that if one who said that his vow applies today is permitted to drink wine that night, one who took a vow for one day will think that his vow also expires as soon as it is nightfall.

Daf 60b

Abaye <b>said to him: If so,</b> the Sages <b>should</b> likewise <b>decree in the case of</b> one who takes a vow for <b>one day</b> that he must keep the vow until nightfall of the following day, <b>due to</b> the confusion that might be caused in a case where one said that his vow applies <b>today.</b> If the vow expires in the middle of the day, twenty-four hours after he took the vow, people might think that if one takes a vow in the morning and applies it to this day, it also expires in the middle of the day.
Rav Yosef <b>said to him:</b> A vow taken for <b>this day</b> might be <b>interchanged with</b> a vow taken for <b>one day,</b> and one might erroneously conclude that a vow taken for one day expires at nightfall. However, a vow taken for <b>one day</b> is <b>not interchanged</b> with a vow taken for <b>today,</b> and there is no concern that one who takes a vow for today will erroneously conclude that it expires in the middle of the day.
<b>Ravina said: Mareimar said to me: Your father said as follows, in the name of Rav Yosef: In accordance with whose</b> opinion is this <b><i>halakha</i></b> taught <b>by Rav Yirmeya bar Abba?</b> It is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Natan, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Natan says: Anyone who vows,</b> it is <b>as if he</b> has <b>built</b> a personal <b>altar,</b> which is forbidden because one must bring all offerings to the Temple. <b>And one who fulfills</b> the vow, <b>is as though he burns</b> portions meant for the altar in the Temple <b>upon it,</b> i.e., the personal altar, thereby increasing his sin. Consequently, even after he has fulfilled the vow, it is preferable for him to ask a halakhic authority to annul it entirely, so that it will be as if he never took a vow.
§ The mishna stated that one who says: Wine is <i>konam</i> for me, and for that reason I will not taste it <b>this week,</b> is <b>prohibited</b> from drinking wine <b>for the entire</b> remainder of the <b>week,</b> including Shabbat. The Gemara poses a question: <b>Isn’t</b> it <b>obvious</b> that this is the case? The Gemara answers: It is necessary, <b>lest you say</b> that <b>he said the days of the week,</b> i.e., he meant for his vow to apply only on the weekdays. The <i>tanna</i> therefore <b>teaches us</b> that the phrase this week includes Shabbat.
The mishna stated: If one says: <b>This month,</b> it is <b>forbidden</b> to him <b>for the entire</b> remainder of the <b>month, and</b> the <b>New Moon</b> is considered part <b>of the next</b> month. The Gemara asks: <b>Isn’t</b> this <b>obvious?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>When it was necessary</b> to teach this <i>halakha</i> it was <b>for</b> a New Moon preceding <b>a deficient,</b> twenty-nine-day <b>month.</b> In such a case the New Moon is celebrated for two days, the first of which is the thirtieth day of the previous month and the second of which is the first day of the new month. The case here is one where he took the vow on the first day of the New Moon.
This is <b>lest you say</b> that the first day of <b>the New Moon is</b> part <b>of the previous</b> month, <b>and</b> therefore the vow should expire at the end of that day, <b>and</b> wine <b>should not be forbidden</b> to him during the upcoming month. The <i>tanna</i> therefore <b>teaches us</b> that since <b>people call it the New Moon</b> of the upcoming month, it is viewed as part of the upcoming month, and the vow applies to the new month.
§ The mishna states that if one says: <b>This year,</b> it is <b>forbidden</b> to him <b>for the entire</b> remainder of the <b>year,</b> and that Rosh HaShana is considered part of the upcoming year.
<b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the scholars: If one <b>said: Wine is <i>konam</i></b> for me, and for <b>that</b> reason <b>I</b> will not <b>taste</b> it for <b>a day, what is the <i>halakha</i></b> in <b>his</b> case? Is it considered <b>as</b> though he said <b>today,</b> and he is prohibited from consuming wine until nightfall, <b>or</b> is it considered <b>as</b> though he said <b>one day,</b> in which case the vow takes effect for a period of twenty-four hours?
The Gemara suggests: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof <b>from the mishna:</b> If one says: <b>Wine is <i>konam</i></b> for me<b>,</b> and for <b>that</b> reason <b>I</b> will not <b>taste</b> it <b>today,</b> he is <b>prohibited</b> from drinking wine <b>only until</b> the conclusion of that day, at <b>nightfall.</b> The Gemara infers that this <i>halakha</i> only applies if he said the word today; <b>therefore,</b> if he said the vow applies for <b>a day,</b> it is considered <b>comparable</b> to a case where he said <b>one day,</b> and the vow is in effect for twenty-four hours.
The Gemara rejects this proof: But <b>say the latter clause</b> of the mishna: If <b>he said</b> that wine is forbidden to him for <b>one day,</b> he is <b>prohibited</b> from drinking wine <b>from</b> the <b>day</b> he took the vow <b>to</b> the same time on the following <b>day.</b> This indicates that it is only if he said: One day, that the vow takes effect for twenty-four hours; <b>but</b> if he said it takes effect for <b>a day,</b> it is <b>comparable</b> to a case where he said <b>today,</b> and the vow takes effect only until nightfall. <b>Rather, no</b> inference is <b>to be learned from this</b> mishna.
<b>Rav Ashi said: Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a resolution to this question from the following mishna (63a): If one vowed: <b>Wine is <i>konam</i></b> for me, and for that reason <b>I</b> will not <b>taste</b> it <b>this year, then</b> if <b>the year was extended,</b> i.e., declared to be a leap year, he is <b>prohibited</b> from drinking wine <b>in it and its intercalated</b> month. The Gemara inquires: <b>What are the circumstances?</b>

Daf 61a

<b>If we say</b> that it is exactly <b>as it teaches, why do I</b> need <b>to state</b> this <i>halakha</i>? It is obvious that a year means that entire year, even if it is a leap year. <b>Rather, is it not</b> referring to a case <b>where</b> he did not say that the vow applies this year, but rather, <b>he said</b> that it applies for <b>a year,</b> and the mishna teaches that the vow applies for the remainder of that year? <b>Apparently,</b> saying that a vow applies for <b>a year</b> is <b>comparable to</b> saying it applies <b>this year; and</b> similarly, <b>the <i>halakha</i></b> in a case where one accepts a vow for <b>a day</b> should <b>also be like</b> the <i>halakha</i> in a case where one accepts a vow for <b>today.</b>
The Gemara refutes this argument: <b>No, actually,</b> the case in the mishna is <b>that he said</b> his vow should apply <b>this year, and</b> it was necessary to state this <i>halakha</i> <b>lest you say: Follow the majority of years, which do not have an intercalated</b> month, and his vow should be understood as referring to a twelve month period. The <i>tanna</i> therefore <b>teaches us</b> that the phrase this year means that the vow should last until the end of the year.
<b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: If one <b>said:</b> Any <b>wine that I taste</b> for <b>a Jubilee</b> is hereby forbidden to me, <b>what</b> is the <i>halakha</i>? Is <b>the fiftieth year</b> considered <b>as before fifty,</b> i.e., is it included in the vow, <b>or</b> is it considered <b>as after fifty,</b> in which case it is not included in the vow?
The Gemara answers: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that there is <b>a dispute between Rabbi Yehuda and the Rabbis:</b> The verse states: <b>“And you shall sanctify the fiftieth year”</b> (Leviticus 25:10), from which it is derived: <b>You count</b> it as <b>the fiftieth year,</b> i.e., the Jubilee Year, <b>but you do not count</b> it as both the <b>fiftieth year and</b> the <b>first</b> year of the next Sabbatical and Jubilee cycles. <b>From here they stated:</b> The <b>Jubilee Year is not included in the counting of the seven-year</b> cycle of the Sabbatical Year. Rather, the year following the Jubilee Year is considered the first year of the next seven-year cycle. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: The Jubilee Year is included in the counting of the</b> following <b>seven-year</b> cycle of the Sabbatical Year.
The Rabbis <b>said to Rabbi Yehuda: Doesn’t</b> the verse <b>state: “Six years you shall sow your field”</b> (Leviticus 25:3)? <b>But</b> according to your opinion <b>there are only five</b> years <b>here,</b> in the Sabbatical cycle following the Jubilee Year, as the first year would be the Jubilee Year, when it is forbidden to sow one’s field.
Rabbi Yehuda <b>said to them:</b> There is a difficulty <b>according to your statement</b> as well. <b>Doesn’t</b> the verse <b>state: “And it shall bring forth produce for the three years”</b> (Leviticus 25:21)? The Torah promises the Jewish people that in the year preceding the Sabbatical Year, the land will bring forth enough produce to last for the duration of that year, for the Sabbatical Year, and for part of the following year, until the new produce grows. However, in the case of the Jubilee, <b>there are four</b> years to account for, as agricultural labor is prohibited in the forty-ninth year, which is a Sabbatical Year, and in the following year, which is the Jubilee Year.
<b>Rather,</b> you must say that <b>it is possible to establish</b> the verse as referring <b>to the other years of seven-year</b> cycles, i.e., other Sabbatical Years apart from the Sabbatical Year right before the Jubilee Year. With regard to <b>my</b> opinion <b>also, it is possible to establish</b> the verse you presented as a difficulty as referring <b>to the other years of seven-year</b> cycles, i.e., other Sabbatical cycles apart from the cycle immediately following the Jubilee. With regard to the dilemma cited previously, according to the Rabbis, just as the Jubilee Year does not count as part of the ensuing Jubilee cycle because it is considered the end of the previous Jubilee cycle, if one takes a vow and states that it applies for the Jubilee cycle, the Jubilee Year is included in the vow. According to Rabbi Yehuda, the Jubilee Year itself actually begins the next Jubilee cycle, and therefore if one takes a vow for the current Jubilee cycle, the Jubilee Year itself is not included.
§ It was taught in the mishna that if one vows that wine is forbidden to him <b>until Passover,</b> he is <b>prohibited</b> from drinking wine until the Festival arrives. However, if one vows that wine is forbidden to him until before Passover, there is a dispute as to whether the vow remains in effect until the beginning or the end of the Festival. The Gemara asks: <b>Is this to say that Rabbi Meir,</b> who holds that it is prohibited only until the beginning of Passover, <b>maintains</b> that <b>a person does not place himself</b>

Daf 61b

<b>in</b> a position of <b>uncertainty, and Rabbi Yosei holds</b> that <b>a person does place himself in</b> a position of <b>uncertainty?</b>
The Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from the following mishna (<i>Kiddushin</i> 64b): In the case of <b>one who has two groups</b> of two <b>daughters</b> born to him <b>from two women,</b> e.g., he has two daughters from his first wife, and after his first wife died he remarried and had two daughters with his second wife, <b>and he said: I betrothed my older daughter</b> to someone, <b>but I do not know if</b> I meant the <b>older of the older</b> group of daughters; or <b>if</b> I meant <b>the older</b> daughter <b>of the younger</b> group; <b>or if</b> I meant the <b>younger</b> daughter <b>of the older</b> group, <b>who is</b> nevertheless <b>older than the older</b> daughter <b>of the younger</b> group, then <b>all</b> three of those daughters <b>are prohibited</b> to marry another man due to the uncertainty, as he failed to clarify which daughter was betrothed. This applies to all the daughters <b>apart from the younger</b> daughter <b>of the younger</b> group, who is certainly not betrothed. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yosei says: They are all permitted</b> to marry, <b>apart from the older</b> daughter <b>of the older</b> group. This demonstrates that according to Rabbi Meir, one must take into account any of the possible meanings of the imprecise expression: My older daughter, whereas Rabbi Yosei maintains that only the narrowest possible meaning of the phrase is taken into account. This contradicts the mishna here.
In response to this question, <b>Rabbi Ḥanina bar Avdimi said</b> that <b>Rav said: The</b> attribution of the <b>opinions is reversed,</b> i.e., the views stated in the mishna here must be reversed in order to reconcile them with the mishna in <i>Kiddushin</i>. <b>And it is</b> indeed <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>this is the principle:</b> With regard to <b>any</b> vow which specifies <b>a fixed time,</b> i.e., an event that occurs on a particular date, and one <b>said</b> that the vow applies <b>until before</b> that event, <b>Rabbi Meir says</b> the vow applies <b>until</b> the event <b>ends, and Rabbi Yosei says</b> that the vow is in effect only <b>until</b> the event <b>arrives.</b> This is another proof that the opinions in the mishna here must be reversed.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one takes a vow that something is forbidden to him <b>until the grain harvest,</b> or <b>until the grape harvest,</b> or <b>until the olive harvest, it is forbidden</b> to him <b>only until the arrival</b> of that season. <b>This is the principle:</b> With regard to <b>any</b> occasion <b>whose time is fixed, and one said: Until it arrives,</b> it is <b>forbidden</b> to him <b>until</b> the specified occasion <b>arrives.</b> If he <b>said: Until it will be,</b> it is <b>forbidden</b> to him <b>until</b> the specified occasion <b>ends. And</b> with regard to <b>any</b> occasion <b>whose time is not fixed,</b> i.e., it does not fall on a precise date, <b>whether he said: Until it will be,</b> or: <b>Until it arrives,</b> it is <b>forbidden</b> to him <b>only until</b> the specified occasion <b>arrives.</b>
If he said: <b>Until the summer [<i>kayitz</i>],</b> or: <b>Until it will be summer,</b> the vow remains in effect <b>until the people begin to bring</b> fruit <b>into</b> their houses <b>in baskets.</b> If he said: <b>Until the summer has passed,</b> the vow remains in effect <b>until</b> the people <b>set aside [<i>yakpilu</i>] the knives</b> [<b><i>hamaktzuot</i></b>] with which the figs are cut after being harvested, and return them to their place of storage.
