This verse <b>juxtaposes</b> the vows <b>preceding</b> her <b>second</b> instance of <b>being</b> betrothed, i.e., those that she took after her first husband’s death but before her second betrothal, <b>to those</b> vows <b>preceding</b> her <b>first</b> instance of <b>being</b> betrothed. <b>Just as</b> with regard to the vows <b>preceding</b> her <b>first</b> instance of <b>being</b> betrothed, her <b>father nullifies</b> them <b>on his own, so too,</b> with regard to <b>those</b> vows <b>preceding</b> her <b>second</b> instance of <b>being</b> betrothed, her <b>father nullifies</b> them <b>on his own.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Say</b> that <b>this</b> <i>halakha</i> that the father nullifies vows on his own after the death of the betrothed <b>applies</b> only <b>to vows that were not disclosed to the betrothed,</b> i.e., those that he did not have the opportunity to either ratify or nullify, <b>but with regard to vows that were disclosed to the betrothed,</b> the <b>father cannot nullify</b> them on his own.
The Gemara answers: <b>If</b> the verse is referring only <b>to vows that were not disclosed to the betrothed,</b> it would be unnecessary to teach that <i>halakha</i>, as that <b>is derived from</b> the words <b>“being in her youth, in her father’s house”</b> (Numbers 30:17). As long as the young woman is in her father’s house, even after the death of her betrothed, her father has the authority to nullify her vows.
§ The mishna states: <b>In this</b> matter, <b>the power of the father is enhanced relative to the power of the husband.</b> In another matter, the power of the husband is enhanced relative to the power of the father, as the husband nullifies vows during the woman’s adulthood, whereas the father does not nullify vows during her adulthood. The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> under which a husband can nullify his adult wife’s vows?
<b>If we say</b> that the mishna is referring to a case <b>where he betrothed her when she was a young woman,</b> and she took a vow, <b>and</b> then <b>she reached majority,</b> that cannot be the <i>halakha</i>: <b>After all,</b> both the <b>death</b> of her father <b>removes her</b> from the father’s authority <b>and</b> attaining her <b>majority removes her from the father’s authority,</b> so the <i>halakha</i> in the two cases should be the same. <b>Just as</b> with the <b>death</b> of the father, his <b>authority does not revert to the husband</b> and the woman’s betrothed cannot nullify her vows on his own, <b>so too,</b> upon attaining <b>majority</b> the <b>authority</b> the father possessed when she was a young woman <b>does not revert to the husband.</b>
<b>Rather,</b> it is referring to a case in <b>which he betrothed her when she was a grown woman,</b> and then she took a vow. <b>The Gemara asks: Didn’t we</b> already <b>learn</b> that <b>on another occasion,</b> in a later mishna that states (73b): With regard to <b>a grown woman who waited twelve months</b> after her betrothal and then requested that her betrothed marry her, Rabbi Eliezer says: Since her husband is already obligated to provide for her sustenance, as he is obligated to have married her by then, he can nullify her vows by himself, as if he were fully married to her.
The Gemara explains the cited mishna: <b>This</b> cited mishna <b>is itself difficult: You said</b> that <b>a grown woman who waited twelve</b> months is entitled to support. <b>With regard to a grown woman, why do I</b> need <b>a twelve-month</b> waiting period before her betrothed is obligated to marry her? For <b>a grown woman, thirty days suffice for her</b> to prepare what she needs for her marriage after she is betrothed. The Gemara answers: The mishna should be revised. <b>Teach</b> the mishna: <b>A grown woman</b> who waited thirty days <b>and</b> a young woman <b>who waited twelve months.</b>
The Gemara returns to the question: <b>In any case,</b> the fact that the mishna here teaches a <i>halakha</i> that is addressed in a different mishna is <b>difficult.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>If you wish, say</b> that the mishna <b>here</b> is <b>actually</b> the primary source of this <i>halakha</i>, <b>and</b> the reference to <b>a grown woman is taught there because</b> it <b>wants</b> to present how <b>Rabbi Eliezer and the Rabbis disagree.</b>
Alternatively, <b>if you wish, say</b> that the mishna that begins: <b>A grown woman,</b> is <b>actually</b> the source for this <i>halakha</i>. The mishna here repeats the <i>halakha</i> incidentally, <b>since it needs to cite the first clause: In this</b> matter the power of the father is enhanced relative to the power of the husband, therefore the mishna <b>cites</b> the <b>latter clause</b> as well, by writing: <b>In this</b> other matter, the power of the husband is enhanced relative to the power of the father.

Daf 71a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If <b>she took a vow as a betrothed woman</b> and then <b>was divorced on the same day,</b> and <b>she was</b> again <b>betrothed on the same day</b> to another man, or <b>even to one hundred</b> men, one after the other, on a single day, <b>her father and her last husband nullify her vows. This is the principle:</b> With regard to <b>any</b> young woman <b>who has not left</b> her father’s jurisdiction and entered <b>into her own jurisdiction for</b> at least <b>one moment,</b> through full marriage or reaching majority, <b>her father and her final husband nullify her vows.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>From where do we</b> derive <b>that</b> her <b>final betrothed can nullify</b> her <b>vows that were disclosed to the first betrothed? Shmuel said</b> that <b>the verse states: “And if she be to a husband and her vows are upon</b> her…and he nullifies her vow” (Numbers 30:7–9), indicating that he can nullify <b>vows that were upon her already.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Perhaps this statement applies</b> only to vows <b>that were not discerned by the first betrothed, but vows that were discerned by the first betrothed,</b> the <b>final betrothed cannot nullify.</b>
The Gemara answers: The phrase <b>“upon her” is a superfluous</b> part of the <b>verse.</b> One can derive from it that all her vows, including those of which an earlier betrothed had been aware, can be nullified by the final betrothed.
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Shmuel:</b> With regard to <b>a betrothed young woman, her father and her husband</b> together <b>nullify her vows. How so?</b> If <b>her father heard and nullified</b> the vow <b>for her, and the husband did not manage to hear</b> it <b>before he died, and she was betrothed on the same day</b> to another man, <b>or even one hundred times</b> on the same day, <b>her father and her final husband nullify her vows.</b> If <b>her husband heard and nullified</b> the vow <b>for her, and the father did not manage to hear</b> it <b>before the husband died, the father may go back and nullify</b> the <b>husband’s portion.</b>
<b>Rabbi Natan said: This is the statement of Beit Shammai,</b> that each of them nullifies half of the betrothed young woman’s vow. <b>However, Beit Hillel say</b> that the father <b>cannot nullify</b> the vow on his own. The Gemara asks: <b>About what</b> do Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel <b>disagree?</b>

Daf 71b

The Gemara answers: <b>Beit Shammai hold: Even</b> with regard to <b>vows that were disclosed to the betrothed,</b> in the event of his death, his <b>authority reverts to the father.</b> In addition, when the father or husband of a young woman nullifies her vow, <b>he severs</b> his portion of it, enabling the father to nullify her vow on his own after the death of the betrothed. <b>Beit Hillel hold</b> that <b>her father and her final husband</b> together <b>nullify her vows, and he</b> who nullifies her vow <b>does not sever</b> it, but rather weakens its force. Even if the first betrothed was aware of the vow before his death, the father can nullify it in conjunction with another betrothed. This ruling is in accordance with the ruling of Shmuel that her final betrothed can nullify even those vows that she took while betrothed to the first man.
§ <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: Is a husband’s <b>divorce</b> of his wife after she took a vow considered <b>like silence, or</b> is it considered <b>like ratification</b> of the vow?
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the <b>difference</b> between the two possibilities? In any case, he did not nullify her vow before the divorce, and once he has divorced her he can no longer do so. The Gemara answers: There is a difference <b>in</b> a case <b>where she took a vow, and her husband heard</b> the vow, <b>and divorced her, and he remarried her on the same day. If the Master says</b> that divorce is <b>like silence,</b> the husband <b>can</b> now <b>nullify</b> the vow <b>for her,</b> since it is the same day. <b>But if the Master says</b> that divorce is <b>like ratification, he cannot nullify</b> the vow <b>for her,</b> as he has ratified it by divorcing her.

Daf 72a

<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a resolution of the dilemma from the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>When did they say</b> that if <b>the husband died</b> the <b>authority</b> to nullify a young woman’s vows <b>reverts to the father? When the husband did not hear</b> the vow; <b>or he heard</b> the vow <b>and nullified</b> it; <b>or heard</b> it, <b>and was silent, and died on that day. And if you say</b> that <b>divorce is like silence, let</b> the <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i> <b>also teach</b> with regard to the husband: <b>Or he heard</b> the vow <b>and divorced</b> her. <b>From</b> the fact <b>that he did not teach this</b> case, <b>learn from</b> the <i>baraita</i> that <b>divorce is like ratification.</b>
The Gemara rejects the proof from the <i>baraita</i>: <b>State the latter clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i>: <b>But if he heard</b> it <b>and ratified</b> it; <b>or he heard</b> it, <b>and was silent, and died on the following day,</b> then the father <b>cannot nullify</b> the vow. <b>But</b> according to this clause, <b>if you say</b> that <b>divorce is like ratification, let</b> the <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i> also <b>teach: And if he heard</b> the vow <b>and divorced</b> her. <b>Rather, from</b> the fact <b>that</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>does not teach this, learn from</b> the <i>baraita</i> that <b>divorce is like silence.</b>
<b>Rather, one cannot learn</b> anything <b>from this</b> <i>baraita</i> about the effect of divorce on her vows. The Gemara explains that the discrepancy between the two clauses is stylistic and can be explained either way: <b>If</b> the cases in <b>the first clause</b> are chosen <b>precisely,</b> allowing for the inference that divorce is like ratification, then one must say that the <i>tanna</i> <b>formulates the last clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i> as he does <b>because of the first clause,</b> i.e., in the same style, although it does not add anything. <b>If</b> the cases in <b>the last clause</b> are chosen <b>precisely,</b> allowing for the inference that divorce is like silence, then one must say that the <i>tanna</i> <b>formulates the first clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i> as he does <b>because of the last clause,</b> i.e., in the same style, although it does not add anything.
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a mishna (71a): If <b>she took a vow</b> while <b>she was betrothed, and was divorced, and was betrothed</b> again <b>on the same day, even to one hundred</b> men, <b>her father and her final husband nullify her vows. Learn from</b> this mishna that <b>divorce is like silence, because if</b> it were <b>like ratification, could the final betrothed nullify vows that</b> the <b>first betrothed had</b> already <b>ratified?</b>
The Gemara rejects this proof: <b>With what are we dealing here?</b> We are dealing with a case <b>in which the first betrothed man did not hear</b> the vow, and for that reason his divorcing her does not constitute ratification. The Gemara then asks: <b>If so, why</b> mention <b>specifically</b> that the divorce occurred <b>on that day? The same would hold true even after one hundred days as well.</b> Since the first husband never heard the vow, the final husband can nullify it on whichever day he hears it.
The Gemara answers: It is referring to a situation <b>in which</b> the <b>betrothed</b> man <b>did not hear</b> the vow <b>but the father heard</b> it. <b>As</b> in that case, <b>it is</b> only <b>on the same day that he can nullify</b> the vow, <b>but he cannot nullify</b> it <b>from this</b> point <b>forward.</b> Once her father has already heard the vow, her betrothed cannot nullify it on a different day. Therefore, one cannot infer from the mishna that divorce is like silence.
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a resolution of the dilemma from a mishna (89a): If <b>she took a vow on that day,</b> and <b>he divorced her and remarried her on the same day, he cannot nullify</b> her vow. <b>Learn from</b> the mishna that <b>divorce is like ratification.</b>
The Gemara rejects this proof: <b>Say</b> that <b>here,</b> i.e., in the mishna cited, <b>we are dealing with a married woman, and that is the reason that he cannot nullify</b> the vow. It is not because it has been ratified by divorce but <b>because the husband cannot nullify</b> his wife’s vows <b>that precede</b> their marriage. The dilemma remains unresolved.

Daf 72b

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The practice of Torah scholars</b> is to ensure that a woman about to be married should not be encumbered by any vows. A father, <b>before his daughter would leave him</b> through marriage, <b>would say to her: All vows that you vowed in my house are hereby nullified. And similarly, the husband, before she would enter his jurisdiction,</b> i.e., while they were still betrothed, <b>would say to her: All vows that you vowed before you entered my jurisdiction are hereby nullified.</b> This was necessary <b>because once she enters his jurisdiction he cannot nullify</b> the vows she made before that.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rami bar Ḥama asks:</b> Concerning <b>a husband, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>with regard</b> to his <b>nullifying</b> a vow <b>without hearing</b> it? In other words, can a husband state a general nullification of his wife’s vows without being aware of any particular vow? When the verse states: <b>“And her husband hears</b> it, on the day that he hears it, and holds his peace at her, then her vows shall be ratified” (Numbers 30:8), <b>is</b> that referring <b>specifically</b> to a situation where he actually heard of a vow, and only then he can nullify it? <b>Or is</b> it <b>not specifically</b> referring to such a situation, and the mention of hearing is merely because the ordinary situation is that the husband nullifies a vow once he hears it?
<b>Rava said: Come</b> and <b>hear</b> the mishna: <b>The practice of Torah scholars</b> is that a father, <b>before his daughter would leave him</b> through marriage, <b>would say to her: All vows that you vowed in my house are hereby nullified.</b> Rava points out: <b>But</b> the father <b>did not hear</b> her vows, so it must be that one can nullify vows without knowledge that they were actually made.
The Gemara rejects this conclusion: The mishna means that the father states a preemptive nullification that <b>when he</b> will <b>hear</b> a particular vow <b>is when he nullifies</b> it. The vow is not actually nullified until he hears it. The Gemara asks: <b>If so, when he has not</b> actually <b>heard</b> those vows yet, <b>why</b> is it necessary <b>for him to state</b> preemptively that the vows will be nullified; why not wait until he actually hears the vow? The Gemara answers: <b>This teaches us</b> that it is <b>the practice of a Torah scholar to pursue</b> such matters, in order to prompt his daughter or his betrothed to inform him of vows she took, which will then be nullified when he hears of them.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear from the latter clause</b> of the mishna: <b>And similarly, the husband, before she would enter his jurisdiction,</b> i.e., while they were still betrothed, <b>would say to her:</b> All vows that you vowed before you entered my jurisdiction are hereby nullified. This implies that he can nullify vows without hearing them. The Gemara responds: <b>Here too,</b> it means <b>that he says to her: When I hear</b> the particular vow, then it will be nullified.
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> another mishna to answer the question (<i>Nedarim</i> 75a): <b>One who says to his wife: All vows that you vow until I arrive from such and such a place are hereby ratified, has not said anything,</b> i.e., the vows are not ratified. If he says: All vows that you vow until then <b>are hereby nullified, Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> They are <b>nullified.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>But he did not</b> actually <b>hear</b> the particular vows, so one can infer from this that he need not hear her vows in order to nullify them.
The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>Here too,</b> one can understand the situation to be <b>that he says: When I hear</b> the particular vow, it will be nullified. The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> if so, <b>why do I</b> need, i.e., why must the husband state his nullification, <b>from now; let him nullify</b> them <b>for her when he</b> actually <b>hears</b> them. The Gemara answers: <b>He reasons: Perhaps I will be preoccupied at that moment</b> and will forget to nullify them. He therefore nullifies the vows beforehand, so that the nullification will take effect automatically when he hears them.
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>one who says to a steward [<i>apotropos</i>]</b> appointed to manage his affairs in his absence: <b>All vows that my wife vows from now until I arrive from such and such a place</b> you should <b>nullify, and</b> the steward <b>nullified</b> the vows for <b>her,</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>they would be nullified.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Her husband may ratify it, or her husband may nullify it”</b> (Numbers 30:14). The repetition of “her husband” teaches that it is the husband alone who may nullify his wife’s vows; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yoshiya.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yonatan said to him: We have found everywhere in the Torah</b> that the legal status of <b>a person’s agent is like</b> that of <b>himself.</b> Therefore, a steward can nullify the vows on the husband’s behalf.
The Gemara points out: <b>And even Rabbi Yoshiya says</b> that a steward cannot nullify the wife’s vows <b>only because it is a Torah edict,</b> based upon the words <b>“her husband may ratify it, or her husband may nullify it”</b> (Numbers 30:14). <b>But</b> according <b>to everyone,</b> the principle that the legal status of <b>a person’s agent is like</b> that of <b>himself</b> is generally valid. The only objection to the steward nullifying the vows is the Torah edict. The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> these vows <b>were not heard by</b> the steward? This indicates that not having heard the vows is not an obstacle to nullification.

Daf 73a

The Gemara rejects this conclusion as well: <b>Here too,</b> it is a case <b>in which</b> the husband <b>says to</b> the steward: <b>When I hear</b> the vow, then it <b>will be nullified for her.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Let him nullify</b> the vows <b>for her when he</b> actually <b>hears</b> them. Why do so earlier? The Gemara answers: <b>He reasons: Perhaps I will be preoccupied</b> at that moment and will forget to nullify them. The question pertaining to nullification of vows without hearing them is left unresolved.
§ <b>Rami bar Ḥama asks:</b> With regard to <b>a deaf man, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>with regard to his nullifying</b> vows <b>for his wife? If you say</b> that <b>a husband</b> who is not deaf <b>can nullify</b> a vow <b>without hearing</b> it, then perhaps this is <b>because he is capable of hearing. But</b> with regard to <b>a deaf man, who is not capable of hearing,</b> perhaps <b>this is</b> an application of the principle derived from the statement <b>of Rabbi Zeira.</b>
<b>As Rabbi Zeira said:</b> For <b>any</b> amount of flour <b>suitable for mingling</b> with oil in a meal-offering, <b>mingling is not indispensable for it.</b> Even though it is a mitzva to mingle the flour and oil <i>ab initio</i>, if they were not mingled, the meal-offering is still valid. <b>But</b> for <b>any</b> amount of flour <b>not suitable for mingling, mingling is indispensable for it,</b> and such a meal-offering is invalid. The principle is: <i>Ab initio</i> requirements prevent the fulfillment of a mitzva in situations where they are not merely absent but impossible. In this case, the deaf man does not merely not hear the vow, it is impossible for him to do so.
<b>Or perhaps</b> the phrase <b>“and her husband hears it”</b> (Numbers 30:8) <b>does not</b> mean that hearing <b>is indispensable</b> to the nullification of a vow, so that even a deaf man can nullify his wife’s vows. <b>Rava said: Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a <i>baraita</i> interpreting that verse: <b>“And her husband hears it”;</b> this <b>excludes the wife of a deaf man.</b> The Gemara concludes: <b>Learn from</b> this <i>baraita</i> that a deaf man cannot nullify his wife’s vows.
§ <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: Concerning <b>a husband, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>with regard to nullifying</b> vows <b>for his two wives simultaneously?</b> Do the words “but if her husband disallows <b>her</b> on the day that he hears it, and he nullifies her vow which is upon her” (Numbers 30:9), stated in the singular, refer <b>specifically</b> to one wife? <b>Or,</b> perhaps it does <b>not</b> refer <b>specifically</b> to one wife, and a husband can nullify the vows of more than one wife simultaneously.
<b>Ravina said: Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a <i>baraita</i>: In the <i>sota</i> ritual, performed by women suspected by their husbands of having committed adultery, <b>two <i>sota</i> women are not given to drink</b> the bitter waters <b>as one.</b> This is <b>because the heart</b> of each becomes <b>emboldened [<i>gas</i>] in the</b> presence of <b>the other</b> woman, and if one is guilty she will lack the humility to confess.
<b>Rabbi Yehuda says: This is not for</b> that <b>reason [<i>lo min hashem hu zeh</i>], but because it is stated: “And he shall make her drink”</b> (Numbers 5:27), which indicates <b>her by herself.</b> Similarly, the words “disallows her” (Numbers 30:9) should be read as referring specifically to a single woman, indicating that a man cannot nullify the vows of two wives simultaneously.

Daf 73b

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>a grown woman who waited twelve months</b> after her betrothal and the time arrived for her betrothed to marry her, <b>or a widow</b> who waited <b>thirty days</b> and the time arrived for her betrothed to marry her, <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: Since her husband is</b> already <b>obligated</b> to provide <b>for her sustenance,</b> as he is obligated to have married her by then, <b>he can nullify</b> her vows by himself, as if he were fully married to her. <b>But the Rabbis say: The husband does not nullify</b> her vows on his own <b>until she enters his jurisdiction.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rabba says: Rabbi Eliezer and the initial</b> version of the <b>mishna said the same thing, as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Ketubot</i> 57a): <b>A virgin is given twelve months</b> from the time of her betrothal <b>to prepare herself,</b> i.e., to prepare her trousseau. If the end of the <b>twelve-month</b> period <b>arrived,</b> even if he has not married her, she <b>partakes of his food,</b> i.e., he is obligated to provide for her. <b>And</b> if she was betrothed to a priest, <b>she partakes of <i>teruma</i></b> like a priest’s wife. <b>However, a man,</b> i.e., a priest, <b>whose married brother died childless [<i>yavam</i>], does not enable</b> the woman awaiting levirate marriage to him <b>to partake of <i>teruma</i></b> until they are actually married.
If the woman <b>completed six months</b> of awaiting marriage <b>under the aegis of the husband,</b> and he died, <b>and</b> then she completed <b>six months under the aegis of the <i>yavam</i>; or even</b> if she <b>completed all of those</b> months <b>under the aegis of the husband, less one day; or all of them under the aegis of the <i>yavam</i> less one day, she may not partake of <i>teruma</i>. This is</b> the <b>initial</b> version of the <b>mishna.</b> However, <b>a court</b> that convened <b>after them,</b> in a later generation, said: <b>The woman may not partake of <i>teruma</i> until she enters the</b> marriage <b>canopy,</b> thereby finalizing the marriage. Rabbi Eliezer’s opinion that her husband can nullify her vows after the completion of the period allotted after the betrothal follows the initial version, which requires the husband to support her from that point forward.
<b>Abaye said to him: Perhaps it is not so. The initial</b> version of the <b>mishna teaches us only about</b> the permissibility of her <b>partaking of <i>teruma</i></b> whose status is <b>by rabbinic law, but</b> with regard to <b>vows,</b> whose prohibitions have the force of <b>Torah law, say</b> that her betrothed <b>cannot</b> nullify them.
<b>And</b> perhaps <b>you did not hear Rabbi Eliezer</b> state his opinion that a betrothed woman is regarded as married <b>only with regard to vows.</b> Perhaps Rabbi Eliezer holds <b>like that</b> which <b>Rav Pineḥas</b> said <b>in the name of Rava, who said: Any</b> woman <b>who takes a vow, takes a vow</b> contingent <b>upon the consent of her husband,</b> since he provides her sustenance. Perhaps Rabbi Eliezer grants her betrothed authority over her vows only because she vows with his consent in mind, since he is now obligated to provide for her. <b>But</b> with regard to <b><i>teruma</i>,</b> Rabbi Eliezer might hold that <b>even</b> if it is <b><i>teruma</i> by rabbinic law, she may not partake</b> of it.

Daf 74a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>a widow waiting for her <i>yavam</i></b> to perform levirate marriage, <b>whether</b> she is waiting <b>for one <i>yavam</i>,</b> if her late husband had only one brother, or <b>whether</b> she is waiting <b>for two</b> or more <b><i>yevamin</i>,</b> if he had several brothers, <b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> A <i>yavam</i> <b>can nullify</b> her vows. <b>Rabbi Yehoshua says:</b> If she is waiting <b>for one</b> <i>yavam</i>, he can nullify her vows, <b>but not</b> if she is waiting <b>for two. Rabbi Akiva says:</b> A <i>yavam</i> <b>cannot</b> nullify her vows, regardless of whether she is waiting <b>for one</b> <i>yavam</i> <b>or for two</b> or more.
The mishna then elaborates: <b>Rabbi Eliezer said: Just as</b> with regard to <b>a woman he acquired for himself</b> through betrothal, <b>he nullifies her vows,</b> so too with regard to <b>a woman acquired for him from Heaven,</b> i.e., the <i>yevama</i>, <b>isn’t it logical that he should</b> be able to <b>nullify her vows?</b>
<b>Rabbi Akiva said to him: No, if you say</b> that a husband can nullify the vows of <b>a woman he acquired for himself, over whom others have no authority, shall you</b> also <b>say</b> that this is the case with regard to <b>a woman acquired for him from Heaven, over whom others have authority?</b> If there are two <i>yevamin</i>, each <i>yavam</i> has equal authority with regard to her vows.
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua said to him: Akiva, your statement applies</b> in a situation <b>with two <i>yevamin</i>,</b> but <b>how do you reply</b> to Rabbi Eliezer <b>in</b> the case of <b>one <i>yavam</i>?</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>said to him: A <i>yevama</i> is not the full-fledged</b> wife <b>of the <i>yavam</i> in the way that a betrothed woman</b> is <b>her husband’s full-fledged</b> wife, and the <i>yavam</i> is not empowered to nullify vows at all.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The latter two opinions in the mishna <b>make sense: Rabbi Akiva holds</b> that the levirate <b>bond is not substantial.</b> Since the obligation of levirate marriage does not create a marriage-like bond between the <i>yavam</i> and the <i>yevama</i>, a <i>yavam</i> cannot nullify the vows of the <i>yevama</i>. <b>And Rabbi Yehoshua holds</b> that the levirate <b>bond is substantial,</b> so that if there is only one <i>yavam</i>, the <i>yevama</i> is considered his wife, allowing him to nullify her vows. <b>However,</b> with regard to <b>Rabbi Eliezer, what is his reason?</b> Even <b>if</b> he holds that the levirate <b>bond is substantial, there is,</b> nevertheless, <b>no</b> retroactive <b>designation.</b> Since it has not yet been established which of them will be her husband, how can either of them nullify her vows?
<b>Rav Ami said:</b> It is a case <b>where</b> one <i>yavam</i> has already <b>performed levirate betrothal with her.</b> According to Beit Hillel, levirate betrothal does not have the full force of a regular betrothal, <b>but Rabbi Eliezer holds like Beit Shammai, who say: Levirate betrothal effects a full-fledged acquisition</b> just like a regular betrothal.
<b>But Rabbi Yehoshua</b> would <b>say to you,</b> Rabbi Eliezer, in response: <b>That</b> statement, that <b>levirate</b> betrothal effects a full-fledged acquisition, <b>applies</b> only to a case <b>with one <i>yavam</i>, but with two <i>yevamin</i></b> it is <b>not</b> so. The Gemara explains why it cannot be a full-fledged acquisition where there is more than one <i>yavam</i>: <b>Is there anything</b> like this sort of betrothal that <b>when his brother comes,</b> he <b>can render</b> the betrothed woman <b>forbidden to</b> the one who performed levirate betrothal <b>by</b> engaging in <b>sexual intercourse</b> with the <i>yevama</i>, thereby performing levirate marriage, <b>or by</b> giving her <b>a bill of divorce,</b> thereby disqualifying her from levirate marriage, <b>and</b> nevertheless the betrothed <b>can</b> still <b>nullify</b> her vows? Since this betrothal can in essence be nullified, it cannot be viewed as betrothal with regard to nullification of vows. Rabbi Ami completes his analysis: <b>And Rabbi Akiva holds</b> that the levirate <b>bond is not substantial</b> at all, and there is no marital bond between a <i>yevama</i> and her <i>yavam</i> until the levirate marriage is consummated.
The Gemara challenges Rabbi Ami’s interpretation of the dispute: <b>And according to</b> the <i>amora</i> <b>Rabbi Elazar, who said</b> that <b>levirate betrothal, according to Beit Shammai, does not effect</b> a full-fledged <b>acquisition except</b> in that it <b>removes a rival wife</b> from being considered substantially bound to the <i>yavam</i> while she is a <i>yevama</i>, <b>what can be said?</b> Only the relatives of the betrothed <i>yevama</i> are then forbidden to the <i>yavam</i>.
The Gemara answers: <b>Here we are dealing with</b> a case <b>where</b> the <i>yavam</i> <b>stood</b> in court <b>in judgment</b> after the woman demanded that he marry her <b>and he was obligated</b> by the court to provide <b>her sustenance. And</b> this is <b>in accordance with</b> that which <b>Rav Pineḥas</b> stated <b>in the name of Rava, who said: Any</b> woman <b>who vows,</b> that which <b>she vows</b> is contingent <b>upon her husband’s consent.</b> Since under these circumstances the <i>yavam</i> must provide for the <i>yevama</i> he betrothed, he is authorized to nullify her vows.

Daf 74b

The Gemara raises a difficulty with Rabbi Ami’s explanation of Rabbi Eliezer’s opinion: <b>We learned</b> in the mishna that <b>Rabbi Eliezer said: Just as</b> with regard to <b>a woman he acquired for himself</b> through betrothal, <b>he nullifies her vows,</b> so too with regard to <b>a woman acquired for him from Heaven,</b> i.e., the <i>yevama</i>, <b>isn’t it logical that he should</b> be able to <b>nullify her vows? Now if</b> Rabbi Ami’s interpretation is correct, and the mishna is referring <b>to</b> a case <b>where</b> a <i>yavam</i> <b>performed levirate betrothal,</b> then <b>it</b> is actually a case where <b>he acquired</b> a woman <b>for himself</b> by performing levirate betrothal. The Gemara answers: Nevertheless, it is a case <b>where he acquired for himself</b> a woman who was imposed upon him <b>by means of Heaven.</b>
The Gemara raises another difficulty with Rabbi Ami’s interpretation, in that if it is correct, <b>you</b> can <b>resolve</b> the dilemma <b>that Rabba raised:</b> Does <b>levirate betrothal, according to Beit Shammai,</b> merely <b>effect betrothal, or does it effect</b> full-fledged <b>marriage?</b> According to Rabbi Eliezer, <b>you</b> can <b>resolve</b> the dilemma by proving <b>that it effects marriage.</b> The proof is as follows: <b>Because if</b> levirate betrothal <b>effects</b> only <b>betrothal,</b> why does the mishna mention only the <i>yavam</i> with regard to nullification of vows? <b>Didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Nedarim</i> 66b) that with regard to <b>a betrothed young woman, her father and her husband</b> together <b>nullify her vows?</b> If levirate betrothal renders her betrothed to the <i>yavam</i>, the father should also be mentioned as a partner in the nullification.
<b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said:</b> This does not resolve the dilemma, as <b>what</b> could be the meaning of <b>nullify</b> in the mishna? It could mean that <b>he nullifies</b> vows <b>in partnership</b> with the father.
The Gemara comments: <b>It is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>that</b> Rabbi Eliezer is addressing a case where levirate betrothal has been performed, <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Ami:</b> With regard to <b>a widow awaiting her <i>yavam</i>, whether</b> she is waiting for <b>one <i>yavam</i>,</b> if her late husband had only one brother, or for <b>two</b> or more <b><i>yevamin</i>, Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> The <i>yavam</i> <b>can nullify</b> her vows, <b>and Rabbi Yehoshua says:</b> He can nullify her vows only in a case where she is waiting <b>for one</b> <i>yavam</i>, <b>but not</b> if she is waiting <b>for two. Rabbi Akiva says:</b> Nullification is not possible at all, <b>not</b> if she is waiting <b>for one, and not</b> if she is waiting <b>for two</b> or more.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>Rabbi Eliezer said</b> to his disputants: <b>And just as if</b> one accepts that a man cannot nullify the vows of <b>a woman in whom he has no share until she enters into his jurisdiction</b> through betrothal, yet <b>once she enters into his jurisdiction, she is fully</b> under <b>his</b> authority for the nullification of her vows, so too with regard to <b>a woman</b> in whom <b>he has a share before she enters his jurisdiction,</b> i.e., his <i>yevama</i>, <b>once she enters his jurisdiction, is it not logical that she be fully</b> under <b>his</b> authority for the nullification of her vows?
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>Rabbi Akiva said to him: No,</b> your <i>a fortiori</i> inference is refutable. <b>If you spoke of</b> a man having authority over the vows of <b>a woman he acquired for himself</b> through betrothal, <b>that</b> would be different: <b>Just as he has no share in her</b> before betrothal, <b>so</b> too <b>others have no share in her. Will you say</b> the same <b>with regard to a woman</b> who is <b>acquired for him from Heaven,</b> i.e., his <i>yevama</i>, <b>for whom, just as he has a share in her, so do others,</b> i.e., his brothers, also <b>have a share in her,</b> as they also are <i>yevamin</i>?
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>Rabbi Yehoshua said to him: Akiva, your statement</b> fits a situation <b>with two <i>yevamin</i>,</b> but <b>what do you answer for</b> the case of <b>one <i>yavam</i>?</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>replied to him: Did we distinguish between one <i>yavam</i> and two <i>yevamin</i>,</b> regardless of <b>whether he performed levirate betrothal or whether he did not perform levirate betrothal? And</b> just <b>as</b> in <b>other matters</b> there is no such distinction, <b>so</b> too with regard to <b>vows.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> adds a comment: <b>Ben Azzai stated</b> his response to hearing this <b>discussion in this language: Woe [<i>ḥaval</i>] to you, ben Azzai, that you did not serve Rabbi Akiva</b> properly.
Since this <i>baraita</i> was cited in support of Rabbi Ami’s interpretation, the Gemara asks: In <b>what</b> way

Daf 75a

<b>is it taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Ami?</b> The Gemara explains that support for Rabbi Ami’s opinion is found in <b>that which is taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i>, as Rabbi Akiva mentions the following distinction: <b>Whether he performed levirate betrothal or whether he did not perform levirate betrothal.</b> This indicates that Rabbi Eliezer’s statement is referring to a <i>yevama</i> with whom he performed levirate betrothal.
<b>Alternatively,</b> support can be derived <b>from the first clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i>, <b>which teaches: Once she enters his jurisdiction, she is fully</b> under <b>his</b> authority. <b>If</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring to a situation <b>where he did not betroth</b> her in levirate betrothal, in <b>what</b> sense is <b>she fully</b> under <b>his</b> authority? <b>Deduce from it</b> that the situation is one <b>in which he has performed levirate betrothal.</b>
The Gemara then asks with regard to the <i>baraita</i> itself. <b>What</b> is the meaning of the phrase: <b>And</b> just <b>as</b> in <b>other matters</b> there is no such distinction, <b>so</b> too with regard to <b>vows, that</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>teaches</b> in the <i>baraita</i>? <b>Rava said: This is what</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>is teaching: Do you not concede that</b> one is <b>not liable to</b> be punished with <b>stoning</b> for adulterous relations with a <i>yevama</i> <b>as</b> he would be if she were <b>a betrothed young woman?</b> The status of the relationship is inferior to proper marriage, as one who engages in sexual intercourse with a <i>yevama</i> does not incur the death penalty. Accordingly, the authority of the <i>yavam</i> with regard to vows is also inferior.
<b>Rav Ashi said: The mishna</b> (74a) <b>is also precisely</b> formulated to indicate this, as it teaches: <b>A <i>yevama</i> is not her husband’s full-fledged</b> wife <b>in the same manner that a betrothed woman</b> is <b>her husband’s full-fledged</b> wife.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One who says to his wife: All vows that you will vow from now until I arrive from such and such a place are hereby ratified, has not said anything,</b> i.e., the vows are not ratified. However, if he states that all vows that she will take until then <b>are hereby nullified, Rabbi Eliezer said:</b> They are <b>nullified, while the Rabbis say:</b> They are <b>not nullified. Rabbi Eliezer said</b> in explanation: <b>If one can nullify vows that have reached the status of a prohibition,</b> i.e., that have already taken effect, <b>shall he not</b> be able to <b>nullify vows that have not reached the status of a prohibition?</b>
The Rabbis <b>said to him</b> in response: The verse <b>states:</b> “Every vow, and every binding oath to afflict the soul, <b>her husband may ratify it, or her husband may nullify it”</b> (Numbers 30:14). This teaches: <b>That which has reached the status of</b> eligibility for <b>ratification,</b> i.e., a vow that she has already taken, <b>has reached the status of</b> eligibility for <b>nullification.</b> However, a vow that <b>has not reached the status of</b> eligibility for <b>ratification</b> has <b>not reached the status of</b> eligibility for <b>nullification</b> either, and it cannot be nullified.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: <b>According to Rabbi Eliezer,</b> do the vows that the husband nullifies in advance <b>take effect</b> momentarily <b>and are</b> then <b>canceled</b> immediately after? <b>Or perhaps they do not take effect at all.</b> The Gemara inquires: <b>In what</b> case is there <b>a difference</b> between these possibilities?

Daf 75b

The Gemara answers: There is a difference in a case <b>where another</b> person <b>associated</b> his own vow <b>with this vow.</b> If someone else heard her vow and declared his vow to be like hers, the status of his vow depends on this question. <b>If you say</b> that such vows <b>take effect,</b> then <b>the association</b> of the other person’s vow <b>takes effect. If you say</b> that such vows <b>do not take effect</b> at all, then the vow of the other person <b>has no substance,</b> as the vow with which he associated it never existed.
<b>What,</b> then, is Rabbi Eliezer’s opinion? <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> the mishna: <b>Rabbi Eliezer said: If one can nullify vows that have reached the status of a prohibition, shall he not nullify vows that have not reached the status of a prohibition? Learn from it</b> that such vows <b>do not take effect</b> at all, as they are described as not having reached the status of a prohibition.
The Gemara rejects this conclusion: <b>Does</b> the mishna <b>teach</b> using the words: <b>That do not reach</b> the status of a prohibition? It <b>teaches: That have not reached</b> the status of a prohibition, which could mean that <b>they have not yet reached</b> the status of a prohibition, but they may take effect, momentarily, when she actually takes the vow.
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a <i>baraita</i> from the <i>Tosefta</i> (<i>Nedarim</i> 6:5): <b>Rabbi Eliezer said to them: And just as in a situation where he cannot nullify his own vows once he has vowed, he can nullify his own vows before he vows</b> by stipulating beforehand that the vows he takes should not take effect, all the more so in <b>a situation where he can nullify his wife’s vows</b> even <b>after she vows, is it not logical that he should</b> be able to <b>nullify his wife’s vows before she vows?</b>
The Gemara explains the proof: <b>What, is it not referring to</b> where <b>his wife’s</b> vows are <b>similar to his own</b> in that <b>just as his</b> vows, which were nullified in advance, <b>do not take effect</b> at all, <b>so too</b> the vows of <b>his wife do not take effect</b> at all when nullified in advance? The Gemara rejects this: <b>No, this</b> case of his nullifying his own vows prior to taking them is <b>as it is, and that</b> case of his nullifying her vows prior to her taking them is <b>as it is;</b> the similarity between the two cases is sufficient for an <i>a fortiori</i> inference, but they are not similar in all respects.
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a <i>baraita</i>: <b>They said to Rabbi Eliezer:</b> Your <i>a fortiori</i> inference is flawed, as can be seen from this example: <b>And just as</b> with regard to <b>a ritual bath, which elevates the impure from their</b> ritual <b>impurity</b> when they immerse in it but <b>does not save the pure from becoming impure</b> if they come into contact with impurity after immersion, so too, with regard to <b>a person, who does not elevate the impure from their impurity,</b> e.g., when a person swallows a ritually impure object and then immerses in a ritual bath, the object remains impure, <b>is it not logical that he should not save pure</b> items <b>from becoming impure?</b> When one who has swallowed a ritually pure object comes into contact with impurity, the object he has swallowed should also become impure. This is not the case, as a swallowed object does not become impure when the person who swallowed it does. Therefore the <i>a fortiori</i> argument is flawed, and one cannot derive from the ability to change a status, like the husband’s ability to nullify his wife’s vows or the ability of the ritual bath to render something ritually pure, that the source of the change can also prevent a change of status or preserve that status.
However, <b>learn from</b> this <i>baraita</i> that, according to Rabbi Eliezer, vows nullified by the husband from the outset <b>do not take effect</b> at all, as the Rabbis’ objection presumes that according to Rabbi Eliezer, the vows do not take effect at all. Their argument is based on the analogy between preemptive nullification and preemptive prevention of impurity. Preemptive prevention of impurity is understood to be saving an item from ever becoming impure and analogously, preemptive nullification is understood to be preventing a vow from ever taking effect.

Daf 76a

The Gemara rejects this conclusion and refers back to the <i>baraita</i>. <b>Say the latter clause</b> of that <i>baraita</i>: <b>They said to Rabbi Eliezer: If one immerses an impure vessel to purify</b> it, <b>shall one immerse a vessel</b> in advance so that <b>when it will become impure it will</b> then <b>be purified? Learn from</b> this clause of the <i>baraita</i> that according to Rabbi Eliezer, vows nullified preemptively <b>take effect</b> momentarily and are then immediately nullified. The Rabbis’ objection is that according to Rabbi Eliezer, prior immersion should purify an item that momentarily became impure.
The Gemara rejects this conclusion: One could <b>say</b> that <b>the Rabbis could not determine the reasoning of Rabbi Eliezer, and this is what</b> they <b>said to him: What do you hold? If you hold that</b> preemptively nullified vows <b>take effect</b> momentarily <b>and are</b> then <b>nullified,</b> then the example of <b>a vessel will be your refutation,</b> i.e., will serve to refute your opinion. <b>If you do not hold that they take effect,</b> but rather that they do not take effect at all, then the example of <b>a ritual bath will be your refutation.</b>
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear: Rabbi Eliezer said to them: And just as ritually impure seeds, once one has sown them in the ground,</b> become <b>pure,</b> then with regard to those which <b>are already sown</b> and then come into contact with impurity, should they <b>not all the more so</b> be pure? Similarly, vows that have been preemptively nullified should be nullified, since a husband can nullify vows after they have been taken. <b>Learn from</b> this <i>baraita</i> that according to Rabbi Eliezer preemptively nullified vows <b>do not take effect</b> at all, just as seeds that were already sown do not become impure at all.
The Gemara comments: <b>And the Rabbis, do they not teach</b> <i>halakhot</i> based upon an <b><i>a fortiori</i></b> inference of this sort? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Can a person sell his daughter</b> as a maidservant <b>when she is a young woman? You</b> can <b>say an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference to show that he cannot: A maidservant who was <b>already sold goes free</b> upon becoming a young woman; with regard to one <b>who has not been sold, is it not logical that she cannot be sold</b> once she already is a young woman? This <i>baraita</i> shows that the Rabbis do utilize similar <i>a fortiori</i> inferences.

Daf 76b

The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, generally they do teach</b> <i>halakhot</i> based upon an <b><i>a fortiori</i></b> inference of this type, <b>but here it is different, as the verse states: “Her husband may ratify it, or her husband may nullify it”</b> (Numbers 30:14). The Rabbis interpret this to mean: <b>That which has become eligible</b> for <b>ratification,</b> i.e., a vow that she has already taken, <b>has become eligible for nullification.</b> However, <b>that which has not become eligible</b> for <b>ratification,</b> i.e., a vow she has not yet taken, <b>has not become eligible for nullification.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The <b>nullification of vows</b> can be performed <b>all day</b> on the day on which the vow was heard. <b>There is in this matter</b> both <b>a leniency,</b> extending the nullification period, <b>and a stricture,</b> curtailing that period.
<b>How so?</b> If a woman <b>took a vow on Shabbat evening,</b> her father or husband <b>can nullify</b> the vow <b>on Shabbat evening, and on Shabbat day until dark.</b> This is an example of extending the nullification period. However, if <b>she took a vow with nightfall</b> approaching, her father or husband <b>can nullify</b> the vow only <b>until nightfall, since, if it became dark and he had not</b> yet <b>nullified</b> her vow, <b>he cannot nullify</b> it anymore. This is an example of a curtailed nullification period.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The <b>nullification of vows</b> can be performed <b>all day</b> on the day on which the vow was heard. <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, and Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, said:</b> A vow can be nullified <b>for</b> a twenty-four-hour <b>period</b> from the time it was heard. The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason for the</b> opinion of the <b>first <i>tanna</i>?</b> The Gemara answers: Since <b>the verse states:</b> “But if her husband make them null and void <b>on the day that he hears them”</b> (Numbers 30:13), he derives that the husband can nullify his wife’s vow only until the end of the day on which he hears the vow.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>the Rabbis,</b> Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, and Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Shimon, <b>what is their reason?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>It is written: “From day to day”</b> (Numbers 30:15), which indicates that a vow can be nullified from a particular hour on one day until the same hour on the following day.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to the first <i>tanna</i>, isn’t it written: “From day to day”?</b> The Gemara answers: That verse <b>is necessary, as if</b> it had said only <b>“on the day that he hears them,” I would say</b> that <b>during the day, yes,</b> he can nullify vows, but <b>at night</b> he is <b>not</b> able to. Therefore, <b>it is written: “From day to day,”</b> to teach that nullification can be performed even between one day and the next, i.e., night.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to the one who said</b> that <b>“from day to day”</b> indicates a twenty-four-hour period for nullification, <b>isn’t it written: “On the day that he hears them?”</b> The Gemara explains here, too: That verse <b>was necessary, as if</b> it had <b>written</b> only <b>“from day to day,” I would say</b> that he <b>can nullify</b> a vow <b>for her</b> until the same day in the following week, i.e., he can nullify her vow during the entire period <b>from one Sunday to</b> the next <b>Sunday.</b> Therefore, it <b>is written: “On the day that he hears them”</b> to teach that nullification is limited to a period of a single day of twenty-four hours.
<b>Rabbi Shimon ben Pazi said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said: The <i>halakha</i> is not in accordance with that pair [<i>zug</i>],</b> Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, and Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Shimon, who hold that one has an entire twenty-four-hour period to nullify vows. Rather, one can nullify only on that day, as stated in the mishna. The Gemara relates: <b>Levi thought to act in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>those</b> two <b><i>tanna’im</i>. Rav said to him: So said my uncle [<i>ḥavivi</i>],</b> Rabbi Ḥiyya: <b>The <i>halakha</i> is not in accordance with that pair.</b>
The Gemara relates that when a case of dissolving a vow was brought before <b>Ḥiyya bar Rav,</b> he <b>would shoot an arrow [<i>gira</i>] and examine</b> the vow at the same time. In other words, he would not examine the case in great depth, but would immediately dissolve it. Similarly, <b>Rabba bar Rav Huna would sit</b> to review the vow <b>and stand</b> immediately, without conducting a comprehensive examination.

Daf 77a

§ <b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>elsewhere</b> (<i>Shabbat</i> 157a): A father or husband <b>may nullify</b> his daughter’s or his wife’s <b>vows on Shabbat and one may request</b> from a halakhic authority <b>to</b> dissolve <b>vows that are for the purpose of Shabbat. A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: May one <b>nullify vows on Shabbat</b> only <b>when</b> they are <b>for the purpose of Shabbat, or</b> may one <b>perhaps</b> nullify vows on Shabbat <b>even when they are not for the purpose</b> of Shabbat?
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>that Rav Zuti from the school of Rav Pappi taught: Vows may be nullified</b> on Shabbat <b>only for the purpose of Shabbat. Rav Ashi said: We did not learn that way</b> in the mishna here. The mishna teaches: If <b>she took a vow with nightfall</b> approaching, her father or husband <b>can nullify</b> the vow <b>for her</b> only <b>until nightfall. And if you say</b> that with regard to nullification of vows that <b>are for the purpose of Shabbat, yes,</b> he may nullify those vows, but nullifications <b>that are not for the purpose of Shabbat, no,</b> he may not, <b>why</b> does the <i>tanna</i> <b>specify nightfall?</b> After all, <b>even during the day he may not nullify that</b> which <b>is not for the purpose</b> of Shabbat.
The Gemara rejects this conclusion: Nullification on Shabbat is subject to a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>: Nullification of vows</b> can be performed <b>all day</b> on the day that the vow was heard. And <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, and Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, said:</b> A vow can be nullified <b>for</b> a twenty-four-hour <b>period</b> from the time it was heard.
<b>According to the one who says all day, yes,</b> one can nullify vows all day, but <b>not more</b> than that; <b>he may nullify</b> on Shabbat <b>even</b> when it is <b>not for the purpose of Shabbat,</b> since otherwise, he could not nullify a vow taken on Shabbat at all. <b>According to the one who says</b> that one can nullify her vows <b>for</b> a twenty-four-hour <b>period,</b> that which is <b>for the purpose of Shabbat, yes,</b> he may nullify, but that which is <b>not necessary for Shabbat,</b> he <b>may not</b> nullify, as he can do so after Shabbat.
§ The mishna from tractate <i>Shabbat</i> teaches: <b>And one may request</b> from a halakhic authority <b>to</b> dissolve <b>vows that are for the purpose of Shabbat. A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: Is this specifically <b>when those</b> who took the vows <b>did not have the opportunity</b> to request dissolution of the vows before Shabbat, <b>or perhaps</b> it is the case even <b>when they did have the opportunity?</b> The Gemara responds: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear:</b> It happened <b>that the Sages attended to</b> the dissolution of the vows of <b>the son of Rav Zutra, son of Rav Zeira,</b> on Shabbat, <b>even for vows that they had the opportunity</b> to dissolve <b>while it was still day,</b> before Shabbat had begun.
<b>Rav Yosef thought to say:</b> With regard to <b>requesting</b> that a halakhic authority dissolve <b>vows on Shabbat, yes,</b> requesting <b>of a single expert</b> is permitted on Shabbat, but requesting <b>of three laymen</b> is <b>not</b> permitted on Shabbat, <b>because it looks like</b> a court <b>judgment,</b> which may not be performed on Shabbat.
<b>Abaye said to him: Since we hold</b> that vows may be dissolved <b>even</b> while the halakhic authority <b>is standing,</b> and <b>even by relatives, and even at night,</b> it <b>does not look like a judgment.</b> Since it is not regarded as an act of a court, vows may be dissolved on Shabbat even by three laymen.
<b>Rabbi Abba said</b> that <b>Rav Huna said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i></b> is that <b>one can nullify vows at night.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But this is</b> already stated in <b>the mishna:</b> If a woman <b>took a vow on Shabbat evening,</b> her father or husband can nullify the vow on Shabbat evening; why would Rav Huna need to state his <i>halakha</i>? <b>Rather, say</b> that Rav’s ruling was as follows: The <b><i>halakha</i></b> is that <b>one can request</b> that a halakhic authority dissolve a vow <b>at night.</b>
<b>Rabbi Abba said to Rav Huna: Did Rav say that?</b> Rav Huna <b>said to him:</b> Rav <b>was silent [<i>ishtik</i>]</b> when this ruling was stated in his presence. Rabbi Abba <b>said to him: Did you say: He was silent [<i>ishtik</i>],</b> indicating that he accepted this ruling, <b>or did you say:</b> He was <b>drinking [<i>shatei</i>],</b> and was therefore preoccupied, so that his silence did not necessarily indicate agreement?
In order to clarify Rav’s opinion on the issue, the Gemara cites <b>Rav Ika bar Avin</b> who <b>said: Rav attended to</b> the dissolution of a vow made by <b>Rabba,</b>

Daf 77b

<b>in</b> a side <b>room [<i>kitona</i>] of the study hall,</b> while <b>standing, alone and at night.</b>
<b>Rabba said</b> that <b>Rav Naḥman said:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i></b> is that <b>one can request</b> the dissolution of <b>vows</b> even when the halakhic authority is <b>standing</b> or <b>alone,</b> i.e., without a court of three, as long as he is a halakhic authority, <b>and</b> that one can do so <b>at night, on Shabbat, and by relatives, and even</b> when <b>those</b> requesting dissolution on Shabbat <b>had the opportunity</b> to do so <b>while it was still day,</b> i.e., before Shabbat.
The Gemara raises a difficulty with this ruling: Can a judge dissolve a vow while <b>standing? But it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabban Gamliel alighted from a donkey, and wrapped himself</b> in his shawl in the customary manner of a judge, <b>and sat, and dissolved</b> a man’s <b>vow for him.</b> If one can dissolve a vow while standing, why did he sit? The Gemara explains: <b>Rabban Gamliel holds</b> that one must <b>broach</b> dissolution based <b>on regret.</b> In other words, a halakhic authority does not dissolve vows directly, but must prompt the one who took the vow to concede that he regrets having taken the vow in the first place. <b>We require that the vow be uprooted, and he needed to examine</b> the case; <b>therefore, he sat down. But Rav Naḥman holds</b> that one need <b>not broach</b> dissolution based <b>on regret, and</b> therefore a halakhic authority can dissolve the vow <b>even</b> while <b>standing.</b>
<b>Rava said to Rav Naḥman: Master, see that Sage who came from the West,</b> Eretz Yisrael, <b>and</b> who <b>said: The Sages attended to</b> the dissolution of a vow taken by <b>the son of Rav Huna bar Avin, and they dissolved his vow and said to him: Go and request mercy for yourself, for you have sinned</b> by taking a vow. <b>As Rav Dimi, the brother of Rav Safra teaches:</b> With regard to <b>anyone who takes a vow, even if he fulfills it, he is called a sinner. Rav Zevid said: What verse</b> teaches this? It is: <b>“But if you refrain to vow, it will be no sin in you”</b> (Deuteronomy 23:23). It may be inferred <b>that</b> if <b>you did not refrain</b> from taking vows, <b>there is sin.</b>
§ <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who says to his wife: Any vows which you will vow, I do not want [<i>ee efshi</i>] you to vow,</b> or one who wants to nullify a vow and says: <b>This is not a vow, has not said anything,</b> as this is not a valid formula of nullification. If he says: <b>You have done well,</b> or: <b>There are none like you,</b> or: <b>If you had not taken a vow, I, myself, would have taken a vow</b> to obligate <b>you</b> in this, <b>his statement is substantial,</b> and the vow is ratified.
<b>A man should not say to his wife</b> when nullifying her vows <b>on Shabbat: It is nullified for you,</b> or: <b>It is canceled for you, in the manner that he would say to her on weekdays. Rather, he should say to her,</b> if she took a vow to refrain from food or drink: <b>Take</b> this <b>and eat</b> it, or: <b>Take</b> this <b>and drink</b> it, <b>and the vow is canceled on its own. Rabbi Yoḥanan said: And he must</b> also <b>cancel</b> the vow <b>in his heart;</b> simply telling her to eat or drink is not sufficient.
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Beit Shammai say: On Shabbat he cancels</b> the vow <b>in his heart</b> and <b>on a weekday he articulates</b> the nullification <b>with his lips. And Beit Hillel say: Both</b> in <b>this</b> case <b>and</b> in <b>that</b> case, i.e., whether on Shabbat or a weekday, it is sufficient if <b>he cancels</b> the vow <b>in his heart, and he need not articulate with his lips.</b>
§ <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: A halakhic authority who pronounced</b> his dissolution of a vow <b>with language</b> appropriate to <b>a husband,</b> i.e., he said the word nullified instead of dissolved, <b>or a husband who pronounced</b> his nullification of a vow <b>with language</b> appropriate to <b>a halakhic authority,</b> i.e., he said the word dissolved instead of nullified, <b>has not said anything.</b> Each of them has the authority to cancel a vow only in the particular manner allotted to him.
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse <b>“This is the thing</b> which the Lord has commanded” (Numbers 30:2) indicates that the husband’s nullification, which is the topic of the subsequent verses, must be done specifically in this way. The Sages concluded that <b>a halakhic authority dissolves</b> a vow, <b>but a husband does not dissolve</b> it. <b>As,</b> one <b>might</b> have thought: <b>And just as a halakhic authority, who cannot nullify</b> vows, nevertheless <b>dissolves</b> them, so too with regard to <b>a husband, who can nullify</b> vows, <b>is it not logical that he should</b> also <b>dissolve</b> them? Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b>

Daf 78a

<b>“This is the thing”</b> (Numbers 30:2), to teach that the husband nullifies vows and <b>a halakhic authority dissolves</b> vows, <b>but a husband does not dissolve</b> them. It <b>is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: The phrase <b>“this is the thing”</b> teaches that <b>a husband nullifies</b> vows <b>but a halakhic authority does not nullify</b> vows. <b>As,</b> one <b>might</b> have thought: <b>Just as a husband, who cannot dissolve</b> vows, nevertheless <b>nullifies</b> them, so too with regard to <b>a halakhic authority, who can dissolve</b> vows, <b>is it not logical that he should</b> also <b>nullify</b> them? Therefore, <b>the verse states: “This is the thing,”</b> to teach us that <b>a husband nullifies</b> vows, <b>but a halakhic authority does not nullify</b> them.
It is taught in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>It is stated here,</b> with regard to vows: <b>“This is the thing,” and it is stated elsewhere:</b> “Speak to Aaron, and to his sons, and to all the children of Israel, and say to them: <b>This is the thing</b> which the Lord has commanded, saying” (Leviticus 17:2), <b>in</b> the verse introducing the prohibition against <b>slaughtering</b> offerings <b>outside</b> of the Temple courtyard. <b>Just as with regard to</b> offerings <b>slaughtered outside</b> the Temple courtyard, the verse is directed to <b>Aaron and his sons and all</b> of <b>Israel, so too, the portion</b> in the Torah <b>about vows</b> is directed to <b>Aaron and his sons and all</b> of <b>Israel. And just as here,</b> with regard to vows, the verse states: “And Moses spoke to <b>the heads of the tribes</b> of the children of Israel” (Numbers 30:2), <b>so too, there,</b> with regard to offerings slaughtered outside the Temple courtyard, Moses spoke to <b>the heads of the tribes.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to the</b> Torah <b>portion on vows, for what <i>halakha</i></b> is the verbal analogy between it and slaughtering offerings outside the Temple courtyard taught? <b>Rav Aḥa bar Ya’akov says:</b> The verbal analogy is the source <b>to authorize three laymen</b> to dissolve vows. The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But isn’t it written: “The heads of the tribes”? Rav Ḥisda said, and some say</b> it was <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> From the phrase “the heads of the tribes” the Sages derive that vows can also be dissolved <b>by a single expert.</b>
The Gemara then asks the corresponding question about the other passage. The verbal analogy connects <b>“the heads of the tribes” to</b> offerings <b>slaughtered outside</b> of the Temple courtyard. <b>For what <i>halakha</i></b> is this connection made? <b>Rav Sheshet said:</b> This connection is made in order <b>to say that there is</b> a concept of <b>requesting</b> dissolution of consecration <b>of consecrated</b> property.
The Gemara asks: <b>According to Beit Shammai, who say</b> that <b>there is no</b> possibility of <b>requesting</b> from a halakhic authority to cancel the consecration <b>of consecrated</b> property, the treatment of the verse <b>“the heads of the tribes” as</b> if <b>it were written</b> also <b>about</b> offerings <b>slaughtered outside</b> the Temple courtyard serves <b>to</b> teach <b>what <i>halakha</i>?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Beit Shammai do not have</b> a tradition of interpreting the verses in accordance with this <b>verbal analogy.</b>
The Gemara asks: Since Beit Shammai do not use this verbal analogy, <b>for what</b> purpose <b>is “this is the thing,” in the portion on vows, written?</b> The Gemara answers: It is written <b>to say</b> that only <b>a halakhic authority dissolves</b> vows, <b>but a husband does not dissolve</b> them; <b>a husband nullifies</b> vows, <b>but a halakhic authority does not nullify</b> them.
According to Beit Shammai, <b>for what</b> purpose <b>is written</b> the phrase <b>“this is the thing,”</b> found <b>in</b> the portion on offerings <b>slaughtered outside</b> of the Temple courtyard? The Gemara answers: It is written <b>to say</b> that one <b>is liable for slaughtering</b> outside, <b>but</b> one is <b>not liable for pinching</b> the neck of a bird-offering outside the Temple courtyard, although that is the way it would be killed if it were a valid offering in the Temple.
The Gemara asks: <b>But according to Beit Shammai,</b> who do not accept the verbal analogy between vows and the prohibition against slaughtering offerings outside of the Temple courtyard, <b>from where do we</b> derive the source <b>to authorize three laymen</b> to dissolve vows? The Gemara answers: Beit Shammai <b>derive it from</b> the explanation given to <b>Rav Asi bar Natan.</b>
This is <b>as it is written: “And Moses declared the Festivals of the Lord to the children of Israel”</b> (Leviticus 23:44). <b>And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yosei HaGelili says: The Festivals are stated, but Shabbat,</b> which commemorates <b>Creation, is not stated with them. Ben Azzai says: The Festivals are stated, but the portion on vows is not stated with them.</b>
The Gemara relates that <b>Rav Asi bar Natan had a difficulty with this <i>baraita</i>. He came to Neharde’a</b> to ask about it <b>before Rav Sheshet, but he did not find him</b> there. <b>He pursued him to Meḥoza</b> and <b>said to</b> him: How can the <i>baraita</i> say that <b>the Festivals of the Lord were stated, but Shabbat,</b> which commemorates <b>Creation, was not stated with them?</b>
<b>But Shabbat is written with them</b> in the portions of the Torah about the Festivals (Leviticus 23:3; Numbers 28:9–10). <b>And furthermore,</b> can it be said that <b>the Festivals of the Lord are stated, but the portion on vows</b> (Numbers, chapter 30) <b>is not stated with them? Isn’t it next to</b> one of the portions in the Torah detailing the <i>halakhot</i> of the Festivals (Numbers, chapters 28–29)? Rav Sheshet <b>said to him: This is what</b> Rabbi Yosei HaGelili’s statement in the <i>baraita</i> <b>is teaching:</b>

Daf 78b

<b>The Festivals of the Lord require sanctification by the court,</b> as the Festival dates are established by the court’s determination of the New Moon, whereas <b>Shabbat,</b> which commemorates <b>Creation, does not require sanctification by the court.</b> Shabbat is sanctified every week independent of any court decision.
As for ben Azzai’s statement, it should be understood as follows: <b>The Festivals of the Lord require an expert,</b> as the start of the month, which is dependent upon the appearance of the new moon, which in turn determines the Festivals, can be established only by a court composed of experts. <b>But the portion on vows does not require an expert,</b> i.e., vows can be dissolved <b>even</b> by <b>a court of laymen.</b> This explanation of the <i>baraita</i> given to Rav Asi bar Natan also serves to explain Beit Shammai’s source for the <i>halakha</i> that three laymen can dissolve vows.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But in the portion on vows</b> the phrase <b>“the heads of the tribes”</b> (Numbers 30:2) <b>is written.</b> How, then, can it be said that vows can be dissolved by laymen? <b>Rav Ḥisda said, and some say</b> it was <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> From “the heads of the tribes,” the Sages derive that vows can be dissolved <b>by a single expert</b> by himself, but three laymen also have that ability.
§ <b>Rabbi Ḥanina says:</b> A husband <b>who is silent</b> and does not formally nullify his wife’s vow <b>in order to annoy [<i>lemeikat</i>]</b> her, but intends to nullify it later, <b>can nullify</b> it <b>even from now until ten days</b> later. <b>Rava raised an objection</b> to this from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>When did they say</b> that if <b>the husband</b> of a betrothed young woman <b>dies,</b> the <b>authority</b> to nullify the woman’s vows <b>reverts to the father?</b> The authority reverts to the father <b>when the husband did not hear</b> of her vow, <b>or when he heard and was silent, or when he heard and nullified</b> it <b>and died on the same day. But</b> if <b>he heard and ratified</b> it, <b>or if he heard and was silent and died on the following day, he,</b> the father, <b>cannot nullify</b> the vow.
<b>What, is</b> the phrase: Heard and was silent, <b>not</b> referring even <b>to one who is silent in order to annoy</b> her, and nevertheless nullification is only possible that day, contradicting the opinion of Rabbi Ḥanina? The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to one who is silent in order to sustain</b> the vow. The Gemara asks: <b>If so, this is</b> the same as: <b>Or he heard and ratified,</b> mentioned earlier in the <i>baraita</i>. <b>Rather,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to one who is silent without any specific</b> intent, as opposed to the husband who is silent in order to annoy his wife, whose intent is to nullify the vow.
<b>Rav Ḥisda raised an objection</b> from a different <i>baraita</i>: In some ways the <i>halakha</i> is more <b>stringent in ratification than in nullification, and</b> in other ways it is more stringent <b>in nullification than in ratification.</b> The <b>stringency in ratification</b> of vows is

Daf 79a

<b>that silence ratifies</b> a vow, <b>but silence does not cancel,</b> i.e., nullify, a vow. If the husband <b>ratified</b> a vow <b>in his heart, it is ratified,</b> but if <b>he nullified</b> it <b>in his heart, it is not nullified.</b> The <i>baraita</i> adds: <b>If he ratified</b> a vow <b>he can no longer nullify</b> it; and similarly, <b>if he nullified</b> a vow <b>he can no longer ratify</b> it. In any case, the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches that silence ratifies</b> a vow. <b>What, is it not</b> referring even <b>to one who is silent in order to annoy</b> his wife?
The Gemara rejects this interpretation: <b>No,</b> it is referring <b>to one who is silent in order to sustain</b> the vow. The Gemara asks: If so, <b>this is</b> the same as: <b>If</b> the husband <b>ratified</b> a vow <b>in his heart, it is ratified. Rather,</b> the phrase in the <i>baraita</i>: Silence ratifies a vow, is referring <b>to</b> a case where the husband <b>is silent without specifying</b> his intent.
Relating to the <i>baraita</i>, the Gemara asks: <b>We found</b> how the <i>halakha</i> is more <b>stringent in ratification</b> of vows <b>than in nullification</b> of vows, but <b>where</b> do <b>we</b> find a case <b>in</b> which the <i>halakha</i> is more stringent <b>in nullification than in ratification? Rabbi Yoḥanan says: One can request</b> from a halakhic authority dissolution of <b>the ratification</b> of a vow his wife took, <b>but one cannot request</b> dissolution of <b>the nullification</b> of a vow.
<b>Rav Kahana raised an objection</b> to the opinion of Rabbi Ḥanina that a husband who is silent about his wife’s vow in order to annoy her can nullify it even several days later. A <i>baraita</i> teaches: <b>“But if her husband altogether hold his peace at her</b> from day to day, then he causes all her vows to be ratified” (Numbers 30:15). <b>The verse is speaking of one who is silent in order to annoy</b> his wife. Do <b>you say</b> that the verse is referring <b>to one who is silent in order to annoy</b> her, <b>or it is</b> referring <b>only to one who is silent in order to ratify</b> the vow?
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>When it says,</b> in the continuation of the same verse: “He has ratified them, <b>because he held his peace at her</b> on the day that he heard them” (Numbers 30:15), <b>the verse is speaking of one who is silent in order to ratify</b> the vow. <b>How do I realize</b> the meaning of: <b>“If her husband altogether holds his peace at her”?</b> It must be that <b>the verse is speaking of one who is silent in order to annoy</b> his wife, and that this is also considered an act of ratification. This <i>baraita</i> is <b>a conclusive refutation [<i>teyuveta</i>]</b> of Rabbi Ḥanina’s opinion.
The Gemara asks about this <i>baraita</i>: <b>And let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>interpret this</b> part of the verse as referring <b>to one who is silent in order to ratify</b> the vow, and <b>that</b> part of the same verse as referring <b>to one who was silent without specifying</b> his intent, as the Gemara suggests above in explanation of the <i>baraita</i>? The Gemara answers: <b>Superfluous verses are written</b> about silence, leading to the conclusion that whatever the reason for the husband’s silence, the vow is ratified.
<b>Rava raised</b> a further <b>objection</b> to the opinion of Rabbi Ḥanina, from a mishna (76b): If <b>she took a vow</b> on Friday <b>with nightfall</b> approaching, her father or husband <b>can nullify</b> the vow only <b>until nightfall, since, if it became dark and he had not</b> yet <b>nullified</b> her vow, <b>he cannot nullify</b> it anymore. <b>Why</b> should this be so? <b>Let</b> the fact that the husband refrained from nullifying the vow out of respect for Shabbat be regarded <b>like one who is silent in order to annoy</b> his wife, who, according to Rav Huna, can still nullify the vow later. The fact that this is not the case is <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion of Rabbi Ḥanina.
<b>Rav Ashi</b> also <b>raised an objection</b> to Rabbi Ḥanina’s opinion, from another mishna (87b): If a husband or father said, after failing to nullify a vow on the day he heard it: <b>I know that there are vows, but I do not know that there are those who</b> can <b>nullify</b> vows, i.e., he was unaware that he can nullify a vow, <b>he can nullify</b> it even after the day he heard it. However, if he said: <b>I know there are those who nullify, but</b> I refrained from nullifying the vow because <b>I do not know that this is</b> considered <b>a vow</b> that I could nullify, <b>Rabbi Meir says: He cannot nullify</b> at this point, <b>but the Rabbis say:</b> Even in this case <b>he can nullify</b> the vow when he discovers his error.
Rav Ashi asks rhetorically: <b>But why</b> may he not nullify according to Rabbi Meir’s opinion? <b>Let</b> his silence by mistake be <b>like</b> that of <b>one who is silent in order to annoy,</b> who, according to Rabbi Ḥanina, can nullify the vow at a later stage. This is <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of Rabbi Ḥanina’s opinion.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>And these are</b> the <b>vows that he,</b> the husband or father, <b>can nullify:</b> The first category consists of <b>matters that involve affliction</b> for the woman who took the vow. For example, if a woman vowed: <b>If I bathe, or: If I do not bathe;</b> if she vowed: <b>If I adorn myself [<i>etkashet</i>], or: If I do not adorn myself.</b>

Daf 79b

<b>Rabbi Yosei said: These are not vows of affliction. Rather, these are vows of affliction:</b> For example, if <b>she said: The produce of the</b> entire <b>world is <i>konam</i> for me as if</b> it were <b>an offering, he can nullify</b> the vow, as it certainly involves affliction. If, however, she said: <b>The produce of this country is</b> <i>konam</i> <b>for me,</b> he cannot nullify the vow, as it does not involve affliction, since <b>he may</b> still <b>bring her</b> produce <b>from another country.</b> Similarly, if she said: <b>The produce of this storekeeper is</b> <i>konam</i> <b>for me, he cannot nullify</b> her vow, as he may still bring her produce from another storekeeper. <b>But if</b> he can obtain <b>his sustenance only from him,</b> that particular storekeeper, <b>he can nullify</b> the vow. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yosei.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara raises a question with regard to the ruling of the mishna: Is it only <b>vows of affliction that he can nullify,</b> whereas vows <b>that do not involve affliction he cannot nullify? But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse “These are the statutes that the Lord commanded Moses, <b>between a man and his wife, between a father and his daughter”</b> (Numbers 30:17) <b>teaches that a husband can nullify</b> any of his wife’s <b>vows that</b> adversely affect the relationship <b>between him and her,</b> even if they do not involve affliction?
The Sages <b>say</b> in response: In fact, <b>he can nullify</b> both <b>these and those.</b> There is, <b>however,</b> a difference between them. When he nullifies vows of <b>affliction, he nullifies</b> them <b>forever,</b> i.e., the vows remain nullified even if they subsequently divorce. <b>But</b> when he nullifies vows that <b>do not involve affliction</b> but merely impact upon their relationship, then, <b>while</b> they are married and <b>she is under his</b> authority <b>it is an</b> effective <b>nullification,</b> but <b>when</b> he <b>divorces her, her vow takes effect upon her,</b> i.e., his nullification is no longer effective. As stated, this is referring <b>to</b> vows concerning <b>matters that</b> adversely affect the relationship <b>between him and her, that do not involve affliction. However,</b> if he nullifies a vow that affects their relationship and also <b>involves affliction, her vow does not take effect upon her</b> even after she leaves her husband’s authority.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> as for vows concerning <b>matters that do not involve affliction, when</b> a man <b>divorces</b> his wife, do <b>they</b> really <b>take effect upon her? But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna with regard to a woman who prohibited her handiwork to her husband by way of a vow (85a) that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri says:</b> Even though the vow is presently invalid, as a woman cannot render forbidden to her husband that to which he is already entitled, <b>he should</b> nevertheless <b>nullify</b> the vow? This is because <b>perhaps he will</b> one day <b>divorce her,</b> at which point the vow will take effect <b>and she will</b> then <b>be forbidden to him,</b> since he will be unable to remarry her lest he come to benefit from her handiwork. <b>Apparently,</b> however, <b>if he divorces her</b> after <b>having nullified her</b> vow <b>from the outset,</b> before their divorce, <b>it is</b> a permanent <b>nullification,</b> and although the vow does not involve affliction it remains nullified after their divorce.
Consequently, the Sages <b>say</b> a different answer: With regard to both <b>these and those,</b> vows of affliction and vows adversely affecting the relationship between them, when the husband nullifies the vow, it <b>is</b> a permanent <b>nullification.
