<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>one who vows</b> that <b>benefit</b> from him is forbidden <b>to his son-in-law, but he</b> nevertheless <b>wishes to give his daughter,</b> i.e., the wife of that same son-in-law, <b>money,</b> then, though he cannot do so directly, as anything acquired by a woman belongs to her husband, <b>he</b> should <b>say to her: This money is hereby given to you as a gift, provided that your husband has no rights to it,</b> but the gift includes <b>only that which you pick up and place in your mouth.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav said</b> that <b>they taught</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>only</b> in a case <b>where he</b> actually <b>said to her: That which you pick up and place in your mouth</b> is yours. <b>But</b> if <b>he said: Do as you please</b> with the money, his stipulation is of no effect, and <b>the husband acquires</b> the money. <b>And Shmuel says</b> that <b>even</b> if <b>he said: Do as you please</b> with the money, <b>the husband does not acquire it. Rabbi Zeira objects to this</b> statement of Rav:

Daf 88b

<b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion among the <i>tanna’im</i> <b>does Rav’s <i>halakha</i> correspond?</b> It is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir, who said</b> as a principle that <b>the hand of a woman is like the hand of her husband.</b> According to Rabbi Meir, a slave has no independent right of acquisition, and anything given to a slave belongs to his master even if it was stipulated otherwise (see <i>Kiddushin</i> 23b). Rav assumes that similarly, a married woman has no independent right of acquisition, but rather, anything that she attempts to acquire for herself is automatically acquired by her husband.
Rabbi Zeira continues: <b>And raise a contradiction</b> from an unattributed mishna (<i>Eiruvin</i> 73b), which presumably follows the opinion of Rabbi Meir: <b>How does one merge</b> the courtyards that open <b>into an alleyway</b> in order to permit its residents to carry on Shabbat from one courtyard to another in the same alley, if a person wishes to act on behalf of all the residents of the alleyway? <b>He places a barrel</b> filled with his own food or wine <b>and says: This is for all the residents of the alleyway.</b> For this gift to be acquired by the others, someone must accept it on their behalf, <b>and</b> the <i>tanna</i> therefore teaches that <b>he may transfer possession to them</b> even <b>by means of his Hebrew slave or maidservant,</b> whom he does not own, <b>and</b> likewise <b>by means of his adult son or daughter, and</b> similarly <b>by means of his wife.</b> These people may acquire the <i>eiruv</i> food on behalf of all the residents of the alleyway.
Rabbi Zeira states the contradiction: <b>And if you say</b> that a woman’s <b>husband acquires</b> anything given to her, <b>the <i>eiruv</i></b> food <b>has</b> consequently <b>not left the husband’s domain</b> when he gives it to his wife, for anything she acquires belongs to him. Rather, it can be seen from here that Rabbi Meir does not extend his principle from a slave to a married woman, in opposition of the ruling of Rav.
<b>Rava said</b> in response: <b>Even though Rabbi Meir said</b> that in general <b>the hand of a woman is like the hand of her husband,</b> in accordance with the ruling of Rav, <b>Rabbi Meir</b> nevertheless <b>concedes with regard to the merging</b> of alleyways <b>that since it</b> is her aim <b>to acquire</b> the <i>eiruv</i> food <b>for others from the hand of her husband,</b> and not to acquire it for herself, <b>she can acquire</b> it from him for this purpose.
<b>Ravina raised an objection to Rav Ashi</b> from the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>These are</b> the people <b>who can acquire</b> <i>eiruv</i> food <b>on behalf of</b> others: The <i>eiruv</i> food can be acquired <b>by means of his adult son or daughter, and</b> by means of <b>his Hebrew slave or maidservant. And these are</b> the people <b>who cannot acquire</b> an <i>eiruv</i> <b>on behalf of</b> others: The <i>eiruv</i> food cannot be acquired <b>by means of his minor son or daughter, or</b> by means of <b>his Canaanite slave or maidservant, or</b> by means of <b>his wife.</b> This indicates that a married woman does not have an independent right of acquisition to acquire the <i>eiruv</i> food on behalf of others, in opposition to the ruling of the mishna.
<b>Rather, Rav Ashi said:</b> In <b>the mishna</b> in <i>Eiruvin</i>, <b>we are dealing with</b> a woman <b>who possesses a courtyard</b> of her own <b>in that alleyway,</b> i.e., it is a case where the husband had earlier stipulated that she should have property of her own, to which he renounces all his rights. <b>As, since she acquires</b> the <i>eiruv</i> food <b>for herself</b> by virtue of the courtyard that she owns in that alleyway, <b>she</b> likewise <b>acquires</b> it <b>for others.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The Torah states: <b>“But every vow of a widow, and of her that is divorced,</b> with which she has bound her soul, <b>shall stand against her”</b> (Numbers 30:10). <b>How so?</b> If a widow or divorced woman <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite after thirty days,</b> then <b>even if she was married within thirty days,</b> her new husband <b>cannot nullify</b> her vow.

Daf 89a

If <b>she took a vow while she was under the jurisdiction of</b> her <b>husband, he can nullify</b> the vow <b>for her. How so?</b> If <b>she said</b> when she was still married: <b>I am hereby a nazirite for after thirty</b> days, and her husband nullified the vow, then <b>even if she was widowed or divorced within</b> the <b>thirty-</b>day period, the vow <b>is nullified.</b> If <b>she took a vow on that,</b> i.e., one, <b>day and was divorced on that</b> same <b>day,</b> then even if her husband <b>took her back</b> as his wife <b>on that</b> same <b>day, he cannot nullify</b> her previous vows. <b>This is the principle: Once she has left</b> and gone <b>into her own jurisdiction</b> for even <b>a single hour,</b> then after they are remarried her husband <b>can no</b> longer <b>nullify</b> any vow she uttered during their first marriage.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>a widow or a divorcée who said: I am hereby a nazirite for when I will get married, and she was married, Rabbi Yishmael says</b> her husband <b>can nullify</b> her vow, <b>whereas Rabbi Akiva says he cannot nullify</b> it. <b>And the mnemonic</b> device for the opinions of Rabbi Yishmael and Rabbi Akiva with regard to this <i>halakha</i> and the following one is the Hebrew acronym <b><i>yod</i>, <i>lamed</i>, <i>lamed</i>, <i>yod</i>:</b> <i>Yafer</i>, <i>lo yafer</i>; <i>lo yafer</i>, <i>yafer</i>, i.e., he can nullify, he cannot nullify; he cannot nullify, he can nullify. As for <b>a married woman who said</b> while she was married: <b>I am hereby a nazirite for when I will get divorced, and she was divorced, Rabbi Yishmael says</b> her husband <b>cannot nullify</b> her vow, <b>whereas Rabbi Akiva says</b> he <b>can nullify</b> it.
<b>Rabbi Yishmael said: It says: “But every vow of a widow, and of her that is divorced</b>…shall be upheld against her” (Numbers 30:10), which means that the practical application of <b>the vow must be in the time of the</b> woman’s <b>widowhood or divorce.</b> Only when the vow is to take effect when she is a widow or a divorcée shall it be upheld against her, since then it is impossible to nullify. <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> by contrast, <b>maintains: It says:</b> “But every vow…<b>with which she has bound her soul,</b> shall be upheld against her,” which means <b>that the binding of the vow,</b> i.e., the taking of the vow creating the prohibition, <b>must be at the time of</b> the woman’s <b>widowhood or divorce.</b>
<b>Rav Ḥisda said: The mishna</b> that links the possibility of nullification to the time of the taking of the vow <b>is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva. Abaye said: Even</b> if <b>you say</b> that the mishna follows the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael,</b> there is no difficulty, for <b>the mishna</b> is referring to a woman <b>who made her</b> vow <b>dependent on days,</b> i.e., she specified that the vow should take effect after a fixed period of time. By contrast, the <b><i>baraita</i></b> is referring to a woman <b>who made her</b> vow <b>dependent on marriage.</b>
In the case of the <i>baraita</i>, since the woman explicitly connected the application of her vow to her marital status, the possibility of nullifying the vow depends on the time that the vow takes effect. But in the case of the mishna, where the application of the vow is tied to a particular date, it is possible that <b>the days will be completed and she was not divorced,</b> or that <b>the days will be completed and she was not married.</b> Since there is no inherent connection between her marriage and the vow, Rabbi Yishmael agrees that the possibility of nullification depends on the time when the vow was pronounced.
§ The mishna in the previous chapter (71a) teaches that as long as a betrothed young woman has not gone out into her own jurisdiction for a single moment, her father and her husband can nullify her vows. The mishna in this chapter teaches the principle in the reverse: If she has gone out for even a single hour, her husband cannot nullify her vows. The Gemara addresses this repetition. The mishna of: <b>This is the principle, which is taught in</b> the chapter called: <b>A betrothed young woman,</b> serves <b>to include</b> a case where <b>the father went with the messengers of the husband</b> after handing over his daughter in marriage <b>and</b> a case <b>where the messengers</b> of the <b>father went with the messengers of the husband. In</b> the case of <b>a betrothed young woman,</b> since the father or his messengers were still with her, she has not yet left her father’s jurisdiction, and nevertheless the mishna teaches <b>that her father and her husband can nullify her vows.</b>
The mishna of: <b>This is the principle, which is taught in</b> this chapter, called: <b>And these are the vows,</b> serves <b>to include</b> a case where <b>the father handed over</b> his daughter <b>to the messengers of the husband, or</b> a case <b>where the messengers of the father handed</b> her <b>over to the messengers of the husband.</b> Although neither the father nor his messengers accompanied her, and therefore she has been fully handed over to her husband, nevertheless, the mishna teaches <b>that the husband cannot nullify previous</b> vows, i.e., vows that preceded their marriage.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> There are <b>nine young women whose vows are upheld</b> and cannot be nullified: If she took a vow when she was <b>a grown woman and she is an orphan;</b> if she took a vow when she was <b>a young woman, and has reached her majority, and she is an orphan;</b>

Daf 89b

if she took a vow when she was <b>a young woman who had not</b> yet <b>reached her majority, and she is an orphan;</b> if she took a vow when she was <b>a grown woman and her father died;</b> if she took a vow when she was <b>a young woman,</b> and she became <b>a grown woman, and her father died;</b> if she took a vow when she was <b>a young woman who had not reached her majority, and her father died;</b> if she took a vow when she was <b>a young woman, and her father died, and after her father died she reached her majority;</b> if she took a vow when she was <b>a grown woman and her father is</b> still <b>alive;</b> and if she took a vow when she was <b>a young woman,</b> and she became <b>a grown woman, and her father is</b> still <b>alive. Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> With regard to <b>even one who married off his minor daughter, and she was widowed or divorced and she returned to him,</b> and according to her age <b>she still</b> is in the category of <b>a young woman,</b> her vows cannot be nullified.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav said: This</b> mishna is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who spelled out all the cases and listed nine young women whose vows are upheld. <b>But the Rabbis say</b> that it is unnecessary to go into such detail. Instead, they simply said: There are <b>three young women whose vows are upheld</b> and cannot be nullified: <b>A grown woman, and an orphan, and an orphan in her father’s lifetime,</b> i.e., a young woman who was divorced or widowed while her father was still alive and is considered an orphan in that her father no longer has jurisdiction over her.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If a woman said to her husband: <b>Deriving benefit from</b> my <b>father or from your father is <i>konam</i> for me if I</b> will <b>prepare</b> anything <b>for you;</b> or if she said: <b>Deriving benefit from you</b> is <i>konam</i> <b>for me if I</b> will <b>prepare</b> anything <b>for</b> my <b>father or for your father,</b> the husband <b>can nullify</b> this vow.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If a woman said to her husband: <b>Deriving benefit from</b> my <b>father or from your father</b> is <i>konam</i> <b>for me if I</b> will <b>prepare</b> anything <b>for you, Rabbi Natan says</b> her husband <b>cannot nullify</b> the vow. She must prepare food for him, as she is obligated to do so by virtue of their being married, and it is prohibited for her to benefit from their respective fathers. The husband cannot nullify a vow that has not yet taken effect and that depends on the fulfillment of a certain condition. <b>And the Rabbis say</b> that even in such a case <b>he can nullify</b> her vow.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: If the woman said to her husband: <b>I am removed from the Jews,</b> i.e., the benefit of my engaging in sexual intercourse will be forbidden to all Jews, <b>if I engage in sexual intercourse with you, Rabbi Natan says he cannot nullify</b> the vow. Rather, she must engage in sexual intercourse with her husband, as she is obligated to do so by virtue of their marriage, and she will be forbidden to all other Jews. <b>And the Rabbis say he can nullify</b> her vow.
§ It is related that there was <b>a certain man who</b> took a vow that all <b>benefit from the world should be forbidden to him if</b> he <b>marries a woman when he has not</b> yet <b>learned <i>halakha</i>. He would run up a ladder and rope but was not able to learn</b> the material, i.e., despite all his efforts he failed in his studies. <b>Rav Aḥa bar Rav Huna came and misled him,</b> allowing him to understand that even if he took a vow, the vow would not take effect, <b>and</b> so <b>he married a woman.</b>

Daf 90a

<b>And</b> Rav Aḥa bar Rav Huna then <b>smeared him with clay</b> to protect him from the elements, as it was now prohibited for him to benefit from the world by wearing clothes. <b>And</b> he then <b>brought him before Rav Ḥisda,</b> to dissolve his vow. <b>Rava said: Who is wise</b> enough <b>to act in this manner, if not Rav Aḥa bar Rav Huna, who is a great man? As he holds that just as the Rabbis and Rabbi Natan disagree with regard to nullification,</b> whether it is possible to nullify a vow that has yet to take effect, <b>so too, they disagree with regard to a request</b> made to a halakhic authority to dissolve a vow, whether it is possible to request dissolution of such a vow. Rav Aḥa bar Rav Huna’s plan was to have the vow go into effect, so that the man could request that it be dissolved.
<b>And Rav Pappi said</b> with regard to this issue: The <b>dispute</b> between Rabbi Natan and the Rabbis is <b>with regard to nullification</b> only, <b>as Rabbi Natan holds</b> that <b>a husband can nullify</b> a vow <b>only once the vow has taken effect, as it is written: “And the moon shall be confounded [<i>ḥafera</i>]”</b> (Isaiah 24:23). He employs this phrase as an allusion, interpreting the word <i>ḥafera</i> as if it were <i>hafara</i>, nullification, and concludes from here that only a vow that already exists, like the moon, can be nullified. <b>And the Rabbis hold</b> that <b>a husband can nullify</b> a vow <b>even though the vow has not</b> yet <b>taken effect, as it is written: “He nullifies the thoughts of the crafty”</b> (Job 5:12), implying that nullification pertains even to thoughts, to prevent them from going into effect.
<b>But with regard to a request</b> made to a halakhic authority to dissolve a vow, <b>everyone,</b> both Rabbi Natan and the Rabbis, <b>agrees that a halakhic authority cannot dissolve anything unless the vow has</b> already <b>taken effect, as it is written: “He shall not profane his word”</b> (Numbers 30:3), which indicates that the person himself who took the vow cannot profane his words and dissolve his vow, but a halakhic authority may do so. This, however, applies only if the vow has already gone into effect, as it says: “His word.”
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> that the following <i>baraita</i> <b>supports</b> Rav Pappi’s opinion: If one says: The property <b>of so-and-so is <i>konam</i></b> for me, and for <b>that</b> reason <b>I will not benefit</b> from it, <b>and</b> deriving benefit from <b>he who I will request</b> dissolution <b>for</b> the vow is also <i>konam</i> for me, then, if he desires to dissolve the vows <b>he</b> must first <b>request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to the first</b> vow, <b>and afterward he</b> can <b>request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to the second. And if you say</b> that <b>one can request</b> dissolution of a vow <b>even though the vow has not</b> yet <b>taken effect,</b> why must he request dissolution of his two vows in the above order? <b>If he</b> so <b>wishes, he</b> can <b>first request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to this</b> vow, and <b>if he wishes, he</b> can <b>first request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to that</b> one.
The Gemara refutes this argument: <b>And does he know if this</b> vow <b>is first</b> or <b>if that</b> vow <b>is last?</b> The <i>baraita</i> does not specify which vow is first and which is last. Perhaps first and last is referring not to the order in which the two vows were taken, but rather to the order in which they are dissolved, so that if he so wishes, he can first request dissolution of the vow not to derive benefit from the halakhic authority from whom he will request dissolution of his vow.
<b>Rather, let us say</b> that a different <i>baraita</i> <b>supports</b> Rav Pappi’s opinion: If one says: The property <b>of so-and-so is <i>konam</i></b> for me, and for <b>that</b> reason <b>I will not benefit</b> from it, and <b>I am hereby a nazirite for when I will request</b> dissolution <b>of</b> this vow, if he desires to dissolve the vows <b>he</b> must first <b>request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to his vow</b> not to derive benefit from a particular person, <b>and afterward he</b> can <b>request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to his</b> vow of <b>naziriteship</b> that he accepted upon himself should he request dissolution of his first vow.
<b>And if you say</b> that <b>one</b> can <b>request</b> dissolution of a vow <b>even though the vow has not</b> yet <b>taken effect,</b> why must the two vows be dissolved in that order? <b>If he</b> so <b>wishes, he</b> can <b>first request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to his vow</b> not to derive benefit from that other person, and <b>if he wishes, he</b> can <b>first request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to his</b> vow of <b>naziriteship.</b> The Gemara rejects this argument: This is no proof, as it is possible that the <i>baraita</i> <b>is</b> taught in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Natan,</b> who maintains that a vow can be nullified only after it has gone into effect. The Rabbis, however, dispute this view.
<b>Ravina said: Mareimar said to me: This is</b> what <b>your father said in the name of Rav Pappi:</b> The <b>dispute</b> between Rabbi Natan and the Rabbis is <b>with regard to nullification</b> only, as Rabbi Natan holds that a husband can nullify a vow only once the vow has taken effect, whereas the Rabbis hold that a husband can nullify a vow even though the vow has not yet taken effect. <b>But with regard to a request</b> made to a halakhic authority to dissolve a vow, <b>everyone,</b> both Rabbi Natan and the Rabbis, <b>agrees,</b> that <b>he can dissolve</b> the vow <b>even though the vow has not</b> yet <b>taken effect. As it is written: “He shall not profane his word”</b> (Numbers 30:3), which Ravina expounds as follows:

Daf 90b

<b>That is to say that there was not</b> yet any <b>action</b> but only speech, and even so the halakhic authority can dissolve the vow.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> against this version of the tannaitic dispute from the aforementioned <i>baraita</i>: If one says: The property <b>of so-and-so is <i>konam</i></b> for me, and for <b>that</b> reason <b>I will not benefit</b> from it, <b>and</b> deriving benefit from <b>he who I will request</b> dissolution <b>for</b> the vow is also <i>konam</i> for me, if he desires to dissolve the vows <b>he</b> must first <b>request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to the first</b> vow, <b>and afterward he</b> can <b>request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to the second.</b> But according to what was stated above, that all agree that a vow can be dissolved even before it has taken effect, <b>why</b> is this so? <b>If he</b> so <b>wishes, he</b> can <b>first request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to this</b> vow, and <b>if he wishes, he</b> can <b>first request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to that</b> one.
The Gemara answers: <b>Does he know which</b> vow <b>is first and which is</b> the <b>second?</b> The wording of the <i>baraita</i> is not at all clear on this point. Perhaps, if he so wishes, he can first request dissolution of the vow not to derive benefit from the halakhic authority from whom he will request dissolution of his vow.
The Gemara <b>raises</b> a further <b>objection</b> from the second <i>baraita</i> cited above: If one says: The property <b>of so-and-so is <i>konam</i></b> for me, and for <b>that</b> reason <b>I will not benefit</b> from it, and <b>I am hereby a nazirite for when I will request</b> dissolution <b>of</b> this vow, if he desires to dissolve the vows <b>he</b> must first <b>request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to his vow</b> that rendered benefit from a particular person forbidden, <b>and afterward he</b> can <b>request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to his</b> vow of <b>naziriteship</b> that he accepted upon himself should he request dissolution of his first vow. <b>But why</b> must he proceed in this manner? <b>If he</b> so <b>wishes he</b> can <b>first request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to his vow</b> not to derive benefit from that other person, <b>and if he wishes he</b> can <b>first request</b> dissolution <b>with regard to his</b> vow of <b>naziriteship.</b> The fact that the <i>baraita</i> does not say this indicates that a vow can be dissolved only once it has gone into effect. The Gemara concludes: Here is <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of this version of the dispute between Rabbi Natan and the Rabbis.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Initially</b> the Sages <b>would say</b> that <b>three women are divorced</b> even against their husbands’ will, <b>and</b> nevertheless <b>they receive</b> payment of what is due to them according to their <b>marriage contract.</b> The first is the wife of a priest <b>who says</b> to her husband: <b>I am defiled to you,</b> i.e., she claims that she had been raped, so that she is now forbidden to her husband. The second is a woman who says to her husband: <b>Heaven is between me and you,</b> i.e., she declares that he is impotent, a claim she cannot prove, as the truth of it is known only to God. <b>And</b> the third is a woman who takes a vow, stating: <b>I am removed from the Jews,</b> i.e., benefit from sexual intercourse with any Jew, including my husband, is forbidden to me.
<b>They</b> subsequently <b>retracted</b> their words <b>and said</b> that in order <b>that</b> a married <b>woman should not cast her eyes on another</b> man <b>and</b> to that end <b>ruin</b> her relationship <b>with her husband</b> and still receive payment of her marriage contract, these <i>halakhot</i> were modified as follows: A priest’s wife <b>who says</b> to her husband: <b>I am defiled to you, must bring proof for her words</b> that she was raped. As for a woman who says: <b>Heaven is between me and you,</b> the court <b>must act</b> and deal with the matter <b>by way of a request,</b> rather than force the husband to divorce his wife. <b>And</b> with regard to a woman who says: <b>I am removed from the Jews,</b> her husband <b>must nullify his part,</b> i.e., the aspect of the vow that concerns him, so that she should be permitted to him, <b>and she may engage in sexual intercourse with him, but she is removed from</b> all other <b>Jews,</b> so that if he divorces her she is forbidden to all.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages, based on the second ruling of the mishna: If the wife of a priest <b>said to her husband: I am defiled</b> to you, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>with regard to</b> whether <b>she may partake of <i>teruma</i>?</b> Is the <i>halakha</i> that just as she is not believed with regard to divorce, so she is not believed with regard to <i>teruma</i>, or is the <i>halakha</i> that with regard to <i>teruma</i> she is believed, and therefore it is prohibited for her to partake of <i>teruma</i>, as is the <i>halakha</i> of a woman married to a priest who engages in sexual intercourse with a man other than her husband? <b>Rav Sheshet said:</b> She <b>may partake</b> of <i>teruma</i>, <b>so that she not cast aspersions on her children.</b> If she is barred from partaking of <i>teruma</i>, people will see this as supporting her claim that she had been raped, and rumors will circulate that her sons are unfit for the priesthood. <b>Rava said: She may not partake</b> of <i>teruma</i>, as <b>she can partake of non-sacred</b> food, and it is preferable that her claim that she is no longer permitted to eat <i>teruma</i> be taken into account.
<b>Rava said: And Rav Sheshet concedes that if</b> this wife of the priest who claimed to have been raped <b>was</b> then <b>widowed</b> from him, <b>she may not</b> continue to <b>partake</b> of <i>teruma</i>. Why? <b>Isn’t the reason</b> that she is permitted to partake of <i>teruma</i> <b>only that she</b> should not <b>cast aspersions on her children?</b> This being the case, if she <b>was widowed or divorced,</b> people <b>will say</b> that only <b>now</b> it occurred <b>that she was raped,</b> i.e., the entire incident occurred after she was no longer married to her husband. Therefore, rumors will not circulate that the children that she bore him beforehand are unfit.
§ <b>Rav Pappa said: Rava tested us</b> with the following question: As for the <b>wife of a priest who was raped</b> in the presence of witnesses, is <b>she</b> entitled to <b>receive</b> payment of her <b>marriage contract or</b> is <b>she not</b> entitled to <b>receive</b> payment of her <b>marriage contract?</b> The Gemara explains the two sides of the question: Is the <i>halakha</i> that <b>since rape with regard to</b> a woman married to <b>a priest is like willing</b> sexual intercourse <b>with regard to a</b> woman married to <b>an Israelite,</b> as the wife of a priest who was raped is obligated to leave her husband, just as the wife of an Israelite who willingly engaged in sexual intercourse with another man is obligated to leave her husband, <b>she</b> is therefore <b>not</b> entitled to <b>receive</b> payment of her <b>marriage contract? Or perhaps she can say to him: I am fit</b> to continue being married, as, if her husband were an Israelite she would not be forbidden to him after being raped.

Daf 91a

<b>And it is the man,</b> her husband, <b>whose field was flooded.</b> In other words, like one whose field was flooded and destroyed, it is he who has suffered a natural disaster, as it is his status as a priest that forces him to divorce his wife. Therefore, she is entitled to receive payment of her marriage contract. <b>And we said to</b> Rava, in response to his question: The answer to your question <b>is</b> found in <b>the mishna,</b> which states: A woman <b>who says</b> to her husband: <b>I am defiled to you,</b> is entitled to <b>receive</b> payment of her <b>marriage contract.</b>
The Gemara analyzes the mishna: <b>With what are we dealing? If we say</b> the mishna is speaking <b>of the wife of an Israelite,</b> consider the following: <b>If</b> she claims that she engaged in sexual intercourse <b>willingly, does she have any</b> right to receive payment of her <b>marriage contract? And if</b> she says it was <b>by force,</b> i.e., she was raped, <b>is she</b> thereby <b>forbidden to the man,</b> i.e., her husband? <b>But rather,</b> the mishna must be referring to <b>the wife of a priest.</b> Again, what are the circumstances? <b>If</b> she claims that she engaged in sexual intercourse <b>willingly, does she have any</b> right to receive payment of her <b>marriage contract? Is her</b> law <b>any less</b> stringent <b>than</b> that of <b>the wife of an Israelite</b> who <b>willingly</b> engaged in sexual intercourse with another man? <b>Rather, is it not</b> that the sexual intercourse was <b>by force? And</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches</b> that <b>she has</b> a right to receive payment of her <b>marriage contract.</b> This answers Rava’s question.
§ <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: If a woman <b>said to her husband: You divorced me, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is she believed or not? <b>Rav Hamnuna said: Come</b> and <b>hear</b> an answer to this question from what is stated in the mishna about a woman <b>who says: I am defiled to you, that even according to the ultimate</b> version of the <b>mishna that teaches</b> that <b>she is not believed</b> in her claim, it may be argued that <b>it is</b> only <b>there that she is</b> suspected of <b>lying</b> when she claims to have been defiled, <b>as she knows that her husband does not know</b> the truth <b>about her.</b> She is relating an incident that supposedly occurred in his absence. <b>But concerning</b> the claim: <b>You divorced me, with regard to which he knows</b> the truth <b>about</b> whether or not he actually divorced <b>her,</b> she <b>is believed.</b> Why? <b>Because</b> the court relies on the <b>presumption that a woman is not brazen</b> enough to lie <b>in the presence of her husband</b> and present a claim that he knows is patently false.
<b>Rava said to him: On the contrary, even according to the initial</b> version of the <b>mishna that teaches</b> that the woman <b>is believed</b> in her claim that she is defiled to her husband, it may be argued that it is only <b>there</b> that she is believed, <b>because</b> a woman <b>would not demean herself</b> by claiming she was raped if she were not telling the truth. <b>But here, where it is sometimes hard for her under</b> the authority of <b>the man,</b> i.e., her husband, <b>she would be brazen</b> to his face, and therefore the court does not believe her.
<b>Rav Mesharshiyya raised an objection:</b> Let the ruling <b>of</b> the <b>initial</b> version of the <b>mishna,</b> with regard to a woman who says: <b>Heaven is between me and you,</b> be a <b>conclusive refutation of</b> the opinion of <b>Rava,</b> as <b>here she</b> suffers <b>no embarrassment</b> on account of her claim, <b>and</b> yet the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches that</b> she <b>is believed.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Rava maintains</b> that <b>there, since it is not sufficient for her</b> if she does <b>not state</b> in precise detail her claim that <b>he does not shoot like an arrow,</b> i.e., his semen is not emitted forcefully, then, <b>were it not as she said, she would not say it.</b> She would be too ashamed to speak of such things before the court. It is for this reason that she is believed.
The Gemara further comments: Let the ruling <b>of</b> the <b>ultimate</b> version of the <b>mishna,</b> with regard to a woman who says: <b>Heaven is between me and you, be</b> a <b>conclusive refutation of</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Hamnuna,</b> who maintains that a woman who claims that her husband divorced her is believed. <b>But here,</b> as in the case of the alleged divorce, <b>she knows and her husband</b> also <b>knows with regard to her</b> whether or not she is lying, <b>and</b> yet the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna <b>teaches that she is not believed.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Rav Hamnuna maintains</b> that <b>here too,</b> the woman <b>herself says</b> in her heart: <b>Though he may know</b> whether or not we engaged <b>in sexual intercourse, does he know</b> whether or not <b>he shoots like an arrow? And</b> it is <b>due to that</b> reason that <b>she lies.</b> Since the woman can make a false claim against her husband without having to fear that he will contradict her, she is not believed. A similar point cannot be made in the case of an alleged divorce, as the husband knows whether or not he divorced his wife, and therefore a woman who claims that her husband divorced her is believed.
§ It is related that there was <b>a certain woman, who</b> on <b>every day of</b> engaging in <b>sexual intercourse</b> with her husband, <b>would rise early</b> in the morning <b>and wash</b> her <b>husband’s hands. One day she brought him water to wash</b> his hands, in response to which <b>he said to her: This matter,</b> i.e., sexual intercourse, <b>did not occur now. She said to him: If so,</b> it may be that <b>one of</b> the <b>gentile</b>

Daf 91b

<b>aloe merchants [<i>ahaloyei</i>] who were here</b> just <b>now</b> should be blamed; <b>if</b> it was <b>not you, perhaps</b> it was one <b>of them.</b> The case came before <b>Rav Naḥman,</b> who <b>said:</b> There is reason to suspect that <b>she might have cast her eyes upon another</b> man, <b>and</b> therefore <b>there is no substance to her words.</b> She lacks credibility and her statement is unreliable, and so she remains permitted to her husband.
It is further related that there was <b>a certain woman who was displeased with</b> her <b>man. He said to her: What is different now?</b> What have I done to make you angry? <b>She said to him:</b> I am upset because <b>you never hurt me</b> while we were engaged <b>in proper relations as</b> you did just <b>now. He said to her: This matter did not occur now. She said to him: If so,</b> it may be that <b>one of</b> the <b>gentile oil merchants [<i>naftoyei</i>] who were here</b> just <b>now</b> should be blamed; <b>if</b> it was <b>not you, perhaps</b> it was one <b>of them. Rav Naḥman said to them: Take no notice of her; she has cast her eyes upon another</b> man, and her words are therefore unreliable.
The Gemara relates another incident about <b>a certain man who was secluding</b> himself <b>[<i>meharzeik</i>] in a house, he and a</b> certain married <b>woman.</b> When <b>the owner of the house entered, the adulterer burst through the wall of palm branches and fled. Rava said: The woman is permitted</b> to her husband. The assumption is that she did not sin, for <b>if it is so that</b> the man <b>had committed a transgression, he would have hidden</b> himself in the house instead of revealing his identity by escaping in the open.
The Gemara concludes with one final incident about <b>a certain adulterer who entered</b> the house <b>of a certain</b> married <b>woman.</b> When <b>the man,</b> i.e., her husband, <b>came</b> home, the <b>adulterer went and sat himself behind the door,</b> so that the husband would not know that he was there. <b>There was</b> some <b>cress [<i>taḥlei</i>] lying there</b> in the house, <b>and</b> the adulterer, but not the husband, saw that <b>a snake</b> had come and <b>tasted of it,</b> perhaps thereby contaminating it with its venom. <b>The master of the house wanted to eat from that cress, without the woman’s knowledge. The adulterer said to him: Do not eat from</b> the cress, <b>as a snake has tasted of it.</b>
The case was brought before <b>Rava,</b> who <b>said: His wife is permitted</b> to him, for <b>were it so that</b> the adulterer <b>had committed a transgression, it would have been preferable for him that</b> the husband should <b>eat</b> the cress <b>and die.</b> This is because one who commits adultery is also suspected of bloodshed, <b>as it is written: “For they have committed adultery and blood is on their hands”</b> (Ezekiel 23:37), indicating that adultery leads to murder.
The Gemara comments: It <b>is obvious</b> that this is the case. What then does Rava come to teach us? The Gemara answers: Rava’s ruling is necessary, <b>lest you say</b> that the man did in fact <b>commit a transgression</b> with the other man’s wife, <b>and</b> the reason <b>that he said to</b> the husband that he should not eat and saved his life is <b>because it is preferable for him that the husband should not die.</b> This is in order <b>that his wife should be to him</b> as it says in the verse: <b>“Stolen waters are sweet, and bread eaten in secret is pleasant”</b> (Proverbs 9:17). That is to say, a person derives greater pleasure from forbidden fruit. Rava therefore <b>teaches us</b> that this is not a concern. Rather, the assumption is that he had not yet actually sinned and therefore acted in the proper manner.
